THE RED PAGE.
When Death comes seeking for a prison'd soul ,
And flings the gate ajar,
The body moulders back to earth again,
The soul becomes a star.
The body rots ; the sun, and wind, and sleet
Fuse it and thresh it o’er
Atom by atom, till its pride complete
Is dust and nothing more.
The storms that roll in seasons round the world-
Take it, anew to raise
The broken paths that they themselves have hurl’d
Clean from the ancient ways.
And thus i 1 vs we mark the sure return
Of mortal man to earth ;
The poor grey ashes of the Grecian urn
Leap up in second birth.
We see with awe the resurrected shapes
Of painted vanity,
The tippler mingling ’mid the roots of grapes
For jocund company:
The buried damsels of a lovely hill,
The mean bare miser plain,
The poets chanting in a lilied rill,
The scowling gulch of Cain :
The priest up-soaring on a snowy peak,
His vestments like a cloud ;
The ice-floe holding in its bosom bleak
The pitiless and proud :
There too the landslip of a Judas lip
Its traitor danger hides,
And scandal falling with its ceaseless drip
Outwears the mountain sides :
The marble pillar of a stalwart face
Above the stagnant fen
Of things by far too horrible and base
To boast the name of men.
When Death comes seeking for a prison’d soul,
And flings the gate ajar,
The body moulders back to earth again,
The soul becomes a star.
N.S.W.
H. McC.
BITS OF MORNINO GLORY
The LODD r x (VIC., 70).
The subtle sense of dawning in the air is felt
first by Murphy, and Murphy’s rooster. For the
one is stumbling and cursing about the dusty yard ;
and the other makes hoarse and ragged peals of
cockcrow in the apple tree; before Madge, high up
towards heaven as the gum will lift her, has let
down a trickle or a bead of sound. Dawn is
manifestly there. You may see Tarrengower
shaping on the sky-line. Mountains as men
moving out of night into day at the impulse of
the dawn. The violet grows into the increasing
yet paling blue along all the east ; and beneath
that violet is the self-same gold as lies beneath the
purple collar of the ripening wheat. On that bar
of gold shapes the fringe of the mountain’s
crest. Delicate as cobweb work ; the forest fringe
webs the gold. Walk the world through and you
shall see no other sky line like that. Elm and oak
make blotched and heavy lines in summer, and
network of fairy skeletons in winter. Larch and
pine are tufted, pinnacled opaque. But the
multitudinous leaves of the eucalypt turned edge-
ward to the light line of dawn or eve, make tracery
which is lovely and rare as network of silver on
apples of gold. It is indigo purple spun on gold.
George Cpllingridge painted it once. He only I
think. Below that lace the foliage falls whorl over
whorl, to the long valley where the mist is still grey
and the mountain side still black.
On the low hills by the river the trees unfold
their dark arms, and fields of wheat and corn
appear. Save where the axe has made death.
There the tall spectres of the night shape slowly
into skeletons. Hark now ! ’Tis an elfin call from
fairy land. From the topmost bough of the
tallest tree a quavering of music ripples down.
It is the magpie awake. But whenever the strain
becomes rich and full, the kookaburra breaks out
incontinent as untimely; the crow awakens with
a groan ; the big white cockatoo with a shriek ; the
parrot and parroquet with joyful but tuneless
calls; the robin and wren, lark and quail with
tweet twitter and peep; raggedly punctu-
ating the dying shrill of the mosquito and
the increasing hum of the abhorrent fly
tribes, who infest the day. One to another,
flock unto flock, flight unto flight. Earliest note
about the time the first of the fairy lace along the
mountain ridge is spun; full clamour while the
first rays consume it, as otherwhere they devour
frost rime and icicles.
As the low rays light the breast of that
world, and the splendour of the full day
spreads fast on to the slopes and searches
towards the depths of the gullies, the clamour
dies away. Like Murphy and his raucous-voiced
cock, the common sort of wild fowl are getting
about their business. But high on the topmost
limb of the dead gum tree the magpie sits and
strangely sings. A broken-linked, bubbling strain,
such as some great human singer might send out, if
memory and power of continuity had snapped
together, but soul and voice of song remained.
Long sings the magpie while the great white
cockatoos flap down on the tasselled com and the
uniform broad grace of the ripening wheat; while
the blue robins and the long-tailed wrens twitter
about the murky scrub and the few belated wattle
blooms. The bell bird tolls “ Amen ” to Madge’s
canticle ere yet the heat of the day is full. A
half-mile up the little creek, where the scrub
growth is thick on the forsaken lead, the bell bird
tolls the sweet “ Amen ” of elfin land. A weird,
sweet sound in that barren, broken land, over
which the full day rolls now in dismal revelation.
MALICOOTA INLET.
The wattle barkers had been there years before,
and our camp was up on staging they had left.
Well above Malicoota water, which runs
inland from a sand bar just west of Gabo,
to the long estuary of the Genoa River. A
lovely and a lonely place. Neither Pop Jackson
nor Broken Bay are fit to hang on the line with it,
in memory’s gallery. Strangely lonely, yet
strangely inhabited while the night held. Un-
familiar calls were made and answered in the big
timber ; the whistle of the wild duck’s wing was
frequent; swans tacked in the ocean of heaven,
squadron hailing squadron the whole night long
“ Xrong! klook ! klook ! krong ! ” Fishes splashed
innumerable, leaping high and falling back on the
smooth water with lusty smacks.
night was long, and dawning welcome. A
band of grey beyond a still slow heaving sea, dull
over its whole breadth as a leaden coffin. Orion low
down to the north, the cross to the south, and a
half-waned moon in mid sky. The long white
breakers dimly seen, be3 i ’ond the Gabo light, as the
tower took shape. The lake a mist cloth, a face
cloth ; Genoa peak a solitary monument, and the
mountain knees of the Big Divide about Bendoc
huge, sombre, menacing. Fearfully and wonder-
fully death-like, corpse-like, is a lone land in the
first grey of the dawn. The scrub birds wake
timidly, the wonga and the bronzewing call in
whispers. The eagle on the gum overhanging the
cliff edge, and the crane on a dead limb over the
shallows, are silent till day is full.
Bendoc is first to see the sun, and a few moments
later Genoa Peak is aglow. And then, to us, in the
low foot hills by the water, appears the sun;
a ball of burning gold, whose rays smite
the cold lead of the ocean to molten, heaving
splendour, and stream over the breakers on
the bar. Seems, then, that the day stands
still, entranced with the perfect beauty all
unveiled and awaking. For the great lake is a
mirror of silver, of steel.' and in the shadows of
the wooded copses, of gltfes. Around all its shores,
doubled by the flawless reflections, is such a braid-
ing of beauty as young Joseph Banks might have
seen round Botany Bay. There are mile-long
ribbands of sand, pink tinted or gold in that
tender light. And within, under the glistening
foliage of the lily-pily, maiden-hair fern thick as
meadow grass dew-beaded and glittering, as a
low ray penetrates the rec'sses.
Over the rocks are the va-lttles. When the rising
bre-ze sways the wattle boughs so as to disclose the
under-leaf there is something of a fairer, richer,
grace than the apples of gold in the network of
silver; and that fair wood-dove, the wonga, knows
it well. Her bower is perfect as is the eyrie of the
eagle, seen over luminous valleys and shining
braes, well swung twixt sky and sea. When he
awakes, that king of the woodlands, and, silent as
majestic and cruel, sweeps out into the sky, all
lesser fowls are silent for a space. His shadow
passes, and the day is full. Genoa Peak is bald
and glaring, and the bones and breasts of Bendoc
hard and haggard. Indeed, th«y are very old.
But down amid the shining leaves and the dew-
beaded ferns and heaths, clipped in with a
marvellously indented shore-line of a hundred
leagues, Malicoota is a single pearl.
F.M.
HERSDIA'S SONNETS
There is formal propriety in the American gift
of a complete translation of Heredia’s sonnets
(Sonnets from the Trophies of Jose-Maria de
Heredia, rendered into English by Edward
Robeson Taylor : San Francisco, Paul Elder &
Co., 3rd edn.) Heredia descends from one of
the Spanish conquerors who wrote with their
swords the epic of the New World. His father,
a distinguished Cuban poet, published a single
volume of Spanish verse in New York, demanding
“le long fremissement ” of Cuban palms above
the abyss of Niagara, and pleading for Cuban
freedom in an invocation of the Fourth of July.
Thus the cordial understanding (or misunder-
standing) between America and Heredia springs
from generations before that of the French writer
and his translator.
That French writer, Heredia the younger, was
born (as it is said) at Santiago de Cuba in 1842 ;
and how his father, according to precise authori-
ties, “died at Toluca, Mexico, May 12, 1839,” is
a matter that may puzzle a Quaker. Heredia’s
mother was of French family, and he came in boy-
hood to receive a French education, to reside
(with occasional voyages) at Paris, and to sit at the
feet of Leconte de Lisle on the shoulder of Par-
nassus. He lived half-a-century before publishing,
like his father, a single volume of verses ; but the
composition of the 118 sonnets, which form the
larger and more important part of this small
volume, represented the slow labour of thirty
years. As a somewhat aggrieved critio has
pointed out, French poets are usually more pro-
ductive —from necessity, if not by choice or inspi-
ration. Only 118 sonnets in thirty years 1 Say
four for every year : one for. each season of the
year. Here surely was opportunity for “funda-
mental brain work.”
Heredia gives it: his sonnets have been medi-
tated well: he is the French master of the sonnet.
One after another they pass in splendid pageant:
a legend of the centuries related with all the con-
cision that Hugo lacked. Their colour, their so-
nority, are obvious attributes. To the eye they
appear like some brilliant tropical plant, its
forgeous leaves glistening with a metallic lustre.
'o the ear they may suggest the falling clash of
the old Conquistadore’s armour in a marble hall.
Each is struck like a brilliant medal into perfect
form ; and as the coin outlasts the city, so it is
possible that the miniatures of Heredia may outlive
the sprawling grandeur of Hugo. In the history
of the French Parnasse, Heredia may be com-
pared to the Arabian genie before he had been
freed from the antique vase that was his prison;
Hugo to the same genie when he rose in a turmoil
of smoke to fill the sky. That Hugo’s power is
greater than Heredia’s one cannot affirm with
assurance. It is more vast, more manifest, and
(in the eyes of the next century) that may bb all.
With the years since 1893 Heredia’s fame cer-
tainly has not lessened. His magnificence of form
was so overpowering that at first critical stress
was laid entirely there ; but now it seems realised
that behind the form there is a poetic substance
more vital and inspiring than was originally
agreed, and that much that glitters may be the
true gold of poesy. Grandiose the manner of
Heredia is plainly, yet by dint of concentration
grandiose can become the grand. To read Here-
dia once is to marvel at his majesty : to read him
twice and thrice is to receive an abiding impres-
sion of his monarchy, He is a model for all the
lax Latins, building in sonnets better than they
know.
E. R, Taylor, who has attempted that task of
Hercules or of young Rossetti, the translation of
Heredia’s sonnets into English, deserves this
praise at least: that his almost inevitable failure
is lit here and there by gleams of success that
may enthrall readers with the original. To re-
create Heredia in English, if that were possible,
needs almost as much time as was taken in the
original creation, Taylor has been able to give
spare time only, and most of his lines and forms
are still inadequate, needing the labour of new
years to polish them into something like ideal
justice. Yet by whiles one meets a version which
is not without its hint of pristine glory, and in
any case the attempt is highly interesting to
students. If in the nature of things such efforts
must err, one can extol the makers for their
courage, and join in tlieir intimate delight when
favourable fortune has lifted even one line, one
word, into felicity. To those who cannot appre-
ciate Heredia in his French, it may be said that
Taylor’s translation does exhibit in English his
subjects, and gives an aspect of the manner in
which they are treated.
For the pleasure of comparison, we quote
Taylor’s version of a single sonnet, linking it with
versions made by a Maorilander and an Aus-
tralian.
A UNE VILLE MORTE.
Cartagena de Indias, 1588-1583-1697 .
Morne Ville, jadis reine des Oceans !
Aujourd’hui le requin poursuit en paix lesscombres
Et le nuage errant allonge seul des ombres
Sur ta rade oh roulaient les galions grants.
Depuis Drake et l’assaut des Anglais mderdants,
Tes murs ddsempards croulent en noirs ddcombres
Et, comme un glorieux collier de perles sombres,
Des boulets de Pointis montrent les trous bdants.
Entre le ciel qui brule et la nm? qui moutonne,
Au somnolent soleil d’un midi monotone,
Tu songes, 6 Guerrifere, aux vieux Conquistadors ;
Et dans l’dnervement des nuits chaudes et calmes,
Bergant ta gloire dteinte, 6 Citd, tu t’endors
Sous les palmiers, au long frdmissement des palmca.
J. M. de Heredia.
TO A DEAD CITY.
City deject, the Queen whom seas obeyed 1
Unhindered now the shark pursues its prey,
And where the giant galleons proudly lay
Naught but some wandering cloud now casts a shade.
Since Drake was here, since faithless Briton’s raid,
Thy walls deserted moulder in decay,
And, like grand collar gloomed by pearls of gray,
Show gaping holes by Pointis’ bullets made.
Between the burning sky and foaming sea,
To drowsefui sun’s noontide monotony,
Thou dream’st, O Warrior, of thy conquering men ;
And in the languorous evenings warm and calm,
Cradling thy glory lost, O City, then
Thou sleep’st to long-drawn rustling A the palm.
E. R. Taylor.
Sad city, ancient sovereign of the seas,
Now unpursued the shark may seek his prey,
And only fleeting shadows float to-day
On waves where giant galleons rocked at ease.
Prom Drake’s rough hand, his rovers wild and stark,
Thy shattered, blackened ramparts crumble yet,
And, sombre pearls in glorious circle set,
Gape the grim muzzles of thy cannon dark.
Between wide skies that burn, white seas that foam,
Neath sleeping noonday’s weary sultry dome,
’Dream, lady, of thy proud Conquistadors ;
Or lulled by sighing, Swaying leaves of palm,
Brooding o ’er glories vanished from thy shores,
Rest in thy tropic night of languid calm.
IF. P. Reeves.
Sad city, O once the sea-queen, undismay’d
The shark may chase the scomber o’er thy bay,
And where of old the giant galleons lay
Only the wandering cloud extends its shade.
Since hither Drake led England's heretic raid
Thy walls are heap’d in ruin, or display
Like to black pearls ranged for the neck’s array
The yawning holes that Pointis’ cannons made,
When from the blazing sky the hard noons press
With weariness of heat, 0 Warrioress !
Thou dreamest of the old Conquistadores ;
And slumberest in the close nights’ languorous calms
Folding unto thy breast thy fallen glories,
Beneath the palm-trees, the long-shivering palms.
C. J. Dremian.
* —-——
Thanks to Sr. Givens’ zeal, the game and play
of copyright becomes more interesting than chess.
Sr. Givens’ zeal is like a star, and dwells apart:
one would not in the least disparage it. Unluckily
the weight of knowledge is on the side of Sr.
Keating, and Sr. Keating contends for the
foreigner against Australia.
The Senate wondered why, on 12 October, Sr.
Givens’ amendment was accepted so easily by his
opponent; and reactionary senators, conscien-
tiously doing their best to injure Australia, voted
I7o with some indignation. The position’s curi-
osity is shown in this : that quite possibly it was
the democrats who gave the reactionary vote, and
the sticks-in-the-mud who gave the progressive
vote. Original clause 13/2 now runs (with Sr.
Givens’ amendment in italics):
2. Copyright shall subsist in every book, whether the
author is a British subject or not, which has been printed
from type set up in Australia or plates made therefrom ,
or from plates or negatives made in Australia in cases
where type is not necessarily used , and has, after the
commencement of this Act, been first published in Aus-
tralia before or simultaneously with its first publication
elsewhere.
Sr. Millen could not understand why Sr. Keat-
ing accepted this amendment with a generous
acceptance. There are several reasons obvious.
In the first place, Sr. Keating had to remove
numerous blots here pointed out (caused, for
example, by copyright being given to any foreign
author who threw a book through the post at
some obscure place in Australia as soon
published). Sr. Givens’ amendment a
means of removing blots without loss of dign 7.
leaving Sr. Keating, indeed, m the pleasant guise
of a man who confers a favour.
[The necessity for registration simultaneous with pubU-
cation remains, and should certainly be secu e y
Representatives.] .
In the second place, Sr. Keating dished the
opposition.
For, look you, Sr. Givens’ amendment goes
beyond the safe line of Canadian preceden , it
goes too far. Not too far, maybe, if Sr. Givens
intends to devote the remainder of his senatorial
life to making his amendment valid ; yet too far
for practical purposes. After 37 years of struggle
Canada has succeeded only in compromising with
Imperial legislation ; and the Canadian public
and Parliament have exhibited more persistence
than this country seems likely to show. Canada
gives the foreign author a month s grace before he
need reprint his book in order to secure Cana-
dian copyright. The Commonwealth Bill gives
14 days only, though Australia is three weeks
farther from the foreign centres than Canada
is. The tendency of legislation is to extend
the period of grace. It is _ recognised that
there is a point beyond which the foreign
author will not go, even when he is kicked;
because practically he cannot go. I he U .M. Act
of March 3, 1905, gives the foreign author 12
months’ grace for printing in the United btates
(provided his book is in a language other than
English) and secures him copyright during those
12 months and thereafter. It seem* within
probability that this U.S. privilege may be
extended by-and-by to books in the English
language.
Thus Sr. Givens, if his amendment is to be
efficient, has against him certainly precedent, and
possibly the merits of the case. _ We can quite
well afford to give British and foreign authors 12
months’ grace for printing or reprinting
in Australia, if thereby we make it more certain
that they will print or reprint eventually.
Canada and America both have found that they
must yield a little in order to conquer; but Sr.
Givens’ amendment stands stark and uncom-
promising, or would stand if it were efficient.
As it is not efficient, and will not be until the
Foreign Reprints Act is applied to Australia and
a Customs duty is levied on new books imported
hither, all that Sr. Givens has done in this
connection makes it harder to reach his end. If
he had only known, he was defeating his own
wishes. Sr. Keating knew, but naturally Sr.
Keating would not tell.
J*
The Canadian compromise, which does actu-
ally bring British and foreign books to
Canada to be reprinted, insists only on
reprint. The type need not be set in Canada, so
that Canadian compositors need not be employed.
Sr. Givens’ amendment insists on the employment
of Australian compositors, which in theory is a
point better, and in practice a point worse; since
it adds greatly to the difficulty of securing the
supplementary legislation by which alone Sr.
Givens’ amendment can be made efficient. One
fears Sr. Givens was injudicious. It was inju-
dicious to overstep the safe line of Canadian pre-
cedent. Within that line we have an overwhelm-
ing case: beyond it the casd ior the other side
may overwhelm us.
What is the effect of Sr. Givens’ amendment as
it stands ? The profit to Australia is practically
none, and there is even loss in the greater
difficulty of securing possible profit. We will
wager Sr. Givens twopence, against his seat in
the Senate : that standing by itself his' amend-
ment will not bring to Australia one book that
would not be printed here otherwise. Indeed, it
is a question whether standing by itself Sr. Givens’
amendment will not drive Australian books to
London for printing, thus securing the very object
which senators who opposed him apparently crave
for. Under Imperial law a book printed in
London starts on its career of copyright in Aus-
tralia independently of the Commonwealth Bill,
and it need not even be registered in Australia in
order to have practical protection. If it be regis-
tered in Australia, it secures Australian copyright
without performance of any of the industrial
devoirs which Sr. Givens presumably imagined he
was imposing. As the Bill stands, Sr. Keating and
the foreign-trader are triumphant, and Australia
is in the soup.
In A White Roof-Tree (and other stories un-
acknowledged on the title-page by the disingenuous pub-
lishers) Ethel Turner completes her baker’s dozen of books
printed. Certainly this popular author deserves a word
congratulatory. In her limited field she is among the
most successful, and her persistence is a rare Australian
quality. Apart from their interest for children and
mothers, many of these books are admirably written ;
and the matter is innocent, gay sometimes, and usually
devoid of sentimentality—that curse of literature for the
young. The publishers should commemorate success by
banishing from future volumes the cheap and often nasty
English illustrator, since it seems that Australian books
can be fitly illustrated only by Australians.
One is happier with “ coogle.” In a Vic.
court the other day, a customer sued a priest for the
return of certain moneys paid for certain masses for
uncertain souls, etc), and alleged to have been misapplied.
The priest won the case ; and it is mentioned merely
because the customer declared that one day he had other
moneys which he did not disclose to a priest for fear of
being’ “ coogied ” out of them (Argus report). What did
the customer mean ? Is coogied a variant of cudgelled ?
or does its sly, wriggling, insinuating shape represe .t a
blandishing idea ?
(Friends are implored not to write more than two
columns in courteous explanation.)
“ With regard to Salvation Army immigrants,
it has been stated in an Ontario paper that they were an
absolute failure. Most of them were not physically strong
enough, and had come out expecting an easy life, which,
of course, they have not got. Whether they will ever re-
pay the Army the fare that was advanced to them is a
question.”—-A.L., writing from Brandon,. Manitoba, to a
Lond. paper ; Sept. 1.

A GOOD
REPORT
From Camperdown.
The Case of
Mr. E. J. SWORDS.
(By a Special Reporter.)
A condition of affairs favourable to
all sorts of ailments that had been in
existence a good while received an ef-
fective check, in connection with which
a newspaper man paid a visit of en-
quiry to Mr. Edward John Swords’
business premises, 150 Missenden-road,
Camperdown, and received the follow-
ing reply
“Yes,” said Mr. Swords, “I can eas-
ily tell you all that is of importance
in connection with the illness 1 escap-
ed from in 1896, when 1 was living at
Trangie, on the great-western line —a
yery hot place, too.”
“How long were you residing there?”
“Three years. Quite long enough to
ascertain that it was principally the
heat which was knocking me out, with
the sedentary nature of my trade,
which is that of a saddler. 1 got to
feel so much out of sorts that 1 could
not stand being indoors!, so 1 had to
leave my work and go travelling. The
clmnge seemed to give me a lift for a
while, but it was not long before 1 got
into my old state again, and as time
wore on my health went from bad to
worse. When 1 got up of a morning 1
felt fearfully languid, and 1 pictured
each day as it started as a long,
dreary term that would never end, and
how I would get through it I did not
know. But even when they terminated
I was no better off, as nightfall only
meant so much restlessness that I did
not know what to do to put in the
time till morning, and when it arrived
and I had to get up, I felt so weak
and low-spirited that 1 did not care
what happened. My internal disorders
caused such a bitter taste to come to
my mouth that on arising I often felt
inclined to retch, but 1 couldn’t, and 1
was never prepared to sit down and
eat a breakfast like a man should do.
As soon as I sat to the table, and the
food came in sight, a sickly sensation
came to my stomach, and 1 would
have to get up and leave
untouched, but, later in the day, 1 was
generally more successful in eating a
little—very little, because my appetite
was weak —but it didn’t make any dif-
ference, as my digestion was gone al-
together.”
“What were your feelings like when
you had eaten ?"
“Dreadful ! 1 can hardly describe
them. My chest had such a sensation
in it that I don’t know what to say.
1 know 1 felt that if I turned round
quiciuv it would break in two, and the
way I sometimes had to gasp for
breath would have made you tlnnk I
was asthmatical. Then I also suffered
from cramps in the stomach, caused,
no doubt, by wind that gathered there
from the decayed food, and if 1 had to
go through the same again, I would
much rather die. Many a time I ac-
tually sang out with pain, which also
used to find its way down my right
side, and almost cripple me. Heaven
knows, I was a martyr !”
“Did you find your weight reduced
at all ?”
“I fell away terribly—from eleven
stone four pounds down to about ten
stone. Just merely a skeleton of what
I had been ; and what made me feel my
afflictions so greatly was hardly being
able to move without feeling incorn
venience. 1 have done a good deal of
rough riding in my time, and used to
enjoy It, but when I was ill. I couldn’t
get on the quietest animal living with-
out the motion hurting me, for be-
sides what I’ve told you, I had ’such
intense pain across my back that I was
compelled to walk bent up like an old
man.”
‘‘H° w long were you ailing like this’”
‘For fully twelve months., and you
had better believe that during that
thus did not continue suffering for
the want of taking physic—no, indeed.
1 took a great many bottles of medi
cines, only, I’m sorry to say, to lind
that my money had gone for nothing,
in some instances, to bo made worse
than I was before. There was one lot
of advertised pills I took which made
me feel that 1 was going to die, I felt
so ill, and after them, thank heaven ! I
procured some Clements Tonic. Ah !
now I soon wished I had taken that
remedy before. What a year of tor-
ture it would have saved me, but it is
130 use regretting-. I’m lucky to have
got off like I did, and there is no
doubt that Clements Tonic is entitled
to all the credit, for that was the only
remedy that could do me any good.
Why, if you could form an idea of the
intensity of the headaches from which
1 regularly suffered you would say that
nothing but a miracle could have
cured them, and if that’s so then Cle-
ments Tonic must be a miraculous me-
dicine, for it cured mine. Besides this
it also put an end to the giddy turns
I used to have. Upon my word, at one
time I could not stoop down without
feeling as if I would surely fall over,
and I have often been sitting on a
chair and expecting to come a cropper
every second owing to the same dis-
agreeable sensation. My sight had
been very dim for a good while, my
eyes feeling swollen, and as if they had
a scum over them ; my tongue used to
be white with fur, too, yet Clements
Tonic sot all these defects to rights,
even when all other things had been
put aside as useless.”
“You mentioned that you had done
some rough-riding in your time. You
require good nerves for that sort of
business, don’t you ?”
“I should rather say so ; but I had
been without them for quite twelve
months previous to using Cloments
Tonic, and would never have had good
nerves again but for that remedy. I
had not only been so nervous that I
could not keep myself from shaking,
but even my mind was easily upset by
any noises, which made me exceeding-
ly irritable, and I was always fearing
some kind of evil. The greater part of
my nights were spent in restlessness,
for I was always thinking, thinking,
and when at last I dozed into slum-
ber, the dreams I had were awful—for
all the world like the nightmare. Well, it
was a happy day for me when I de-
cided to buy Clements Tonic, as it put
quite a different complexion upon the
whole of my career. ! t imparted more
strength to my nerves than thev ever
had before, and there’s nothing in the
world like that remedy to make one
sleep and eat. In a few weeks I
couldn’t locate a pain of any sort
about my body, and the best of it was
that I was gaining strength and
weight all the time.”
‘‘Did Clements Tonic cure you tho-
roughly V’
“Mv cure couldn’t have been more
complete, and 1 trust my experience
may be an object lesson to all who
require treatment. In any case you
may publish this narrative in any form
you desire.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, EDWARD JOHN SWORDS, of No. 150 Mis-
senden Road, Campcrdown, In the State of New
South Wales. Commonwealth of Australia, do
solemnly and sincerely declare that 1 have
carefully read the annexed document, consist-
ing- of five folios, and consecutively numbered
from one to five, and that ic contains atnd
is a true and faithful account of my illness
and cure by Clements Tonic, and also con-
tains my full permission to nublish in any
way my statements—which I give voluntarily,
without receiving anv payment ; and 1 make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believ-
ing the same to be true, and by virtue of
the provisions of the Oaths Act, 1900
Subscribed and declared at Camperdown this
fifttr day of September, one thousand nine
hundred and four, before me,
E. BARNES, J.P.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
The Wind and the Rose.
If you were as the rose, love, and I were as the
wind,
No swathe about your bosom your loveliness
would bind ;
On bending stalk and slender you to my breast
would lean,
Your swooning heart lie open, no fleshly veil
between;
You never were averted, nor ever were unkind
If you were as the rose, love, and I were as the
wind.
If you were as the wind, love, and I were as the
lose,
My petals were the wanton of every breath that
blows;
My beauty you could see not, nor I your voice
could hear
If’t sighed when I was lovely but laughed when
I was sere.
Oh, we were gone with summer that all too quickly
goes.
If you were as the wind, love, and I were as the
rose.
If you were as the rose, love, and I were as the
wind,
Though never you could hear me, and I to you
were blind,
Yet lavish on my pinions your sweetness should
be borne,
Till lovers dreamt ’mid roses, but never felt the
thorn ;
Though Autumn see you withered, the Spring new
buds should find,
It you were as the rose, love, and I were as the
wind.
If you were as the wind, love, and I were as the
rose,
Your life were spent in sighing and mine in fleet-
ing glows;
Mine were no dream, no thrilling to feel your lips
on mine ;
What, then, were all my beauty, and what your
voice divine ?
What spell could break the rigor of passionless
repose
If you were as the wind, love, and I were as the
rose ?
If you were as the rose, love, and I were as the
. wind
Ah, never could I hear you, and you to me were
blind ;
In summer we would glory though summer quickly
goes
If you were as the wind, love, and I were as the*
rose ; ' >
No swathe about your bosom your loveliness
would bind
If I were as the rose, love, and you were as the
wind !
MAORILAND.
JOHANNES G. ANDERSEN.
A Boat Song.
Over the bosom of waters deep,
That sigh as they turn in their dreamless sleep,
Kissed by winds, and wrapped in light,
Sad as the morrow, but sweet as to-night,
We float as a song that the wind wafts by,
As a flower on the stream or a cloud in the sky,
Making the little waves break and flow
Softly and silently as we go.
This is the saddest of all sad lights,
This is the sweetest of all sweet nights.
Faintly elusive the joys of it seem,
As the half-lost words in a long-past dream.
Drifting we follow the virgin moon
In the shining steps of her silver shoon.
Making the riverside rushes sigh
And restlessly rustle as we go by.
What though this hour is wed to its grave,
As dawn is to dark or as wind is to wave ?
What though no memory of it may last,
When this joy is gone with the joys that are past,
We count not the cost of each kiss as it dies,
We float to Love’s time and the light of our eyes,
Making the envious white stars glow
More softly and silver-white as we go.
TAS.
JEUNESSE.
The Cheese Conference.
The little mites that ate and sprawled
Within a cheese of ancient air,
A mighty convocation called
Of all their best and cheesely wise,
To settle how it did arise
That they, and their great cheese, were there.
Most learnedly they all discussed
How their vast, noble, mitey world
Grew from a particle of dust ;
How, in a power magnetic held,
Its parts together were compelled ;
How it from other cheese was hurled.
With argument intense and vain
They settled when, and why, and how;
They spoke, refuted, spoke again ;
Each had his own unbending plan,
How they and it and all began—
But none of them perceived the cow.
And while each scientific wit
That in that Parliament did crawl
Disclosed his plan and proof of it,
A boy, descending on the scene,
Cleared the supporting platter clean
Of cheese, and mites, and proof, and all!
N.S.W.

By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Tioenty-Six
Shillings sterling you can have The Bulletin posted
regularly to you every week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the paper
(and, to an extent, themselves) by taking some care as to
methods of address.
Business letters (with regard to subscriptions, advertise-
ments, etc.) should in no case be addressed to the Editor,
but to the Manager. To send them to the Editor is to
invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters to
anyone by name. “ The Editor, Bulletin,” is the proper
superscription for “copy." Delay, and even loss, is
sometimes caused by addressing to persons instead of
officials. The person may not be there; the official
alivays is.
Special Notice.
Insufficiently stamped letters are liable to be refused.
The contents of this paper are copyright by the pub-
lishers. The right to reproduce or translate any part of
the contents of this number is hereby expressly reserved by
the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and, where practicable,
acknowledge in the “ Correspondence ” column all contri-
butions submitted, whether in the form of Political,
Social, or other Articles, Verse, Short Tales or Sketches
(those dealing with Australian subjects, and not exceeding
two columns in length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially
acceptable), Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper Clippings.
All communications will be regarded as strictly confi-
dential.
Tiie Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed But no
MS. will be returned, or preserved, unless these conditions
are observed. When MS. is forwarded without stamps
for return it will be understood that the copy is to be
destroyed if not acceptable. Personal applications for
return of MS. cannot receive attention. The Bulletin
desires, further, those sending MS. to distinctly under-
stand that, it is not responsible for the preservation or
transmission of MS.

Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-draivn—will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

THURSDAY, OCTOBER 26, 1905.
THE EXCESSIVE AFFABILITY OF
ALFRED DEAKIH.
The session of the Australian Parliament draws
near its end, and what has been done is hardly
worth mentioning. A too-affable gentleman named
Deakin is at the head of affairs, and out of his deep
anxiety to damage no one’s feelings, he has, to a
great extent, let things slide. What work has been
done during the session has been good, but there
is so pitifully little of it. The Commerce Bill, a
measure which simply provided for a little honesty
and fair-dealing in trade, took up—it is not easy to
remember how many weeks of the time of the
House of Representatives ; and other business
travels at the same pace. The majority in favor of
the Commerce Bill was large and solid, but in that
matter, as in others, the Opposition was resolved
that, if it couldn’t prevent majority rule, it would
at least prevent the majority doinganylargeamount
of business. By slow, lingering obstruction, by
interminable speeches, by innumerable amend-
ments, and by constant foolish remarks about a
quorum, a little gang of blatant talkers—almost
every man of them from New South Wales—fills
in time, till, to all intents and purposes, majority
rule goes overboard. The rowdy knot of obstruc-
tives professes that it does all this silly clatter and
tinkling foolishness owing to the unwise character
of the Government’s measures, but their wisdom
or unwisdom is a thing for the majority to decide,
and the divisions so far show that the great
majority is behind Prime Minister Deakin all the
time. The awful affability of Deakin is the
chief reason why, even with a majority, hardly
anything is done. The truculent minority which
has bailed up Australia trades on Deakin’s affa-
bility. There is a general understanding that
the House shall finish each week's work on Thurs-
day, and shall close the session somewhere
about November, and the minority reckons that if
it can fill in the interval, it can prevent the
.rovernment and the majority doing any work
worth mentioning. There would be a chance with
a I rime Minister whose lower jaw stood out like
the track-finder of a locomotive—a man who simply
&xid that so much work had to be done, even if the
House sat on Friday nights and Saturday nights,
and kept on doing so till March, and that the
obstructionists could either give up their week-end
visits to Sydney or let Bills be shunted through in
* vxn . sence. Such a Prime Minister, backed by
a Whip who would find a quorum or die in the
attempt, could do wonders—but Alfbed Deakin
is too affable.
* * * *
In its systematic blocking of business, in its
sourness, its mean cantankerousness, its volu-
bility, and its petty parish jealousies, the con-
tingent of members from Sydney and its sur-
roundings has been the curse and disgrace of
i a j Australian Parliament over which Deakin
sheds so much needless affability. For many
years the State Parliament of N.S.W. was the
dreadful example of the British empire, and it
sent its bad traditions to the Commonwealth
Legislature. It was a Parliament which toiled
not, neither did it spin—except narratives in the
smoking-room. It had so utterly lost the habit of
industry that for the past 20 years it has been
practically impossible to get it to deal with any
large, comprehensive measure on any subject-
close attention and sustained industry were
foreign to its nature. Until the Labor members
brought into it some element of sobriety and
decency, its drunks and its free fights were a
matter of world wide notoriety. Years ago a
Maoriland paper broke into savage verse about it
asking :
Is it lag-dom in the blood
Of a convict-gotten brood
Ever breaking- out in ribaldry with language loud and lewd ?
Or what is it that ails
These men of New South Wales
That they emulate the rhetoric of the bagnios and the
jails?
The Parliament about which that was written had
raised obstruction to the level of a science, and
brought liquor, language, scuffling, and the
neglect of business to the dignity of an art. It
was a Parliament which thought for 40 years
about bringing in a Local Government Bill, and
for 50 years about building a North Shore bridge,
and produced nothing out of all that thinking.
And even when intoxicated members ceased to
rush into the Speaker’s chair and sit there with
waste-paper baskets on their heads, it was still
an assemblage where the maximum of talk went
with the minimum of work. It sent its atmosphere
and its old bad practices to the Australian Parlia-
ment, and Alfbed Deakin is too affable to cope
with the evil.
* * * *
There are such a number of great things waiting
tobedone-if only Alfbed Deakin were not so
affable. There are two transcontinental railways
to be built. The Northern Territory is waiting to be
taken over and opened up. There is urgent need
f n ' a Federal graduated land-tax, heavy enough
break up the big estates all over the continent
and Tasmania, and make room for millions of new
settlers. There is a new tariff to be built—one
that will start new industries all over Australia,
and provide work for hundreds of thousands of
people. Then when the land is made avail-
a >le and the factories are provided, there is
urgent need for a big immigration policy to
fiirmsh men for the land and for the factories.
I here is a loud call for a better military system,
for better defences, for an Australian navy, for
steel works, for an ammunition factory, and for
factories where we will make our own cannon and
our own small arms. Then, even if the formal
taking over of the State debts is postponed, there
is room to do a great work by creating a Federal
sinking fund, which will buy up annually a cer-
tain amount of State securities in preparation for
the time when the debts will be consolidated. (A
few millions to form the nucleus of this fund
might be raised by requiring the banks, as is done
in Canada, to hold at least 40 per cent, of their
cash reserves in Government notes.) There
is that famous peg to be driven in, * which will
be the beginning of the settlement of the Federal
capital question, and so far as is known the end
of the peg isn’t even sharpened yet, nor has the
mallet been bought or borrowed. Or, as an alter-
native, there is something to be done by abandon-
ing the peg altogether, by ceasing to be foolishly
affable to Cabbuthebs, by passing an Act for
taking possession of the M*onaro site, and leaving
Cabbuthebs to howl for redress in the High
Court if he' doesn’t like it. There is work to
do at beginning a negotiation for the federalisa-
tion of the railways, with a uniform gauge and
uniform management. The question of building
a Federal fleet of mail-boats requires considera-
tion. And above all, there is need for a steady
policy of the assertion of Federal rights against
State encroachments, and for a steady movement
towards the time when the State Governments
and Governors and legislative paraphernalia will
be swept away. But Premier Deakin is too
affable to tackle such questions with the deter-
mination that means success.
* * » *
If Deakin would sell three-fourths of his affa-
bility and buy violence and resolution with
the money, he would then be in a position
to make the first beginnings of reform by
putting a heavy heel down on the whole wretched
policy of obstruction. Unfortunately he is too
affable to have any heavy heel to put down.
He wouldn’t wilfully tread on a worm, or on Joe
Cook. It requires a bitter tyrant now to get the
♦demoralised House back into working order.
Such a tyrant would begin by driving the Parlia-
; ment to adopt an uncompromising set of Standing
Orders, and, if necessary, would include in them a
proviso for pushing- Bills through on the compart-
ment system—so much time to so many clauses —
and a proviso that the yap of the long-winded
member “be no longer heard” to prevent the
whole time being taken up by much yapping over
one clause. He would set a good example bv being
himself much more brief, and much more direct and
uncompromising, as well as much less affable. And
when the beginning of the new order of tilings
provoked a final burst of fury from the obstruc-
tionists he would break their hearts and their
stonewall together by long sittings and by the
remorseless use of every weapon with which the
Standing Orders provided him. And having got
the means to push a great programme through, the
kind of tyrant who is required would make sure
that he had one to push through. But Alfbed
Deakin, in the wealth of his affability, doesn’t so
much as tell the obstructionists what he thinks of
them. And if he did it would probably be
of little use ; he is too affable even to think very
hard things of the mob that has built a barrier to
block the traffic, and is yelling behind it with a
wild expression in its hand, so to speak, and a
broken bottle in its eye, if the matter may be put
that way. Prime Minister Deakin is a gentle-
man, which some of his opponents are not, but lie
is a great deal too affable to be of much real service
in politics.
* * * *
And J. 0. Watson is also a viptim of the affable
habit. And Lyne is affable, though the country
is blessed in the fact that he isn’t so much so as
Deakin. And Isaacs, who isn’t needlessly affable,
is a victim to the unfortunate affability of his chief.
It is difficult, however carefully the House is in-
spected, to select among its members any man
who is quite equal to the occasion, so bad has the
demoralisation become. Outside the Federal
arena, Arthur Morgan, State Premier of Queens-
land, and George Swinburne, the boa-constrictor-
looking man who supplies the brains of the State
Cabinet in Victoria, seem about as promising
as any. If one of them occupied the position of
Prime Minister of Australia, and had a workable
set of Standing Orders to limit obstruction, and
got the loan of Bent’s roar (the same to be
returned in good order and condition when done
with) to quell the preliminary disturbance, many
things might become possible. But at present
very few things seem possible in the Australian
Parliament. For slowness, for reluctance to deal
with large subjects in a large way, for lack of
courage to abandon the ancient precedent and to
climb out of the aged rut, it seems as if it
might almost get down to the level of the
State Legislature of New South Wales,
and that represents about the lowest
depth to which incapacity can descend.
The only time when it put up a fine record of
hard, useful work w r as in the days when Kingston
was chief nigger-driver and wasted no uncalled-
for affability on the obstructionist faction. That
time, unfortunately, is a good while ago, and the
great Democrat who did such splendid service is
now little more than a name. Since his time
things have degenerated till they have reached
such a point that four blockheads were allowed
to set down thirty-one fool questions (Bruce
Smith, of N.S.W., asked sixteen of them, and
Johnson, of N.S.W., asked seven, and Kelly, of
N.S.W., asked five and someone else asked three)
about one consignment of imported hats, without
receiving the incivility which their folly called for.
These thirty-one hat absurdities should be the
climax. When this session ends the party should
insist on the Prime Minister taking lessons in
haste, anger, and iniquity, and getting rid of his
affability. He is too affable for this world.
Whether he is also too affable for the next The
Bulletin doesn’t pretend to say. It has never
been there.
THE CAPITAL SITE.
It is quite time the Australian Government put
an end to the long-drawn-out humbug about
stating a case for the High Court, to obtain a
decision as to the legal questions involved in the
selection of the capital site. The negotiations
have served one useful purpose. They have
proved conclusively that Carruthers of N.S.W.
is determined to disagree over anything and
everything which is likely to lead to a settlement.
Having served that purpose, they should now end.
The rest of the business can be done, and ought
to be done, by the Australian Government alone,
without the slightest reference to the disagreeable
and tricky person who misrepresents N.S.W.
The present position of affairs is this : In 1904
the Federal Parliament passed an Act, providing
that the seat of Government should be somewhere
within 17 miles of Dalgety. It had a perfect
right to pass this Act; not even the unpleasant
Carruthers denies that. But the .Seat of
Government Act of 1904 did not assume jurisdic-
tion over any specific area, nor confer any rights
of any kind upon the Commonwealth. It merely
expressed the intentions of the Commonwealth
Parliament. The State Parliament refused point-
blank to grant a site near Dalgety. The Consti-
tution provides that the Federal territory shall be
either “ granted to or acquired by ” the Common-
wealth. The State having refused to “ grant ” it,
it became necessary for the Commonwealth to
“acquire ” it. The main question at issue between
Commonwealth and State is whether the Common-
wealth can “acquire” territory at present belong-
ing to N.S.W. without the consent of that State.
* * * *
The State Premier’s notion of the way to test
that question, is that the Commonwealth should
commit some act of technical trespass, on the
selected site, and that the State should bring an
action in respect of it in the High Court. If he
knew anything about constitutional law, he would
know that such a proceeding couldn’t possibly
raise the real issue. At present everybody admits
that the Australian Government has no more
right to drive pegs in the Dalgety site than it has
to steal pictures from the Art Gallery. If the
course suggested by Carruthers were adopted,
therefore, the only possible result would be a
verdict for the State, on the ground that the Com-
monwealth has no rights of any kind over any
specified area of land on the Dalgety site. We
don’t need the High Court, assisted by expensive
gentlemen in ridiculous wigs, to tell us what
everybody knows already.
* * * *
The real issue—whether the Commonwealth can
acquire rights over the site, without the consent
of the State —can’t be tested until the Common-
wealth tries to acquire such rights. One would
have thought that this must be clear to anyone
who can see the sun at noonday; but the com-
bined intelligences of the State Premier and his
Attorney-General doesn’t seem to have been
equal to making the discovery. They complain
that the Commonwealth Government ought to
have told them before that the Seat of Govern-
ment Act did not purport to “acquire” the
Dalgety site. No doubt the Commonwealth
Government made the natural assumption that
Mr. C. G. Wade, K.C., Attorney-General of
N.S.W., was just as capable of reading and under-
standing an Act of Parliament as its own Attor-
ney-General. That assumption, however, natural
as it seems, was apparently wrong. Prime Minis-
ter Deakin now proposes to raise the real issue by
passing a law actually assuming rights over the
selected site; and Mr. Wade objects that this would
legalise the proposed trespass by the Common-
wealth. Surely it is clear to Mr. Wade that an
Act of the Commonwealth can’t legalise anything
at all, unless the Commonwealth has power to pass
the Act. And the question whether the Common-
wealth has power to pass the Act is the very
question at issue between the Commonwealth and
the State. This attitude towards Mr. Deakin’s
proposal is not surprising in the State Premier,
who has consistently opposed any course that was
likely to bring about a settlement of the disputed
questions. But it is disappointing, to say the
least of it, in a man who, whatever we may think
of his political opinions, has always been regarded
as a sound and careful lawyer. But there’s no
telling what may happen to a man when he gets
into bad company.
* » *
However, the State Attorney-General’s legal
opinions, and the State Premier’s ill-natured
snarls are of no real importance. The
Australian Government can test the question
by simply passing an Act to assume jurisdic-
tion over the selected area, and by taking
possession of it just as if there were no
such person as J. Carruthers in existence. If
that eminent nobody wishes to take objection to
this proceeding, he can take his objection in the
High Court, by bringing an action for trespass
against any Commonwealth officer found on the
site. At present, so far as one can discover from
his published utterances, his only ground for
objecting is that he has an unpleasant habit of
yelping at everything that is done by the Com-
monwealth. This ground seems to be good
enough for the State Parliament. But the High
Court will probably want some stronger reason
for the objection than the unpleasant habits of J.
Carruthers. However that may be, it is per-
fectly clear that the only way in which the
Australian Government can hope to get a legal
decision as to the extent of its powers is by exer-
cising them, and going straight on until it is
stopped. If Mr. Deakin waits until the N.S.W.
Premier has done throwing stones at him from
round the corner, sheep will continue browsing on
the Dalgety site for the next twenty years.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Hat Disease.
Ever since a certain small and wretchedly unim-
portant incident of three years ago, hatters and
hats have been a mania and a nightmare with the
Foreign-trade faction in the Australian Parlia-
ment. That Collection of Superior Persons sees
hatters in its dreams, and goes to bed with its hat on
lest some political problem about headgear should
arise before morning. It lays in extra hats lest
there should be a hat famine, and when it falls
asleep in the House of Representatives it calls out
“ Belltoppers ” in its visions. Some of it has got
the obsession so badly that it now drinks its beer
out of a hat instead of a glass. A little while ago
this benighted party had a recrudescence of the
hat disease. There was a suspicion that an im-
portation of the dreadful article was under-
valued, and the Customs seized the lot by virtue of
the clause which provides that, in such cases,
the Government may compulsorily purchase the
goods at ten 'per cent, above invoice value, and take
its chance of selling them at a profit. If the
invoice was a fraud, then the importers deserve
to lose the goods. If the invoice wasn’t a fraud,
then they get their full money and ten percent.
frofit, which is a very reasonable profit indeed.
t was a matter that was hardly worth a two-line
paragraph in the papers, but the Foreign-trade
mob went hat-mad over it. It got hat-mania in a
dreadful form, and should have been put in an
asylum for deranged hats—if there had been
such a place. This is the awful record of the busi-
ness paper in the House of Representatives last
week:—
Bruce Smith (of N.S.W.) asked sixteen questions about
hats
W. E. Johnson (of N.S.W.) asked seven questions
about hats 7
W. Kelly (of N.S.W.) asked five questions about hats 5
J. Hutchison (of S.A.) asked three questions about
hats.. ..
31
And, in addition to all this, samples of the hats
were brought in and handed round, and silly
members dabbed specimen hats on each other’s
craniuins, and laughed the loud laugh which betrays
the bare-headed mind. The men who did this
sort of thing are supposed to represent the Com-
monwealth. The Commonwealth is a place of
over 3,000,000 square miles, and with 4,000,000
population, therefore it is a large contract—in
area, if not in population. It has all its future
before it, and very little history behind it. Just
now it is troubled about many things—about fixing
and building the Federal capital, taking over the
State debts, building two transcontinental rail-
ways, taking over the Northern Territory, getting
immigrants and finding land for them to settle on
and factories for them to work in, about a
military and naval defence system, about a
new mail service, about paying off a huge
foreign liability, about a new banking law,
about appointing a High Commissioner, about
settling for the State properties taken over, and
other things besides. And a gang of pettifogging
legislators can’t rise above a problem about a box
of hats—which will be honestly paid for in any
case, and the seizure of which, whether the con-
signment was under-valued or not, was perfectly
legal. They were a tired and rather inert lot till
these hats rose on their mental horizon, and then
they recognised that they had struck something
about their own size, and they rushed at the hats
to see if there was anywhere in the heap a few
small enough to fit them. It is a pitiable thing to
see the time of a Legislature—especially a Legis-
lature which represents a whole continent—fooled
away over an alleged grievance which, even if it
were real, is only large enough to take up the
time of an inspector of nuisances. Parliament is
supposed to attend to great questions first, and
to be a fussy and benevolent old grand-
mother afterwards—assuming that it must
be a fussy old woman at any time. If
everybody in private life wasted as many hours
over every petty grievance or small quibble as
Smith and Johnson and Kelly do, the world
would be at a standstill. Many years ago, when
Bruce Smith was still connected with a certain
shipping co., this writer—who was very young
then—had the misfortune to take a steerage pas-
sage by one of his boats, a patriarchal boat that
afterwards left its skeleton on the Victorian coast.
In the course of that trip—between sleeping on a
table, cockroaches, dubious Irish stew, and the
presence on board of a spieler with a smellful
monkey—-he acquired more grievances than
ten consignments of hats would amount to.
If he had been as lingering an ass as
B. Smith he would probably have called
at B. Smith’s office on arrival, and put about 160
questions on the notice paper, and made a speech
of at least six hours, and also moved the adjourn-
ment of B. Smith’s whole establishment. Being
on the hustle for a position as bookkeeper to a
tinned meat factory, he just went ahead and
attended to ousiness. B. Smith and his friends
are hereby urged to do likewise. They are
elected to attend to Australia’s business, and that
doesn’t consist wholly in a deranged babblement
about hats.

More People Wanted.
That remarkable gentleman, Bruce Smith, ad-
dressed a meeting in Sydney last week about the
necessity for more population, and, if a daily
paper report is correct, he decorated his address
with the gross slander that statistics proved “ tJ»j
population of Australia was not only not ad van I-
mg, but actually receding.” The last population
returns show that the population of every State
on the mainland is increasing, and while Tas-
mania is losing a little, there is a steady aggregate
increase, so if Bruce Smith really did say what
the Freetrade daily paper ascribes to him, he
meanly maligned a country in which he has
got a living all his life, and for which he has
done very little except expound law points and
help to run a finance company. Further, he is


said to have explained how the United States
“opened its arms wide to the whole worm
(which it doesn’t), and how “the true Imperial
spirit recognised the right of the King s subjects
to pass from one part of the empire to, another as
they found it most convenient to do so. JN othing
was said about the equally solid theoretical right
of the King’s subjects to have an equal voice m
the government of the empire—which the King s
nigger certainly has not. But perhaps the most
remarkable feature of the Smith logic was the ex-
planation, given in Smith’s best Superior Person
style :
The members of the Labor party had an idea in their
minds that. .. every fresh workman introduced into this
country must reduce the amount of labor which those
already here are employed to do. They lose sight
altogether of the fact that every workman who comes
here not only provides for himself , but gives employment
to others.
After all these years of politics this is apparently
all that Bruce Smith knows! How does every
workman who comes here employ himself. It
he is almost any kind of a worker in iron, brass,
tin, or zinc, in woollen, cotton, linen, jute or silk—
if he represents any one of the trades which nnike
up three-fourths of the world’s manufacturing
industries —he doesn’t employ himself when lie
arrives here. He is simply told that Australia
doesn’t make these things, that it imports them,
that Bruce Smith says that imports are one of the
great sources of prosperity, and that he had
better get away, or apply to the Benevolent
Asylum. It is a ghastly mockery to see a stout,
comfortable legal gentleman —a member of a
purely parasitic profession and also a member of
the closest trades union on earth —rising publicly
in a boiled shirt to say that “every workman who
comes here employs himself” when he knows
perfectly well that workmen belonging to the
great majority of the world’s trades who have the
misfortune to come here don’t employ themselves
—there is no employment for them. When a
workman goes to a strange country he employs
himself if there is employment for him ; if not he
becomes one of the unemployed. According to
the Smith doctrine there are no unemployed, and
there can’t be any. How do the Smiths of this
world happen, anyhow ?

The Old Man’s Will.
The State Parliaments of N.S.W. and Victoria
are just now both considering Bills which provide
that when the cantankerous old person so be-
queaths his property as to leave his wife and
children penniless, the courts may partly set the
will aside and order them to be provided for. This
idea has aroused quite a storm of opposition, par-
ticularly in N.S.W. Assembly; and in the main
it is very illogical opposition. The wisest-looking
owl who rose to argue on the side of what he
thought was ancient precedent (as a matter of
fact, it isn’t—most ancient law recognises, as the
Scotch law now does, the right of a widow to a
share of her dead husband’s property) could find
nothing wiser to say than that when a man
deliberately left his wife to starve —
His reason for doing so might be perfectly good, and it
might be a secret locked within his own breast, and no
tribunal was competent to say whether he had acted
rightly or wrongly.
That looks plausible at first sight, but if this sort
of logic holds good, then, of course, the testator
should be allowed —for reasons locked within his
own breast —to refuse to provide for his wife and
infants while he is alive. To give his corpse privi-
leges which his living personality didn’t possess is
absurd. If a wife or some young children are to be
deprived of food, clothes, lodging and money, and
generally thrown out on a cold world without any
reason assigned, it is reasonable to let the husband
and father commence the process while he is alive.
The reason, whatever it may be, is as good then as
when he is dead. Moreover, while he is alive he is
able to enjoy his family’s sufferings, which are a
wasted entertainment when he is in the mossy
tomb. Therefore, if the two Legislatures are not
going to pass the Bills now before them, they
should be logical and abolish all statutes which re-
quire the old 'tnan, while he still cumbers this
earthly sphere, to support his wife and his off-
spring. If it is proposed to be brutal, there is
nothing like being logically brutal, or brutally
logical. On the other hand, if the two Legis-
latures can shake off a bad modern English habit,
and take a fair view of things, a very good case
can be made out for the proposed, change
in these four propositions. (1) Somebody must
support the old woman, however bad she
may be, and it is as well for her dead husband to
do it as for live people who are not in any way
related to her. (2) Somebody must support the
infant, however villainous it may have become at
the early age of three or so, and its dead father
may as well do that as strangers. (3) If the old
man could stand the wrong and injury of being
compelled to support his wife and infant (even if
the wife was bad and the infant had committed
forgery in the cradle) while he was alive —and
the Law says he must do so—his remains can put
up with the shock also, (4) If he has “ locked up
in his own breast ” a secret reason for desiring to
leave all his money away from his family, that
reason may be creditable, but it is much more
likely to be a girl at the variety show. At all
events, no person should be condemned unheard-
even if the person is an old woman or a three-
years-old infant, and even if the sentence is only
one of poverty, hunger and wretchedness ; and if
the old man doesn’t assign any reason for pass-
ing this atrocious sentence, then it should
be assumed that he has none that can bear inspec-
tion. Even the oldest woman and the smallest
infant should be regarded as innocent until they
are proved guilty.

An Appeal to Bent.
Thomas Bent, the remarkable Premier of
Victoria, after doing better work, for a while,
than any other Premier that Australia has had
for 40 years, shows signs of falling away. During
his Premiership there have been a very large
number of things which Bent has left undone,
but as a set-off to this he did two things that were
so great and so memorable that, if he had only
continued doing them, they would have out-
weighed all his bad deeds present and past.
They would have even compensated for his comic
songs and his bear-like antics, and have left such
a balance of merit in his favor that he should
have been allowed to kill Dr. Em-bung, M.L.C.,
with impunity if doing so would have given him
any satisfaction. During the last financial year
Thomas Bent proved that it was possible for an
Australian State to pay for its new public works
out of revenue, to dispense with loans, and also
to actually reduce its public debt. He proved
these things by simply doing them, and thereby
he committed the best action that any State Pre-
mier has been even suspected of for a generation.
Having got so far, there were hopes that Bent
would go further and follow out his policy to its
legitimate conclusion. To do that would only
require three Acts of Parliament—the whole lot
need not amount to more than a dozen clauses—
providing:—
(1) That all further borrowing should be illegal except
as regards borrowing for the renewal of old loans.
(2) That a certain sum (say, £100,000) B *_ l . oulf P aK
annually out of revenue to a public debt sinking bind.
(3) That a certain sum (say £100,000) should be pawl
annually out of revenue to a Public Works I und for the
construction of new public works. ..
Bent has proved, by actual demonstration, that
these things are possible, and it is only reason-
able therefore to ask that he should con-
tinue doing them, and should make them a
fixed policy by Act of Parliament instead
of a mere precarious accident. 1 erhaps he will
yet do so, but of that his latest financial state-
ment contains no definite promise. It foieshadows
nothing 1 more than a nominal surplus, and instead
of reading like the statement of the extraordinary
financial Bent, who dragged the Victorian
Treasury out of the mire last year, it might be the
work of a Carruthers, or an anybody. Yet
Bent plainly has ambitions to be an unusual kind
of Premier, and in no way could he be more
unusual, not to say astonishing, than by passing
the three Acts above mentioned. The man who
did that would leave so large a footprint on the
sands of time that ordinary Premiers would fall
into it and disappear. He would be paragraphed,
lauded, advertised or denounced in every paper m
the English-speaking world, and he would be
translated into foreign languages as well. _ Also
he would give the Stinking Fish organisation
the most sudden shock it has yet received, and
Bent, whatever his faults, has never been to any
great extent in the Stinking Fish business.. The
Bulletin appeals to Bent to lead the way in this
great reform, because he is the only Premier here-
abouts who looks unusual enough to do so. He
isn’t bound by precedent—no man who had a
respect for precedent could sing the songs he does
or sing them in the way he does —and only a man
who can escape from precedent is of any use in
such a case. Borrowing has become so habitual
in Australia that it requires someone quite dif-
ferent from the ordinary, commonplace, harmless,
stereotyped, bell toppered multitude to escape
from it, and no one can take even a casual look at
the back view of Bent without realising that he
is something apart from the ordinary multitude.
For a while this paper had hopes that it might
prove the right kind of apartness. There are still
hopes, but not quite the hopes that there were a
few months ago.

Carruthers’ Arbitration Sham.
Broken Hill takes Attorney-General Wade’s
Arbitration Abolition Bill so seriously as to pro-
pose a general strike if it is pushed on in its
present shape. What gets on the miners’ nerves
most is the provision that a board may be consti-
tuted in any trade, half employers and half
employees, whereupon three things may happen :
(1) The decision of the board cannot be enforced ; in
fact, there may never be a decision.
(2) Once a board is constituted, there can be no appeal
to the Arbitration Court.
(3) Though the award or decision of the board cannot
be enforced, and though there is no power even to
compel the board to come to any decision, and
though even if the board breaks down it is impos-
sible to go to the Arbitration Court, yet the right
to stride is taken from the workmen. This means that
the workmen are not allowed to do anything in the
nature of a strike if the board should come to a decision
against them, while if the decision is against the em-
ployers, they can strike as much as they like, seeing that
the award cannot be enforced. In other words, awards or
decisions can be enforced against workmen, but not
against employers.
Broken Hill talks about strikes' and arbitration
with a good deal of personal knowledge and ex-
perience. It had just the very kind of board that the
N.S.W. Government’s Bill mentions ; and it acted
splendidly—so long as there was nothing for it to do.
Then there was trouble. The co.’s said it was no
use for the two sides on the board to meet because
they (the companies) wouldn’t agree. They also
said it wasn’t any use arbitrating, because they
knew what they wanted, and were going to get it.
That was honest, anyway. The “ Reform ” brand of
arbitration is much less honest; it gives employers
“the office” to arbitrate and accept the award if
it suits them. It is simply a question of arbitra-
tion and the enforcement of awards, on the Maori-
land plan (and the N.S.W. plan, after all, hasn’t
been a bad copy), or strikes. Carruthers and W ade
cannot dodge that alternative. N.S.W. tried the
optional brand of arbitration, and some of the most
disastrous strikes the country has known occurred
while it was in force. In nearly three years of
serious industrial unrest only one small case of
conciliation and one small case of arbitration were
dealt with ; and at the end of that time a dis-
gusted Parliament refused to grant supplies to
keep the useless thing going any longer. So much
for the optional system in the light of practical
experience.
Very Deaf.
Thorold Dalrymple writes from England about
the very deaf Englishman
The Englishman won’t listen. After twelve months
study of the breed here on the spot, I diagnose deafness
as the great national ailment. Earlier observations were
confined to people of the lower middle strata, but lately
I’ve met a few who ought to have ears for everything, if
they really wish to justify the positions they have taken
as authorities on certain subjects. They have tongues
for everything ; ears for nothing.
Last night I encountered one of the greatest figures in
British statesmanship. He has achieved an international
reputation as “the foremost authority on England’s
colonial interests.” He commenced with the startling
announcement, “ Your country’s a failure, sir ; a rank
failure,” and for the next half-hour I listened with mixed
feelings to nicely-selected statistics from Coghlan, inter-
spersed with bits of Argus leading article and other flum-
mery. I was permitted to say “Yes” seventeen times
and “No” eleven or twelve ; but when I ventured “I
don’t quite ”- the great Imperialist silenced me with a
benign wave of the hand, and finally bowed me out
without hearing one word from his Australian visitor
about Australia. I thank him here and now for the vast
information he gave me about my country, but I’m sorry
he’s so deaf.
Lately I was invited to meet an eminent writer on
Japan. He talked Monkey until he looked apoplectic,
and when I tried to state why we don’t want the brown
creature in our yard, he cut me off with a comprehensive
statement that‘the Jap would be our making. “Ever
been in Australia?” I asked. “Well, no,” he hesitated.
“S’pose you’ve been in Japan, though?” “Know the
country through, sir,” he proudly answered. “ I was
there once for nearly two months.”
And so right through England’s politics and her litera-
ture. The standard book on naval construction as
applied to actual wariare was written by a boy just from
school, who is said never to have trodden the deck of any
vessel more menacing than a Thames ferry. “Special-
ists ” become so by battening upon the work of other
people. But to be “specialists” they must pose, and
above all things they must be oracular and—deaf.
The authority on things is only, however, a higher de-
velopment of the average Englishman, particularly the
Scotch variety. One of the Scotch kind I encountered
aboard ship making Londonwards from South Africa. He
argued with me for three days that Australia (where he’d
never been) was emphatic in its demand for Chamberlain’s
preferentialism, and that great public meetings had been
held in Australia. He was greatly applauded by many
other Scotch-Engiish who’d never been in Australia.
Somebody gave the show away by telling them I’d been
in the very thick of political journalism out there. But
he went on arguing, and said he didn’t care a (something)
if I was the Governor-General and the whole (picturesque)
Commonwealth ; he wasn’t going to have other people’s
opinions rammed down his throat. Still he was no worse
than a University product who assured me, the other day,
that Bendigo was not one of the large towns of Victoria,
and brought out a school book to demonstrate that the
four leading cities were Melbourne, Ballarat, Geelong, and
Sandhurst. When I explained that Sandhurst had become
Bendigo several years ago, be said I was mistaken.
What’s a fellow to do when people are so deaf ?

A Labor Lesson.
Experience of the extremist section of the
Queensland Labor party at the last two bye-
elections and of the forlorn Labor party in Wes-
tralia shows, what it was rather necessary to
show, that there is no special virtue in the word
“Labor” to cover political delinquencies. The
electors are just as ready to punish a Labor
member who defaults on his political pro-
mises as a “Liberal,” or a “Reformer,”
or any other brand of candidate. From the
earlier stages of contempt and hostility the
Labor party very quickly, almost magically, came
in Australia to a position of commanding respect.
Very many Labor men seem to think that this
was because it was a “ Labor ” party. But it
was really because it was a radically progressive
party, which promised to bring a much-needed
leavening of honesty and sincerity into politics.
So long as the Labor party continues to live up to
that promise it will flourish. As soon as it
begins to fall away from it, it will perish.
In Westralia the Labor party won to office,
and the Labor Ministry, led by Daglish (who
now fitly ends his career by renegading)
misused and abused the opportunity. The result
was that the Labor Ministry went out with
ignominy, and, judging from the results of recent
Ministerial bye-elections, will have a hard time at
the polls. In Queensland the Labor Party organi-
sation outside of Parliament has thought fit to
declare war on the men inside Parliament be-
cause, following the sensible lead of Kidston,
Airev and Kerr, they insist on working for prac-
tical reforms instead of crying for the moon. This
intransigeant attitude the electors have now con-
demned decisively at two bye-elections, and, so
far as can be judged, the extremist Labor Party
could not win a seat at the present moment. It
might, at a general poll, keep one or two of the
men with whom it is quarrelling from getting back
to the House ; but that seems to be the.limit of its
power. The lesson from each State is the same.
A “Labor” party can win support from the
electors only by being practically progressive.
It is not sufficient to brand itself “Labor’’and
rattle chains and platitudes in the ears of the
electors.
