Humors of Australian Defence.
A correspondent on the humors of that tragic
farce, Australian Defence :
The Federal fort of Albany (W.A.) has been provided
with a complete set of submarine electric mines, their
object being to prevent an enemy’s ships entering the
harbor in time of war. These mines require careful
handling by specially-trained experts, but no provision
whatever has been made for any submarine miners on the
Albany establishment; and no one at present in the fort
is even competent to lay mines down in the harbor. The
Defence Department apparently intends to forward a
detachment of qualified men from the eastern States by
sea as soon as the enemy’s fleet has arrived in Australian
waters. .
A quartermaster-sergeant-artificer was recently des-
patched from Brisbane to Thursday Island on a tour of
instruction. Entrusted to his care were implements for
taking the impressions of muzzle-loading guns, and his
mission was to teach their use to another titled func-
tionary on the island. Muzzle-loading guns are, however,
out of date, even at Thursday Island, and as the imple-
ments were totally unsuitable for use with breech-
loaders, the impressions of the latter could not be
obtained. It is, however, believed that if secured they
would have expressed very strong opinions on the intel-
ligence of the Defence Department in Queensland. It is
not clear whether the armament of Thursday Island will
be revised to suit the implements, or further implements
despatched to suit the armament. The matter will, no
doubt, be considered when the Council of Defence meets ;
but it is understood that the quartermaster-sergeant
artificer returned, after a most enjoyable holiday, with all
his titles unimpaired.
Swan Island, near Port Phillip heads, is armed with a
little battery of quick-firing guns. Grave doubts, how-
ever, exist as to whether an enemy will ever have a chance
of attacking the fortification if peace prevails much longer.
The works, commenced some years ago, are still without
a concrete apron, and the emplacements consequently
crumble away after the discharge of each gun. A few
more years and a little more practice and the bulk of the
fort will have disappeared. Already a chasm 2ft. deep
yawns beneath the battery, and demands a victim.
It is hardly credible that the Federal Government, after
having already ordered 3618-pounder field guns at a cost of
about £90,000, with 12 more to follow next year at a cost
of another £30,000, has made no provision at all for ranges
on which these guns may be fired with safety. It has
been suggested that suitable ranges cannot be secured,
owing to the dense population of Australia ; but, on the
other hand, artillery ranges have been secured in England
in spite of population, so that difficulty should not be
insuperable. The Minister for Defence, however, pleads
want of money as the reason —“Think what an artillery
range 10 miles by four would cost!” and imagination
falters before the task. But surely it was a pity to
indulge in the newest guns with an effective range of six
miles if the cost of the ranges required for their use in
practice is beyond what Australia can afford ?
In Kyneton (Vic.) the military forces appear to be held
in greater esteem as contributors to the recreation of the
district than in their more prosaic capacity as defenders
of the hearth and home. It is, therefore, only natural
that the local authority (can this possibly be J. W. M'Oay,
M.H.R., ex-Minister for Defence and Colonel of the Regi-
ment ?), more convivially inclined than the military board,
has substituted a band-sergeant for the color-sergeant
authorised on the establishment for the Kyneton Com-
pany of the Bth Aus. Inf. Regt. The result has been
a most efficient band for each half battalion, and has
largely increased the capacity of the Regiment to strike
terror into the ear of the invader. Military discipline
must, however, be maintained, and the Inspector-General
is obliged to regard the change as a very serious defect.
He therefore recommends that early steps be taken to
appoint a color-sergeant; that the company be
trained by its own officers at its own headquarters ; that
its clothes and arms be kept at Kyneton instead of at.
Castlemaine ; and that the cost of taking the company to
Castlemaine to drill be saved. N.B.—-This change re-
quired the interference of the Inspector-General! Who
shall say hereafter that his office is a useless one ?
The 9th regiment of Australian Light Horse provides a
safety valve to the martial ardor of Ballarat and Bacchus
Marsh. Its commanding officer administers the affairs of
the regiment from Melbourne, where he resides ; while
the acting adjutant patronises Ballarat, but does not call
on Bacchus Marsh. To maintain the tone and social
standing of this happy family, all applications to enlist
must be submitted to the 0.0. in Melbourne for approval,
entailing much office work and expenditure on postage.
But Finn, iconoclast, objects ; urging, as most essen-
tial, that the 0.0. should occasionally visit outlying units,
and the adjutant still more frequently, in order that the
supervision essential to efficiency may be assured —and,
more ruthless still, suggests that the junior officer com-
manding a mere squadron is better able to recognise a
suitable recruit when he sees him than a 0.0. in Mel-
bourne in receipt of a neatly-written application (and
pedigree;). .
The object of search lights in harbor defence is to
enable guns to be trained on ships making a night attack ;
it is, therefore, instructive, but not surprising, to find
that at Queenscliff the steam engines used for driving the
electric light machinery are so arranged that the smoke
from their chimneys, when the search light is in use, in-
terferes materially with the effective working of the guns
in the adjoining batteries. The suggestion that the use
of oil engines would be preferable is probably too simple
a solution of the difficulty. The batteries themselves
might be removed, and so provide the Defence Depart-
ment with a fresh opportunity of displaying its'engmeer-
ing skill.
Careful hair-dressing is in future to be a feature of
Federal defence. In other countries hirsute warriors
may still strike terror into the hearts of their foes on the
field of battle. In South Australia, owing- no doubt to a
strong strain of German sentimentalism among its
citizens, the army offends conspicuously in this respect,
and, accordingly, .Major-General Finn “ finds it necessary
to impress upon all ranks, especially in the Militia
Infantry and in the Army Medical Corps, the necessity for
wearing the hair short.”
The Man Westralia Wants.
The financial question is by far the biggest ahead
of Westralia, and neither the Rason party nor the
Labor party seems to have the grit to tackle it in
earnest. In its last year the James Government
let the State drift £148,000 to the bad ; Daglish’s
year showed the lamentable deficit of £129,000;
and for the current year the even more shiftless
Rason faces a prospective deficit of £323,000 with-
out turning a hair. There were a few thousands
of borrowed money that Daglish had authority to
spend, and didn’t; and the only explanation the
shiftless Rason can offer for the fact that the State
isn’t as prosperous as it ought to be is that Dag-
lish withheld these few thousands. “If they asked
him,” he said in his policy speech, “to put his
finger on the spot where the depression existed in
Westralia to-day, he would say it existed because
the Government had not shown that faith that
it should have done ” —and all because Daglish
didn’t spend £216,000 of borrowed money .that he
might have spent. Rason’s one idea of showing
confidence in to-morrow is to go to the pawnshop
to-day. Daglish was an incapable financier; but
the still more incapable Rason blames him for
one of the few bright spots on his administration.
He had just had one taste of the treatment
Westralia was likely to receive from Cohen in the
future ; his £1,400,000 loan cost £IOO,OOO to raise,
and down to this day most of it remains
in the hands of the underwriters. All that
Rason can say in reply is that it will be all right
now—the war is over, and Cohen is open for
business at the old address ; and if he isn’t open
for business at the old address, then Westralia
holds heaps of money itself, and he will borrow
some of that. Rason is too magnificent a spend-
thrift to notice the hard, brown fact that money
is dearer in London to-day than it was when
Daglish tried to raise £1,400,000 in the open mar-
ket there, and couldn’t; and as for the other
string to his bow, he apparently isn’t capable of
seeing the folly of giving Westralia its own IO U.
If Westralia. has money, then it isn’t because it
can’t afford to pay that the State is having
one vast deficit after another; it is simply
because it has the misfortune to have shiftless
Treasurers who won’t ask it to pay. The
Labor party, if it isn’t satisfactory, is a shade
better than that. Even Daglish was better ; for
he, anyhow, did propose some little extra taxa-
tion. And it isn’t a pleasant thing to reflect that
he had no sooner proposed it than he was violently
thrown out, while Rason, who just proposed to
drift—who said that the prospective £323,000 de-
ficit “ gave him no alarm,” wherefore he wouldn’t
go on with Daglish’s taxation proposals, and
would scorn anything in the way of retrenchment
— Rason came back, and his colleagues with him,
returned with increased majorities in every case.
Apparently, therefore, Westralia doesn’t want
honest finance, it doesn’t want to pay its way, and
it takes debts and deficits for preference. In that
case Rason is clearly Westralia’s man.
The Chinese Horror.
A CORRESPONDENT to the RAND DAILY EXPRESS
instances the crowning horror, so far, of the Chow-
British occupation of the Dutch Republics :
A woman was assaulted while she was walking from one
place to another near Johannesburg, and the Chinese who
assaulted her, after having tried in vain to deprive her of
her honor, bit and tore her breasts. I learn that the
woman is now insane, and that she always cries out
“ Chinamen ! Chinamen ! ” and I further learn that, when
the nurse takes off the bandages', the poor woman’s breasts
hang in rags and tatters.
Will Mr. Bruce Smith kindly table another
motion for the free admission of the gentle and
civilised Chow to Australia ? By the way, it is
of these men that E. W. Cole wrote the other
day :
South Africa is importing multitudes of Chinese to
work its low-grade but almost inexhaustible gold mines.
These grand workers will make S. Africa boom, and
multitudes of our young men will be drawn thither by
high wages.
And our young women ? Will they be drawn
thither to have their breasts torn off by the teeth
of Chinamen ?

On Vastness.
Mr. G. H. Knibbs, who, judging from his
speeches, wouldn’t know an aesthetic idea from a
parallelogram if he met them both in a poor light,
told N.S.W. Public Works Committee, apropos of
the proposed Mitchell Library building, to cost
£120,000: —
If we compare the structures of Sydney with those of
Berlin and Washington, ours will be found much too
small for requirements, and anything but imposing enough
for national monuments. Our buildings, the best of them,
would be too good to ruthlessly destroy, but yet small
enough to mark the mediocrity of our aesthetic ideas.
And he then mildly proposed that “ about £500,000
should be spent on the library.” Mr. Knibbs
is a fair sample of a class of people, not
peculiar to but very common in Australia —
a class from which nearly all aldermen and
most politicians are recruited —which has an
incurable confusion of ideas as to the meanings
of “magnificent,” “artistic” and “vast.” To
this class almost anything vast is magnificent, and
nothing is artistic that is not very large—where-
fore, one of Meissonier’s pictures isn’t a patch on a
dead whale or a tailings-dump at Broken Hill. The
prominence of such ideas in a community is a sure
sign either of lack of cultivation or of decadence.
The Greeks at their best were content to put all
that is accepted as greatest in human art into the
building of small temples and the writing of small
books and the shaping of small life-size sculp-
tures. It was the outside barbarians who had
the idea that an artistic effect could only be con-
veyed by vastness. In Australia the uncouth
confusion of size with beauty is very pronounced.
When Government House, Melbourne, was being
built, the architect was instructed to make it like
the ugliest of British Royal homes, “only bigger,”
and the artistic beauty of the place was held to be
guaranteed when it was provided that it should
include a ball-room alleged to be the biggest in
the world. Discussing the grandeur of the Spring-
street Parliament House the typical Melbourne
man will always tell you that one of its lobbies is
as big as the debating chamber of the House of
Commons. Sydney boasts as its claim to musi-
cal fame the biggest organ on earth ; of the
size in the musical world of the man who plays
it nothing is said. The Owe’Sullivan aimed at
making Sydney’s the biggest railway station in
the world, and that unfortunate nest of struggling
shops and fortune-tellers’ rooms (Queen Victoria
Markets, Sydney) was built with the notion of
being the biggest market in the world. All this
worship of bigness is barbarous. A small build-
ing can be almost as artistic as a big one,
if both follow a good architectural style, and
generally is much more so, since the big one, as a
rule, depends only on its bigness for effect. Aus-

N.S.W. Politics—a Cursory Glance.
Carruthers is not a “strong
man. He is a person of great ability in
some respec tSi but cursed with a small, niggling
soul. With a good deal of cleverness, he spofls it
lackoTnbmk 00 WL 7 ’> nd by an durable
{ft-L , P m k Wher f Wlt h a little courage every:
ShU? easy ’ h ? srpw-s tearful in his desire
to be conciliatory ; and only when it is obvious to
nlssS° Cy that th ® tlme for a fight has quite
hmd ilo fi 1 R eVer C °^ e ’ Joseph raise the
loud yelp of defiance. Yet it is this man who is
Z 5 v bei - g f Pasled r actual1 y pushed-by his
Opposition into a niche as one of the masterful
1 remiers of the day. The Labor party in ISi. S. W.
is laboring—sometimes 24 hours a day— to make for
iw a . le P.station, and there is a good deal of fear
that it will succeed. The N.S.W. Labor party
has, in the past, had a deal more luck and a deal
more merciful consideration than it deserved
It, m addition to its other follies, it now
fastens Joseph Carruthers as a “strong-man”
incubus on to New South Wales, its tale of
misdoings will be complete. During last Parlia-
ment, the party supported the See-O’Sullivan-
Orick Administration, which left as its souvernirs
the Lands scandals, the unfinished white elephant
railway station, the Manly tram, and the busted
borrowing boom. For all the shocking incidents
of that Administration the Labor party was
blameable to at least the extent that it supported
the Cabinet solidly. Yet, when the elections
came the electors were quite prepared to forget
all that and to take the Labor party to their
bosom if that party had shown any signs of having
a practical reform policy. But the party refused
distinctly to rest under such a creditable imputa-
tion. It persisted in semi-attaching itself to the
deplorable Owe Sullivan and his loan methods,
and could be induced only to promise to be mode-
rately honest as regards the public finances. Still
the electors, in an incurable spirit of optimism
showed some belief m a party which didn’t profess
to beheve in itself to any remarkable extent, and,
of the three parties going before them, practically
wiped out theO Sullivan crowd, trimmed down the
CARRUTHERS crowd, and added to the strength of
the Labor crowd only. A little experience of that
Labor party in opposition has probably convinced
the electors of their mistake. At the opening of
Parliament, affairs were so divided that Car-
ruthers had no working majority against the
Labor party and the remnant of the O’Sullivan-
ites, and the solidarity of his party was eaten into
by the fact that many of its members were
solemnly bound to vote against any further
addition to the public debt. The Labor party,
which had reluctantly pledged itself to a strict
limitation of borrowing, might have, as a matter
of tactics if not of honesty, raised the question of
financial reform as soon as Carruthers started to
trot to the pawnshop to raise a trifle for the usual
permanent and reproductive tombstone, and the
coat of paint and china pot for the Government
accommodation house at Jenolan. But it didn’t.
When the Lands scandals set all honest noses
sniffing and _ the Carruthers Government was
obviously doing its best to burke a full inquiry, the
Labor party might have justly refused to allow any
public business to be transacted until the action the
public demanded of the Administration was taken.
But it didn t. Certainly it drove the Government
to action, but by easy stages and with a very
tender sympathy for jibbing. From the point of
view of the public interest this was deplorable, for
the delays thus permitted did much to rob the
investigation of its value. From the point of
view of political tactics it was even more con-
demnable, for now the Government is beginning
to plume itself on its rugged honesty in “pushing
on ” with the Lands inquiry, and within a very
few months the Public—the great Public of the
Long Ears and the Short Memory—will remember
only the fact that it v u is the Government which
granted the inquiry, and forget all about the urging
of the Opposition.' Having thus missed two golden
opportunities, the Labor party suddenly woke
up to the fact that it was its duty as an
Opposition to oppose, and began to stonewall
the Local Government Bill—a measure which,
with all its faults, is of real value. So the
Labor party stands to the public eye to-day
in this position, that it doesn’t object to Car-
ruthers continuing a wholesale borrowing policy,
limit which he is pledged and it is pledged,
and to stop which altogether many in the House
are pledged; that it didn’t feel deeply enough'
about the Lands scandals to attempt any stone-
walling or vigorous opposition on their account;
but that it was prepared to do its utmost to help
the Owe Sullivan and the roads-and-bridges
members to keep back a measure of Local Govern-
ment. . Asa natural consequence of this sort of
opposition,” the Carruthers Government has
been immensely strengthened. It began the
session in an almost hopeless position. It will
end it strengthened by many triumphs, with
improved morale and discipline. This additional
strength, too, it is threatened, will be used to
repeal and maim the democratic legislation of
past administrations. The position is not one on
which the Labor party is to be congratulated.
At present there is in Australia a newspaper com-
bine which maintains an agency in London to
supply cable news. By dividing the cost among
members of the combine the amount paid by each
paper is very small. The combine can kill all
fresh daily newspaper enterprise by refusing to
allow anew paper to join its ranks. The new
paper could, in theory, set up its own London
agency and send out its own cables, but then it
would have to carry the whole cost of the service
itself, and no paper could do that; it would be in
trie position of a man who was boycotted by the
1 ram way Department, but was told there was
nothing to prevent him building a tramway sys-
tem of Ins own and being the sole passenger! In
theory, also, the new paper could join with other
new papers and make a fresh combine, but only
the simultaneous starting of new daily papers in
every city could supply material for anew com-
mne, and the multi-millionaire who is to do that
hasn t eventuated. The Federal Senate has taken
steps to break up the monopoly by adding
™ n £° Pyrlght Bill . a c l aus e under which
the Combine must admit new members or lose
copyright in cable news. In other words,
it the old papers conspire to prevent anew
paper getting cable news except on impossible
terms, the new paper may steal the old ones’
cables—if it can steal them while they are still
fresh enough to be of any use to it. Supposing it
is an evening paper, it can do so ; if it is a morn-
-1 i^+u aP i er can * :- This is an inefficient remedy
at the best, though perhaps a better one could
hardly be included in a Bill dealing merely with
copyright. When a measure for the abolition of
Irusts comes along something more may be
effected. But even this partial remedy for a very
ugly system of boycotting has roused some papers
to boiling point. Sydney Telegraph, which
awhile ago arrogantly threatened newsagents if
they sold—in their own shops, which they run at
their own risk and cost —a rival paper which then
promised publication, is especially loud in its
complaints about “interference with private
enterprise.”

Another instance of the pernicious influence State
Governments can exercise over Federal matters
has just been brought to light in connection with
the lasmanian defence forces. In 1901, under
State control, the forces had become hopelessly
disorganised, without equipment, and without
ammunition ; they were armed with M.H. rifles
15 years old, and practically useless; and the
State Minister for Defence described the service
as being “more a menace to the State than a
security. Defence was then transferred to the
Commonwealth, and matters remained much in
the same condition till 1903, when the forces were
re-organised on a Federal basis. This entailed an
increased expenditure for Tasmania of £4OOO to
enable an efficient force of militia to be enrolled.
1 hereupon Premier Propsting had the impudence
to write to Prime Minister Barton objecting to the
payment of militia, as far as Tasmania was con-
cerned. Bigjohn Forrest, though protesting
strongly, collapsed like a pricked bladder before
the attack, and Australia’s uniform organisation
scheme was torn to bits just to oblige Propsting
Ihe Tasmanian militia, finding that, unlike all
similar Federal troops, it received no pay, was
naturally indignant. The bulk of the Hobart
regiment refused to turn out to welcome Hutton
when he visited the village, and Hutton re-
turned their civility by disbanding them. Scandal
a nd much ill-feeling were created, for all of which
the Federation has been blamed, though it now
appears that Eropsting was the real culprit. And
through Bigjohn’s vast incapacity to realise his
responsibility as a Federal Minister every little
State politician in Tasmania who has wanted to
pose as a patriot has, for the last two years, been
able to blither and bleat against Federation all
over the island.

Some evidence recently given before the Federal
Boyal Commission on old age pensions shows that
Victoria owes it to Iceberg Irvine that it has been
Put in the position of acting as a sort of Shylock
creditor to the old age pensioner. The secretary
of the Manchester Unity Lodge of Oddfellows, in
his evidence before the Commission, pointed out
that the result of Irvine’s amendment of the Old
Age Pensions Act was that the Curator of Intestate
Estates claimed the death allowances due from a
lodge to the widow of a pensioner. This was
verified by the Curator, who stated—
In dealing with the affairs of the deceased the Treasurer
has given instructions that first a decent burial shall be
provided. Then, if there be assets remaining in the
estate, these assets are applied in payment of the Crown
debt—that is, the sum paid by the Crown in old age
pensions. Finally, if there still be a surplus after these
charges are met, it is made available for payment to the
legal representative of the deceased pensioner.
The State which can use its officials to distrain on
the little “funeral money” provided by an old
a lj® pensioner s thrift, and snatch that paltry crust
from the widow’s mouth, ought to run a cent, per
cent, money-lending business from its Treasury.
Otherwise its SHYLOCKcapacities are wasted.
By dint of much outcry against “Tattersall,” the
Victorian Government has at last been induced to
take action, and a very remarkable kind of action
it is. Barrister Cussen was turned on to dig up
some law that would enable the agents who send
for “ Tattersall’s ” tickets to be prosecuted. He
got back to 1733, and found an Act of George II
which imposed a penalty of £2OO for procuring for
anyone a ticket in a “ foreign ” lottery. Cussen
interprets “foreign ” to mean “outside the juris-
diction of victoria, * and a writ has been served
on a Wesleyan gentleman named Abraham Hyam
Moses for the recovery of £2OO. Under another
Act of George IV., passed in 1824, Moses is
asked to hand over £SO for having sold a ticket in
a lottery not authorised by Act of Parliament.
Whole tram-loads of arguments in defence will be
raised, and the utter lunacy of speaking of the
“foreigners” of Tasmania, as viewed from the
standpoint of the Commonwealth Constitution
will be the principal line of defence. Wren’s
Tote still flourishes as of yore, and more so
Perhaps Barrister Cuss will be able to find ari
Act of William Rufus or Boadicea to squelch
Wren’s proposition also.

Apparently, “L’Aiglon” wasn’t thought
quite the thing for Trafalgar Day ; so “ Camille,”
which Miss Brune played in Melbourne in the
early part of the year —at a time when “ Camilles”
were bursting out all over the country without
warning has been put on at Her Majesty’s,
?A C ] ney ’ to ln le we eh of waiting for
Merely Mary Ann.” And,, in a manner of
speaking, “Camille” is merely Mary Ann too.
f he as ex plosive as some people feared
that Miss Brune would make her; in fact, with
what seems like a fine effort of self-suppres-
sion, she preserves some eloquent silences
at moments when other Camilles have littered the
stage with hysterics. But there is little grace or
charm of manner about the French beauty with
the stormy Past, and the pampered Present, and
the great longing for a Future which won’t be a
Past later on. The primitive emotions are played
well enough ; Miss Brune 'bears herself admirably
also under the lash of her Armand’s tongue; and
no fault whatever can be found with the way in
which she dies. The final waiting for Armand is,
in fact, one of the finest things Miss Brune has
done. But generally there is no rare quality in
the performance, no delicate handling, no new
suggestions. And she has no help from her
Armand. Poy Redgrave puts no tenderness and
little passion into the part. If pa Duval knew
what a circumspect, matter-of-fact, deliberate
lover Ins youngster is, he probably wouldn’t make
half the fuss he does. J; B. Athol wood, as pa
.Duval, follows the ultra-starchy models of that
well-meaning old gentleman. But why make him
unnecessarily unpopular by keeping upthe ultra-
starchiness to the end ? Why not let him bend a
little, and show that, if he has the stern duties of
a father to perform, he has still the sympathies of
a mere man who can recognise the worth of sacri-
fice m others ? Miss Susie Vaughan is a suitably
frivolous Prudence, and one of the brighter fea-
tures of the performance.

Sydney evidently likes its bushranger, and
plenty of him. In spite of the opposition at the
Royal, Alfred Dampier at the Criterion has to
hang out early notices to the effect that the house
that gazes upon the doings of Starlight is full.
I lie audience always loves the ’ranger who is
altogether good and pure, but the good and pure
bushranger played by Alfred Dampier it just
adores in a blind, dog-like fashion. He has the
knack of making appear quite natural Starlight’s
arrival just m the nick of time to prevent Mor-
gan s bloodthirsty capers, and Starlight’s genero-
sity to the lady passengers, as Alfred Dampier
manages it, doesn’t look as though it were
done specially to please the gallery, but seems
to arise from genuine goodness of heart. These are
the little things that go to make a great actor,
and it is pleasant to have the leg pulled by such a
master of the art. Dampier, jun., now plays the
part of Warrigal, but, although he plays it very
well and with heaps of activity, Sydney has grown
accustomed of late to having' its Warrigal speak
with a Scotch accent. Young Alfred will
have to cultivate a Scotch accent for the expres-
sion “My worrd ! ” Miss Rose Dampier is inclined
to give Aileen Marston a most unbush-girl-like
drawl, but the other members of the company
back up the central figure as he deserves to be
backed up.

Miss Eugenie Duggan continues to bush-
range nightly under false pretences at Sydney
Royal, and Laurence Dunbar, as Thunderbolt, is
II °/ P ersori in the theatre who doesn’t
tumble” to the base deception. It is just as
well, of course, that he doesn’t. If he did, then
the most exciting parts in the melodrama of
Ambrose Pratt and A. S. Joseph would have to be
cut out, including the most ingenious and ex-
citing section of the story—the plan to sub-
stitute somebody else for Miss Duggan in
custody, followed by Thunderbolt’s extra-final
stickmg-up job in which he liberates Miss
Duggan, so that at 11 o’clock there isn’t
anybody in custody at all. Something ought to
be done, however, to improve matters with regard
to the arrest of Miss Duggan, in the first place,
lhe impression the scene leaves on this writer is
that Thunderbolt just about sacrifices the assistant,
who had saved his life, in order to again save
his own neck. There isn’t any hanging about to
see that the youngster is safe. And this casts a
reflection upon the benevolent bushranger’s char-
acter. The piece retains its popularity, however,
and its brisk movement has been appreciated by
large houses throughout the season.
** # *
Sydney Tivoli has a very strong team of
entertainers just now, there being no less than
five “star” turns to relieve the efforts of the
comedian with the bit of mirror stuck on his nose,
and the coon singer, and the sad soubrette, and
the other somewhat weary items. It is an axiom
of the variety business that you must not have all
the programme first-class : there must be variety
of good and bad, bright and dull. Rickards pro-
vides at present so unusual a number of first-class
turns and of good second-hand ones that the inter-
vals of dulness are very few. Latest comers
Crimmins and Gore —a lady who is certainly the
longest and thinnest yet seen on the vaudeville
stage (except as a living skeleton), and a man
who makes up as about the shortest and fattest on
record—open in a shriekingly funny sea-side
sketch, which gives more promise than is fulfilled
in their later efforts. But, on the whole, they are a
genuinely comic pair, and, with Hill and Whitaker,
Les Bruniu, Payen and Lucie, and Diamond and
Beatrice, give the public big value in mirth for its
money.

A revival of “ The Orchid,” billed for six
nights only, broke out at Her Majesty’s (Melb.)
last Saturday with all the enthusiasm of a musical
comedy that has come to stay for three months.
Also, Melbourne vvas there in vast numbers ready
to roar at its Lauri (who took himself too boister-
ously at times) and receive the plaintive ballad of
“Little Mary ” with unqualified joy. When the
song was new a good many people, it seems, failed
to catch the true significance of its burden, but now
they all know what “Mary” means, and the effect is
more touching. For several reasons “The Orchid ”
is an easy matter for praise. The music bites
one’s ear, the story has humor and a pinch of salt
in it, and the chorus dressing is generally known
as a dream—or several dreams. Changes in the
cast since the previous production are Miss Olive
Godwin where Florence Young used to be, and
John Doran in place of the droll, careworn citizen
who formerly took charge of his uncle’s flower-pot
when he went away on the wrong honeymoon
Comment on the new comers can wait until next
week, should “The Orchid’s” second crop of
success remain standing in the Williamsonian field
longer than the advertised period. “ Veronique,”
for the first Australian time, is understood to be a
joy in waiting, but there are obvious business
reasons why the present revival shouldn’t be
hastily cut down.

The first of two performances of “ Every-
man ” is due at Melbourne Town Hall this after-
noon, the curtain if there be any curtain —rising
at 2.30 precisely. An interval of two days will
give the Old Morality Drama a chance to get
acclimatised before being repeated on Saturday
night. “Everyman,” needless to say, is not
divided into acts, or embellished with a ballet. It
was written by the monks of old, who didn’t
believe m intervals for refreshments, but were
mindful of the fact that Morality should not out-
stay its Christian welcome. The lesson lasts
about 90 minutes, so a matinee performance,
beginning at 2.30, may be appropriately discussed
over a subsequent cup of tea.

Bland Holt, also, is preparing a drama
with a seasonable moral in it, for the warning of
his brother Everyman. Bland’s morality is
entitled “The Betting Book, or the Gambling
Evil and it will be impressively presented at
Melbourne Royal on Saturday next (28th).

Spraying Fruit Trees.— Mr. G. A. Jones
writes from Bomaderrv ' —l have about 700 orchard trees,
and everyone says they are the cleanest trees they have
ever seen. I attribute their cleanliness and freedom from
disease solelv to proper spraying. I have used every kind
of wash for diseases of fruit trees that has been recom-
mended by the Government, but Sunlight Soap is the best
of them ail. It will cure a tree of San Jose scale, or red
scale, or olive scale. One tablet of Sunlight Soap should
be sliced up into two gallons of water and boiled until the
soap is dissolved, and, when nearly cold, spray on the trees
in the afternoon, when the sun is not too hot. For peach
aphides (which come on the tree when the trees are in
blossom and eat all the flowers off, which disease does
some thousands of pounds’ worth of harm in New South
Wales every year) I make the solution stronger, that is,
I add two tablespoonfuls of Lever’s Dry Soap (Sunbeam
Washing Powder) to every four gallons of the above spray,
and I spray the trees just before the buds burst. It will
also kill tlie aphides on rose trees, cabbages, and, Swede
turnips.*

A Melbourne correspondent scoffs :
The burlesque landing of Captain Cook at Botany Bay,
which some foolish persons carried out during the Com-
monwealth celebrations, was recalled at Melbourne Town
Hall, on Saturday evening last, when some misguided
persons perpetrated a fake tableau of the Death of Nelson.
The affair was sufficiently ridiculous to cause Nelson to be
remembered by the youngsters who witnessed the show
as a cross between George Lauri and Tommy Bent at his
best. One of the .most amusing features of the shivoo
was the appearance of all the noble tars of Trafalgar with
short hair. The committee guilty of this degradation of
a great historical event to the level of a Dan Barry melo-
drama had been seeking for a week for pigtail wigs, but
Bland Holt had cornered the Melbourne pigtail market,
and none were available. Just as the show was about to
open, on Saturday evening, a breathless yellow gentleman
arrived from “ Lillybuk-stleet ” with the offer of 50 false
pigtails used in Chow processions. He was dismissed
with scorn.

At the Opera House the golden harvest-
time has been marked by some further varia-
tions in the performances, but all the names
on the bill are what they were a fortnight ago and
earlier. The Mephisto illusionists accounted for
the boom at the outset; the Jackson Family and
other people help towards the rejection of hard
cash on Saturday nights. Ryder Slone is an artist
among comic songsters. His ditty concerning
Muggins might be a poor thing enough if another
man gave it lip in an orthodox music-hall way.
Slone is an unorthodox workman, and his Muggins
appeals to a mixed audience’s heart.

The Age of September 1 (this is going a
little back) reported Mr. R. J. Alcock, of Mel-
bourne, as having asked the Minister for Trade
and Customs at a deputation re the Commerce
Bill that'“merchants should not be required to
brand each tin with the number of ounces of jam
or other stuff it. contained ; that it should be
sufficient if they stated the exact weight to the
men to whom they sold the goods and did not de-
ceive them.” On.this The Bulletin commented,
Sept. 14, that the suggestion was a “ cheeky ” one,
and showed a notion of business ethics not likely
to be generally commended. Subsequent inquiry
and correspondence with Mr. Alcock shows that
that comment was based on some misapprehen-
sion and therefore not fair. The deputation, it
seems, was discussing the export trade, and the
request was made that it should not be necessary
to put the weights on packages of goods exported
to Asia, so long as the buyer was not deceived.
Mr. Alcock writes
It was felt that the producers and manufacturers should
not be debarred from supplying their goods for export to
foreign countries in any size packages demanded, pro-
vided, of course, that there was no attempt at misrepre-
sentation. It was never for a moment suggested by me
or any member of the deputation that a different weight
should be marked on the package to that it contained.
That puts a quite different complexion on the
matter. To sell to John Chinaman a tin of jam
with no weight marked (which he is presumably
quite content to buy as a tin and on which a
weight label would convey no useful information to
him) is fair business; to sell locally a 14oz. tin of
jam branded as containing 16oz. would be pretty
close to robbery. The Bulletin regrets that the
earlier comments (based on a newspaper report
which was hardly accurate) did Mr. Alcock an
injustice, and is glad to make this amende after a
full inquiry into the facts.
Something like the most amazing citizen on
record sought redress from the Prahran (Melb.)
Bench lately, when John St. Clair Farquhar
Kirkpatrick charged his wife with the larceny of
a packet of letters, which he valued at £l5O.
Boiled down, the recent history of Kirkpatrick
disclosed that, when a clerk in the Union Bank,
two years ago, he married a handsome young
woman, with whom he was still living. Pie had
left his situation eight months ago, and had been
more or less loafing on a friend since then. Six
months after marriage be became acquainted with
a young lady, whom he subsequently betrayed,
and it was her love letters —which his wife had
found and taken possession of—-that he wanted to
recover. He solemnly assured the Bench that he
“would not take the title deeds of the city of
Prahran for them,” and explained that he had
arranged to elope with the ruined girl. Appa-
rently, however, he had neglected to do it, and
added one more to a record of failures. The
Bench dismissed the charge with scorn, and called
Mr. Kirkpatrick a name not often heard in the
law courts.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“ Thorold Waters Judging from the press appraisement
of him, and the one scant par that chronicled his death in
Melbourne, few Australians seem to have understood the
position once held in the English singing world by the late
veteran tenor William Parkinson. I always knew that he
had been a big man in his day, but until coming to
England I never dreamt that he had been so big that 20
years’ burial as a singing coach in a Melbourne suburb
had not sufficed to obliterate his memory. Now before
me is a recently published book of “ Musical Recollec-
tions,” by Bessie Palmer, .once at the top of the list of
English oratorio singers, in which several references are
made to Parkinson, and a quoted criticism relates that
“ for years we have,heard Mr. Parkinson sing, and though
always pleasing, he seems now to have so completely
mastered and improved his voice as to be as near a great
singer as it is possible to be. . . . He fairly stormed the
house.” Within the past year his name has recurred
several times in London journals, and a couple of months
ago the Daily Telegraph, in chronicling the death of an
opera star, laid a special tribute at the feet of the retiring
old tenor, who has passed out in Melbourne with so little
notice. In the North of England, to mention that you
were a friend of “Bill” Parkinson is to secure open
sesame to the hearts of the Geordies. I remember as a
pressman being recalled from a holiday to help write a
page of “ weep ” about Softgoods Sargood when he died.
Parkinson achieved a 12-line' par in just one paper. At
this distance the contrast is particularly striking.

somebody about these theatrical. companies. A little
entertainment is good in'season, and not unbecoming a
philosopher ; but the spectacle: of six companies desper-
ately fighting for the patronage of a one-show town is a
worry to the mind and a weariness to the flesh. Perth, at
time of writing, is very much entertained —and very much
tired. Williamson’s Repertoire Co. is Gilbert and Sulli-
vaning at His Majesty’s ; Johnny Sheridan is mashing
‘The Lady Slavey’ at the Royal ; Nellie Stewart is wait-
ing for an opening to plant ‘ Sweet Nell's ’ feet upon the
boards; and Stephenson’s opera, co. waves its limbs and
whirls its lingerie in the background.Bostock and VVom-
bell’s menagerie has just been let loose upon this afflicted
town, and Wirths’ circus is threatened. Leonard Davis
has arrived to re-open the Palace Gardens; the ‘J.P.’
company is announced, and a visitation from Harry
Rickard's is a matter of daily dread. On Sunday nights
the bands wrestle with the parsons for the threepence,
and theatrescopes and orchestral societies, and other
small fry, fill up the week.”

Irving’s greatest distinction came to him after death.
He was the first man to have his ashes buried in West-
minster Abbey. His predecessors, turned to dust in the
old gruesome way, or were embalmed and are decaying
slowly. None of them was cremated.

The Sydnev, Summer Nights Amusement Syndicate
hopes to turn an honest penny by means of the motor
craze. It has announced a gymkhana on Sydney Agri-
cultural Ground. One event is “Musical Cars.” The
motor-cars are to waltz round the arena, each with a
motorist and a lady inside, while the band plays. In the
middle of the ground there are to be a number of flags,
and when the band stops playing all hands drop out of
their motors and make a dash for the flags. It isn’t very
clear what happens afterwards, but presumably the
swiftest lady gets the best flag. Whether the slow ladies
have to kiss anybody by way of forfeit isn’t known yet.
Lingering’ memories of “ Our Boys,” a popular drama of
his younger days, seem to have helped to inspire Premier
Bent’s favorite saying that he always takes off his hat to

“ Out of bacon alone I made a small independence. Why,
I never comes across a pig in the street now but what I

“ Edyson ” : Although I cannot call myself one of the
younger generation of playgoers, it was my lot to see
“Our Boys” for the first time at the Bijou the other
night. I ' went mainly because it is one of the plays of
our fathers. Since my earliest youth I had heard “Our
Boys ” raved about as the height of excruciating comedy,
and my expectations were great. I found it uncommonly
dull. ‘Possibly the acting was responsible for much of
its -loom, as many of the actors who couldn’t and many
of the actresses who shouldn’t, were, engaged in the
lamentable execution of Byron s comedy. .But the
retired Butterman was the work of an old favorite in the
part and ancient critics assure me through the press
that he has lost none of his drollery. Yet I didn’t find
that representative of ancient grease any more comic
than an ignorant alderman at a banquet in our tune.
There is more material for laughter in three minutes of
Pinero's policeman in “Dandy Dick’’than in the three
acts of Perkyn Middlewick.
The late Herbert Campbell, a cockney music-hall singer,
whose bio- rasping voice and rough humor made him a
valuable'’comedian in the Drury Lane panto, for many
years left a few thousand pounds behind him. Also he is
said to have left a last will and testament in some dark
place which has not been discovered. A law suit re the
rnissin-- will turned mainly on the question whether
Campbell’s unwed wife (of 12 years standing) was
entitled to the three-fourths of the income from his
estate, which he had bequeathed to her in a draft will.
Testator’s executors propounded this draft, and the court
finally decided that it was a reasonable document and
should be admitted to probate. But by the time the
opposition was defeated Herbert Campbell’s estate had
passed away in legal expenses. One of the counsel
engaged jauntilv remarked that “ the oyster had gone.”
Only" the shell'was left for the dead man’s best girl.
Moral—Don’t lose your will after your decease.
“Kaylee”: Saw my first living Aunt Sally at the
Adelaide Government House Charity fete and have
pondered much on the possible consequences supposing
several people had chanced to hit their alleged relative
Sarah at the same time. Government House might not
only have been left with a dead Aunt Sally on its hands,
but the Law might have had to arrest all fashionable
Adelaide for manslaughter. Some day a man with a
straight eye will come along and hit the lady on a tender
spot and there will be results. It might be wise if
Parliament made Aunt Sally Slaughter a crime punishable
by a fine not exceeding 2s. 6d.

“ Yarrab ” : A late production of “ The Fires of St.
John ” has set the dramatic scribes of this country ablaze
with comparisons of Sudermann and Ibsen. Though
the aforesaid conflagration flared up for a night only, the
embers of the paper men’s ideas of the two dramatists
still smoulder. ’Tvvixt Sudermann and Ibsen there is a
wide difference. Though they in places apparently belong
to the same school, the former is merely a carcase
butcher, the latter a skilled surgeon. Sudermann takes
hold of a theme, and, though often varnishing beautifully
with literary embellishment, fails in execution. Fails
also in technique. He throws his opinions roughly,
immaturely, before the public. His plays, which con-
tinually joit in action, all end abruptly. With the forcible
old Norseman the work is differently executed. The
natural order of things is Unswervingly maintained, and
though ofttimes descending into the morbid and the
antagonistic in order to obtain the and
shade, the rhythm is gloriously kept. He Ynoves his
characters with chess-like precision, and though his
command of literary adornment is comparatively finite,
his treatment and choice of characters are so admiiably
arranged that Ibsen leaves a lasting impression where
Sudermann would only cause a remark. To maintain
that the white-headed old -NprwegiSh and the rough
carpenter from Germany are allied, in class and style is a
fallacy. ' ",
“Melb.”: Bob Heir, the once popular actor, was
touched upon at the meeting of the Dramatic and Musical
Association, the other day. His grave at Invercargill
(Maoriland) is neglected, but good resolutions were made
about putting it in order.. G. V. Brooke came to Mel-
bourne accompanied by Miss Cathcart, as his pupil. Heir
joined the company in Melbourne, under Mr. Coppm. His
engaging teacher was Miss Cathcart. They married. The
most distinct recollection of them now is in their support
of Barry Sullivan throughout his long and distinguished
management. J. F. Cathcart (Mrs. Heir s brother) came
with Mr. and Mrs. Chas. Kean. The Heirs played in
opposition, with Sullivan at the Royal, while the Keans
were at the Hayniarket.
“S.S.”: Noting the feminine enthusiasm over Julius
Knight’s somewhat meaty, somewhat Napoleonic, des-
perately humorless David Garrick, and remembering how
female Melbourne once took to the slimmer, brighter,
neater David of “Curly” Bellew, I profess to discover a
decided change in the Australian woman’s taste for actor
man. She liked him spare and trim when Bellew was on
these boards, but she now asks for fat with her actor.
Julius tends that way, so did the recent American, Mack.
Certainly Cuyler Hastings had hard,edges, and. was not
fatter than a'hurdle, but after all he did not get a real
hannnerlock on the affections of the girl, the girleen, and
the elder specimens. On the whole, I think Tom Bent
made the mistake of his life in not sticking to the stage
when he took to it.
Florence Young was in a sensational boat accident in
the Hudson before leaving America, and a damp and
clammy Australian song bird was fished out of the river
after being in the water longer than was good for her. A
boat in which Flo and a bunch of friends were picnicking
turned turtle ; hence the excessive dampness and the
over-Flo. . .

“ Yarrab ” : What combinations apropos the occasion
are sometimes fluked by the biograph. On the evemng-of
Yom Kippur, I journeyed into the “Pa ace to see a bit
of the world whirled before me. One film revealed The
Wandering Jew” laboriously trudging over a broken
landscape with a liberal rock formation. The faulty
macadam upset the locomotion of the Peripatetic Semite,
and he spread down in an old elo’ heap to sleep. His
dream depicted the Journey to Calvary, and he awoke
with an expression on his face akin to mortal dread. A
quartette of the Chosen People—two of each sex-sitting
on my starboard side, grinned at the whole performance,
and one of the ladies voted it “ boskar. Truly, the Day
of Atonement hath lost its significance.
Horace Lingard made his first Melbourne appearance at
the Royal (March, 1876) as Perkin Middlewick, in Our
p )OVS ” —a part which he recently played again, for the
manyth time, at the Bijou. On its first Melbourne pro-
duction the piece ran for eight weeks to overflowing
houses. Dampier played Talbot Champneys, and the
beautiful Alice Dunning Lingard was Mary Melrose.
“ o’F.” : The man who wants to tell you how it is done
has been strongly in evidence at Melbourne theatres
lately. I vainly tried both entreaty and insult to make
him refrain from telling me all of Thurston’s secrets. He
(or another of his detestable race) insisted on explaining
the coin and flag manipulation at Rickards show, and a
few nights later he wanted to teach me how to give the
illusions of Leroy, Talma, and Bosco next time 1 was at
an evening party. I thought I would be free from him at
“The Cingalee,” but the unspeakable pest bobbed up
With information about Lauri’s Thurston travesty. It
was the last straw. I took him out, ostensibly for a
drink, and he is now being advertised for daily 1,1 the
“ Missing Fiends ” column. In justification I may plead
that I don’t want to know how the tricks are done ; I
prefer to remain in awed ignorance. And if I did want
to know I would consult a manual of legerdemain and
illusion at the Public Library, instead of listening to the
qackle of some fool, who knows everything but does
nothing.
Henry James Byron, author of “Our Boys” (recently
revived in Melbourne), turned out in 25 years at least
130 plays lone-handed, besides collaborating- with Bouci-
cault, Farnie, etc., and appearing as an actor. H. 6 was
responsible for 10 of the plays produced in London in
1871. In his burlesques, Byron was the most inveterate
punster of the pun-worshipping period, which ended with
the decline of Nellie Farren, and the replacement of
burlesque by musical comedy.
Carrie Moore, it seems, has scored the hit she was ex-
pected to make in the “ Blue Moon,” at the Lyric Theatre
(London), where she has just such a chance as she used to
get in the Williamson show's. A recent Poverty Pointer,
referring to the fact that the Australian girl was not
going to be the “ prima donna ” of the co., misrepresented
the situation somewhat. “ The Blue Moon ” appears to
be much of a muchness with “The Cingalee” (the scene
laid in Burma instead of Ceylon), and Carrie Moore’s part
corresponds to Rose Musgrove’s in the other musical
comedy, whilst the principal ballad singer (a Miss Florence
Smithson) fills a place similar to that given to Miss
Thomas in “The Cingalee.” Both girls are warmly
praised, the one as a dainty ingenue vocalist only, and
Carrie as a dashing, w'arbling comedienne. She, like Rose
Musgrove, has a duet with the funny man, in which an
imitation of the Pygmies bears a strong family resemblance
to the Gollyw'og business of Rose Musgrove and Lauri. Her
old colleague, Harold Thorley, is a successful hero of the
flimsy yarn, and the production is generally praised for
splendid staging and pretty faces. So Carrie Moore has
made a name in an important London attraction —thanks
to the fact that the show is run by Robert Courtneidge,
who selected her (for a Liverpool panto, in the first
instance) on his personal knowledge of her Australian
merits. “ The Blue Moon ”is Courtneidge’s first big
speculation in London, and, because he backed his judg-
ment of the Williamsonian co.’s “form” by engaging two
ex-members for first-class parts, theatre-goes on this side
will wish him luck. Writing of Carrie Moore’s perform-
ance to a friend in Melbourne he says it was “very good,”
with the “ very ” underlined, which, coming from him, is
equivalent to ‘a string of florid adjectives from a more
effusive critic.
Marie Studholme, who may head an Edwardes company
that comes to Australia next year to play “Lady Mad-
cap” and other pieces, is perhaps the most-photographed
woman in the w'orld. She is enormously popular just
now. At Dublin, t’other day, 18 policemen were em-
ployed to keep the crow'd away from the stage door. This
paper doesn’t believe it, but will try to look as if it did.
“Lam”: They are genuine, all genuine. Not only do
the open work stockings prove it, but I have it from the
lips of his dresser, who raves over them with enthusiasm.
Be it understood that I speak of Julius Knight’s legs—the
ones he displays w'hen he plays Microbe Tremendous.
After abandoning the concert platform for a couple of .
years, Queensland soprano Florence Schmidt is returning
to the old love, and intends to give a recital in London
during the autumn, whilst she is also on the warpath for
other engagements. When marrying sculptor Derwent
Wood, the Bananaland songstress was urged by her hus-
band to give up concert-singing, and the matter was
clinched when she lost some hundreds through the failure
of a leading London manager. A return to the platform
after retirement inevitably' means the repetition of much
uphill work even to those who have been most successful,
and Miss Schmidt shows considerable pluck in her new
decision.
It is twenty-one years since Mary Anderson first
appeared in Gilbert’s “ Comedy and Tragedy” at London
Lyceum. W. Muskerry’s “ Davy Garrick ”is also past its
first youth. With Edward Compton playing lead, it was
produced-at London Strand in 1886. The playwrights
snatched Garrick for a hero almost before the breath was
out of his body. A farce, entitled “Garrick in the
Shades, or A Peep into Elysium,” was produced soon after
his death in 1779, He is the principal male character in
at least nine plays, besides being introduced in many
other plavs and novels. The authors of most of these
works depict a painfully ideal Garrick, instead of the
mean man who reproved his temporary domestic partner,
Peg Woffington, because she didn’t count the leaves she
put in the teapot. The Garrick worshippers also forget
to record that when the affair was broken off, Peg
promptly returned the presents David had given her, but
her miserly ex-lover could not be induced to part with
the diamond shoe-buckles she had given him.
“ Melb.” : We expect something great from Williamson
in pantomime this year. It will be remembered that last
season he-was choked off through a shipwreck of props.
A photographer in the North of England claims that be
has set up a record in the sale of photo post-cards with
that of Mary Conly, erst of Melbourne. The plump little
soprano is now a festival singer in the critical north of
Chamberlain Land, and is far and away the most popular
vocalist visiting Scarborough, Bridlington, and other
Yorkshire watering places. This is attested by the fact
that, in a single week at Bridlington recently, 1254 of her
photo post-cards were.sold at one shop. The photo-
grapher says he had never before sold more than 100 cards
of even the greatest of stars in one week.
Old Joseph Docker, a politician of the
past, and father of the present Judge Docker, was
the first person to receive a grant of land in Aus-
tralia under the charter by which the East India
Company was entitled to property in a general
way “ east of the Cape of Good Hope.” The pro-
perty was situated near Scone (N.S.W.), and there
Joseph, with his lately-wedded wife, began the
Docker career. Joseph died in 1884, but the
widow survived till about a year ago, reaching the
long age of 95.
A whoop from Victoria :
The Melbourne police, being notoriously incapable of
capturing the right man “ on information received,” seem
bent on putting up records for prosecuting the wrong
man after their precious information has been proved
worthless. Last week a soft-headed farm laborer from up
country was standing on the Melbourne wharf with a view
to voyaging to Geelong (a sort of place which would
materially appeal to such a person) when a spieler came
along, borrowed £3 10s. and left him for ever. Somebody
advised the absurd person to go to the police, and presently
a detective took him in tow, whereupon poor Juggins
pointed to a respectable citizen as the man who had
borrowed his seventy shillings, and a prosecution resulted.
The prosecution, under the circumstances, was a police
outrage, for the detective admitted in court that the
accused man had given a perfectly satisfactory account of
himself, as a carpenter and contractor, and all his move-
ments from the time he left his home until he was taken
into custody. Police inquiries had practically proved an
alibi, in addition to showing that the accused was above
suspicion of being the man wanted, apart from the alibi.
But the fat-headed yokel from Euroa had given the police
a case, and they were not going to relinquish it because it
was obviously rotten. “ I’ve done nothing,” said the inof-
fensive victim in the old music-hall yarn. “ Neither have
I,” said the constable who casually grabbed him by
the collar, “ I’ve done nothing for six months, and I’m a
disgrace to the Force. So, come on.” Magistrate Panton
summed up the outrage in a few well-chosen snorts:
“ This case shows the misfortune a man may have to put
up with in Melbourne. If the police set about their work
in this way, any lunatic may come down from the country
and have anyone arrested.” Mr. Panton furthermore
remarked that, from the description of the spieler given
in the warrant, the police should be able to arrest him at
once. But thev wouldn’t be typical Melbourne police if
they often did the right thing. They’d be a disgrace to
O’Oallaghan’s Force.
THE LITTLE SHOWS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
They travel through the ways of all the earth,
In lands of snow and sun ;
They judge the world of men by what it’s worth
When all is said and done.
They rove throughout the land of silver sheen,
They brave the sleets and snows,
These remnants of the things that might have been
The Little Shows.
With beauteous face, and free and facile tongue,
And hopes that all-time burn,
’Twas from those ranks the brightest stars have sprung
(And to those ranks return !).
But they still dream, like boys, of fame-to-be—
The dream that comes and goes,
And furnishes With farce and tragedy
Our Little Shows.
No march of triumph rich with garlands fair,
No blazoned path for them ;
They take their rest in Nature’s open air,
Or in the tents of Shem.
Out in the ghostly glare of western lamps,
Or where the gaslight glows
In towns, or in the roaring mining camps,
The Little Shows.
They find the lonesome spots of all the earth,
Where day has long been dumb ;
They bring the furthest outpost songs of mirth,
And laughter to the slum.
So from the luckless day they make their start
Until their chapters close,
I’ll keep for them a corner in my heart,
The Little Shows.
N.S.W.

WHISKY
At a Melbourne municipal feast the other
day, Mr. John Madden, of the soaped moustache,
said he remembered the time when the council table
was a gum log and the councillors sat on the top
of a rail fence. John did not say that the mayor’s
banquet of that day was baked ’possum and burnt
damper, but it seems a probable event. Mayoral
banquets and trappings have improved since
then. Whether alderman have done so or not, ho
one knows, but many have doubts.
* * * *
Director Maiden of the Botanical Gardens,
Sydney, has drawn up for the information of
N.S.W. Parliament a weighty series of arguments
against the proposal to seize on the Macquarie-
street frontage of the Domain as a site for the new
Public Library. As he points out, that would
involve the destruction of the very crown of a
notable pleasaunce—the wooded knoll from which
a wonderful view stretches to the Heads. There
is just as good a library site at Cook Park. There
is not in all Sydney such a view spot as the
Macquarie-street entrance to the Domain.

Evidently the success of the Maoriland
footballers has “narked” some of the cockney
newspapers and won the condescending approval
of others. The Daily Mail sent a telegram to
King Dick, expressing its British amazement at
the brilliancy of the—haw ! —colonials’ playing,
and asking whether their victories against a
succession of dear old Ma Country teams hadn’t
made their compatriots feel awfully proud, don-
cherknow. Seddon having replied in a suitably
Imperial strain, a scornful other journal—the
Westminster Gazette— got its hair off. True,
the visitors had been wiping the grass with a
number of local football teams, but was this
“quite the sort of thing for a Premier to talk
about ? ” asked the W.G.
* * * *
Seddon unfortunately hasn’t a sense of
humor. Else when the Daily Mail sent that
leg-pulling request for his views on the brawn of
the Maoriland footballer, the frozen-mutton patriot
would have replied, “No astonishment this coun-
try victories Maoriland footballers over English.
Our men fed on best Christchurch lamb (to be
bought in London at M.L. Government depots),
which explains their prowess.”
Victoria has had something of an outbreak
of crime among its children. An 11-year-old boy
came before the Bendigo police court charged with
climbing into a store through the skylight in order
to steal cigarette cards from the packets on the
shelves. Next day another 11-year-old in Colling-
wood was convicted of snatching a woman’s purse.
1 inally, the same week, three youngsters—l 3, 11,
and 10 years—broke into the Baptist Sunday-
school in Portland, and rifled the missionary-box
of its pennies. The front names of four of these
criminals were an aggravation of the offence;
they were called, respectively, Cecil, Stanley,
Esmond, and Nelson. The fifth boy was named
Bill. The only deteriorating influence in Bill’s
life was the fact that he had won a first prize in
the Sunday-school he helped to loot.
* * « *
That chubby, elderly Cupid, Dr. Bevan,
of Collins-street Independent Church, has re-
turned from his English wanderings, and tells the
Argus that he discovered much dislike and
distrust of Australians in the Cold Country,
“ mainly,” he says, “due to unfortunate examples
of Australian character which the South African
war brought into prominence.” So, after all the
gush and enthusiasm about the contingents, the ser-
vice done to the Empire is recollected only because
of a few larrikins who broke up a bar or two in
Capetown, and a Bush veldt Carbineer who got
himself shot by mistake, or for doing what he
didn’t ought to —nobody quite knows which. Next
time Australia sends contingents abroad, it should
recruit them from the Y.M.C.A. and thus make
sure that they will behave up to the high moral
standard expected of Thomas Atkins.
* * * *
The Age tells of a Melbourne suburban
J.P. who delivered a stern ungrammatical address
to a youthful prisoner, and ordered 12 months’ im-
prisonment, whereupon prisoner retorted scorn-
fully, Gram, Whiskers, yer carn’t do it!”
“It’s done, sir, and entered,” said his Worship
severely. “ Look ’ere, d’yer know what yer
about ? What’s the charge ? ” “ Attempted
larceny. We must teach you fellows a lesson.”
“Well, yer carn’t give more’n three months
for attempted larceny.” Then his Worship fell
over things, consulting authorities, and it was a
squelched J.P. who hastily reduced the sentence
to a quarter of a year.

It is proposed to establish a military college
at Sydney University. A wise step certainly, if
m other directions a movement were being made
towards national defence. But the establishment
of steel works and factories for the production of
ammunition and large and small arms are the first
essentials, and until these are supplied the teach-
ing of military tactics at a college is mere frill.

Beware of the girl who carries a book under her arm !
The book is her bait, her pinch of salt, her toasted cheese,
to ensnare the foolish male. It’s reckoned to make her
look interesting and intellectual ; and there are hundreds
of girls who use this subtle weapon (mainly poem-hooks
and up-to-date novels). Many a gilded youth has gone
under to an unread Iveats or blue-covered Byron—even to
a ca,lf-bound Eric Macka.v. A variation is a bundle of
music, even though the owner can’t play the pianola or
Jew’s harp ; or a paint box, though she can’t draw beer.
Beware, therefore, young man, of the sesthetic maiden
with paint, music, or books ! Bernard Shaw observes
that the modern Don Juan is the female. She lurk after
the shv male with divers weapons. And when they meet
their fate, both generally deserve it. Young man, marry
the girl who is carrying home the groceries.

* * * *
A matter concerning two legacies was
mentioned last week in a Sydney police
court. The legatees were two sisters, and the
mention arose out of the fact that one was on her
way to Callan Park Asylum as the result of
drinking methylated spirits, and the other, for
the same reason, was bound for a Rescue Home.
Wealth came to them unexpectedly, and they
didn’t know what to do with it, so they “ blewed ”
it on the horrible fluid that smells like varnish,
and tastes like paint, and makes your breath blow
up if you snort at a lighted candle.

* * * *
Evidence given before a N.S.W. Select
Committee on bread alleges that bakers who cater
for the poorer classes prepare a special doughy,
semi-cooked loaf, as “poor folk like it doughy.
They generally have a lot of youngsters, and if
the bread is doughy and moist there is no need for
so much butter.” It’s very kind of the baker man
to so study the tastes of the poor, and it would be
cruel to hint that he has any motive other than
kindness, or to bear in memory the fact that
doughy, badly-cooked bread contains more than a
fair share of water, which is cheap, and
can be sold at 21b. for threepence with
great profit. But it’s rough on the public
health. Writer, who doesn’t belong to the
very poor, and who makes special efforts to get
well-cooked bread, finds that, as a rule, the loaf
prepared for well-to-do folk is far from well
cooked. Its material is mainly starch—modern
milling takes most else out of the flour—and, with
really thorough cooking, it would be fairly nourish-
ing. As cooked by nine bakers out of ten, it is
harmful, and to the dyspeptic actually poisonous,
bince that is so with “good bread,” what must be
the quality of the special doughy stuff prepared
for the stomachs of the poor ?
* * * *
Transpired in a Senate debate on the
Copyright Bill that the total cost last year of the
European cable service to the Press Trust was
£15,182. That is divided among 20 daily news-
papers published in the capital cities within the
Commonwealth, and an association which supplies
the Maoriland newspapers, and another association
which supplies the provincial newspapers. The
cost to any one daily must be therefore infini-
tesimal; yet the service is kept at its present
miserably inadequate standard on the score of its
“vast expense.”
* * * *
Language from Melbourne calculated to
cause a breach of the peace :
Curator Guilfoyle, of Melbourne Botanic Gardens,
cloesn t like the efforts of well-meaning*, but one-idead
people who strive strenuously, in piling up rocks and
jamming green-stuff in between, to help Melbourne to get
close to the mighty heart of Nature, and lay its ear
against her palpitating bosom, and all the rest of it. He
speaks of their efforts as obstructing* and disfiguring the
roads, as an artistic disg*race to Melbourne which ought
to be wiped out, and says that every individual should
study the best models and protest against what he con-
siders inartistic or futile. Let us protest. Perched on a
peak overhanging* the Monier-built bridge leading to the
Botanic Gardens stands a princely pepper-pot erection
known as the Temp le of the Winds, or HSolus, or words to
that effect. The painful pilgrim climbing the long'flight
of rough-hewn steps to the fane finds suddenly sprung* on
him that most futile of all artistic shams—a hollow
mockery of super-fatted concrete blatantly masquerading*
as what it is not; and round the inside cornice of the
abonunation, in deep-sunk, foot-long* lettering*, is the
unblushing statement that it was designed and built by
W. K. Guilfoyle to gratefully perpetuate somebody nobody
ever heard of, who dug trenches and relieved pot-bound
geraniums when the gardens were yet young and innocent
oi guile. Rock work decoration, far from being futile, is
nonest, therefore artistic ; the thing to be deplored is a
lamentable lack of variety, eye-wearying in its irritating
monotony. But this hypocritical, jelly-mould lime-house,
perkily perched on high, only fills the beholder with a
whole-souled loathing at the abominable concrete
effrontery of the thing as it stands, a horror and an
offence against the morning sun.
After which few remarks the correspondent went
away and put a cool, damp, green cabbage-leaf on
his aching head.
THE RONTGEN GIRL.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Her figure’s dainty chrysalis
Now wriggles in its sombre shell,
But mantles deep that come amiss
The summer sun will soon dispel.
Her wraps and rugs aside she’ll fling,
Her cerements of old winter furl,
So hold your breath, while sun-rays bring
The Rontgen Girl.
She is Australia’s own ; the fire
That flickers in her eyes and mouth,
The misty mode of her attire,
Are offspring of the sunlit south.
Her form is modelled to enslave,
From dainty toe to topmost curl,
And hlest be Fashion when it gave
The Rontgen Girl.
Her skirt will be of muslin then ;
Her blouse will be a filmy one ;
And both be far more filmy when
She stands before the setting sun.
She’ll set, enshrined in rose and gold,
Your heart aflame, your head awhirl,
When, Truth-clad, .you once more behold
The Rontgen Girl.
VIC.
A.B.
THE MORMINGTON MAID.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Mornington (Vic.) Council has negatived a motion that
all male bathers over 12 years of age should wear cos-
tumes, on the ground that visitors would be driven away
b.V restrictions not adopted by rival watering places. No
motion was made regarding the costumes to be worn by
female bathers. —News item.
VIC.
Morn ngton has not suggested
That the powers with Which it’s vested
Should apply as la-k of dress did
To each maid ;
So the Council’s left its waters
For its charming, shapely daughters
Free to wade.
We will give our firm adhesion
Henceforth to that spot Elysian,
Where the merry maenads Grecian
May appear
On the sunlit beaches, flitting
In a garb of tightly-fitting
Atmosphere.
When we view each sunburnt Venus,
Naught intruding in between us—
Nothing in the world can wean us
Back to town ;
There we’d stay throughout the summer
Watching every pink newcomer
Turning brown.
Mornington is our objective,
For its Nereids collective,
Individual, irrespective,
Claim our hearts,
When their costume only air is
(Kindly tell us what the fare is
To those parts).

It was sworn in a wife-desertion case in
Melbourne lately that the lady who had seduced
the defendant away from his appy ’ome prevented
him giving his wife anything by annexing all his
earnings—£s a week. She even “made him put
down in black and white an account of every
threepence he spent.” The magistrates were so
pleased at this evidence of thrift that they didn’t
order the man to support his wife, but merely de-
sired him to “see if he couldn’t do something for
her.” But it looks as if that “something” depended
mostly on the other lady.
re * * *
Melbourne Y.M.C.A. is making a special
effort to collect £7lOO in order that it may
buy out the building in Flinders-street, known as
Schuhkraft’s —now occupied by the Civil Service
Stores. The good young men had once a respect-
able place in Russell-street, which is now a sporting
club. They lost their heads in the boom days,
and built the huge pile now belonging to the Salv-
army, at the top of Bourke-street. They put up
the cash for the first storey, or thereabouts, and
trusted to the Lord to provide the wffierewithal to
pay for the upper floors, and when the burst was
over the new boom building was gone, and the old
previous building was gone also, and the good
young men were a shockingly bad example.
Since then they have hung out their holy shingle
precariously here and there, and have learned the
wisdom of strictly cash dealings.

The law-court struggle between Tom Tre-
wartha, miner, and the Confidence Extended Co.,
of Bendigo, pr-omises to be a prolonged affair.
Trewartha, defeated in his action for damages in-
curred through alleged negligence at the mine,
succeeded in his application for a new trial, on
the ground that two members of the jury.had been
seen in conversation with the manager and one of
the directors of defendant co. whilst the trial was
proceeding. Judge Chomley ordered the case to
be re-heard at Bendigo County Court, next month,
the costs of the trial to abide the result of the re-
hearing. Now it is said that the co. will appeal
to the Supreme Court against the Judge’s decision.
Poor old Tom Trewartha ! Soon he will begin to
wish that Jus, accident had proved fatal. Though
we are a long time dead, we can be almost as long
a time in the Supreme Court. Moreover, there
are no costs when we are dead.
A HERO OF THE PUSH.
LUCINDA: “Heard you give big Connor a hawful doin' at
the match lars Saturdee, Skipper.”
SKIPPER : “ Me oath! ’N’ I’d ’a’ give him worse if the

LUCINDA : “ What ’d they want ter stop yer fer?”
SKIPPER : “ Oh, they was busy on ’ini theirselves.”
The Law in Victoria begins to be weary of
certain ill-regulated persons who can’t make up
their minds. Under last year’s Justices Amend-
ing Act, when an accused person pleads guilty at
Petty Sessions and is committed for trial, the
depositions are taken then and the witnesses
allowed to disperse. But should the accused alter
his mind and his plea at the higher court, the trial
must necessarily be postponed in order to rope in
the missing witnesses once more. A shifty-minded
person just now before the Courts first pleaded
guilty, then, at Sessions, altered his plea, and his
trial had to be postponed. Subsequently he noti-
fied the Crown Solicitor that he had again altered
his mind and would plead guilty, but when he once
more faced the music this fickle individual s first
remark was, “Not guilty,” and as some of the
witnesses were by this time at vast distances the
discussion had to be adjourned again.
Two lots of inter-State Church dignitaries
have been talking about fire in Sydney. Ihe
Anglicans discussed the Athanasian Creed, which
holds out prospects of a hot grill for unbelievers;
and after pursuing the discussion along about ,wo
miles of type The Bulletin lost it in a stack of
theological straw, and doesn’t know for certain
where it ended. Most likely its m the stack
still. The meeting that Dill Macky s friend, Car-
dinal Moran, presided over also discussed fire,
but instead of wheeling along truckloads of theo-
logical straws it produced its bank book, and
remarked that the fire insurance co. s seemed to
be dipping deep into the collection plate, and
asked why the Church couldn’t have an insurance
fund of its own. In the end two bishops were
told off to draw up a working scheme, for submis-
sion to a meeting of bishops later on. A very
practical person is the bishop whose front name is
mostly Patrick or Jeremiah.

the hospitals. When it comes to cadging, the
Sal v’army is as full of points as a three-card

The casual, gradual State official ! A Tie.
officer was taking a Neglected Children’s Depart-
ment youngster to Geelohglately, when, afew miles
on the Melbourne side of Little River, the infant
leaped from the train —going at 30 miles an hour or
thereabouts—and was seen to be lying alongside
the line. The official didn't stop the train, or tell
a porterat Little .River to go back ; he just thought
the matter over carefully till he re iched Geelong,
whence a rescue party was dispatched. But the
boy’s mind worked faster than the officer s, and
bv the time the party arrived he was a very scarce
infant.

“N.” : The demise of a very
venerable capitalist, call him Gilt-
gore, leads to reflection on the mis-
chief of waiting for crabshells —
otherwise shoes. He emerged from
humble life, and his surroundings
were as modest as Uriah Heep s or
Pope Sarto’s. His only child, a
daughter, was the great heiress of
the suburb. Forty years ago a
draper’s assistant, call him Smith,
annexed her. The match ended his
luck. His fellow yardsticks envied
him even as Tomlinson was envied,
when he bounded from Swan and
Edgar’s, London, upon the stage,
as Walter Montgomery. Giltgore
put him into a shop. Yet v. » e the
Smiths sulky. Giltgore thought he
was magnificent, but they thought
him parsimonious. The business
did not prosper. Giltgore dribbled
assistance but not enough, and so
the shop was disposed of. Giltgore
was of the kind who say, “You 11
get it all when I’m gone.” Of
course it is on the tip of the heir s
tongue to reply, “ But when are
you going?” The Smiths were
blighted beings, and battlers.
Their daughters grew up under the
buzz of “Giltgore’s heiresses!”
Consequently they wanted hus-.
bands with twice as much money
as they could get. Poor but honest
fellows were thrust aside. The
women mildewed into discontented
old maids. Smith, as an old man,
said, “What good will it be to
me? I wish my wife never had
the prospect of a bean.” Then Gilt-
gore died, and lo ! some boom bust
had left him really poor, and his
latter years had been a pretence.
Wise Jim Lick, of San Francisco,
put his beans into a telescope.
His hungry heirs can look through
it, to get a view of his spirit in
heaven. He didn’t think to leave
a boring apparatus, so that they
could sink for his spirit In case they
didn’t see it through the telescope.
Melbourne is a remarkable place for steal-
ing things. They will steal your bicycle or your
good name while you wait. The mace was stolen.
Treasurer after Treasurer has purloined pension
funds and little things like that. The other night
a gang of enterprising thieves walked off with a
pile of steel tram rails belonging to the big tram-
way monopoly. The rails only weighed a ton and
a quarter, and were in nice handy lengths of 12ft.
each. Someone will steal Fink’s Buildings next.

Some faddists are trying to start a society
to guard against premature burial. In this coun-
try there is more need for a society to protest
against the undue postponement of burial. In
any Upper House may be found several men who
are dead, quite dead, but they don’t know it and
persist in remaining unburied.
