Mayor Cooke, of Unley (S.A.), lias solved
a difficult problem. Mayors have always found it
hard to discriminate between those who should
and those who should not be invited to social
functions paid for out of the Mayoral allowance.
Cooke has arranged a big continental evening at
the local recreation ground, and issued invitations
to all who exercised their civic and voted
at the municipal election last year, borne fami-
lies of specially high tone are left out because they
wouldn’t trouble to make a cross on a polling-
paper, while Polly Jones Bill Smith have
received their gilt-edged cards, and are not quite
sure whether to take them seriously or not, never
having met a sensible mayor before.

Maoriland Government, which is, on the
whole, about the most business-like and practical
in the world, proposes to take another step in
combating phthisis, by establishing sanatoria
where the convalescent or the threatened may
live, and follow some lucrative ooen-air occupa-
tion. In M.L. perhaps, in Australia certainly,
tuberculosis could be, with proper precautions,
made as rare as smallpox, and to make it so would
remove a very heavy tax on the productive capa-
city of the people.

After seven long years of Ministerial
muddling the matter of selecting a site for the
Mitchell Library buildings has been passed on to
N.S.W. Public Works Committee for inquiry.
Mr. D. S. Mitchell has already handed over to the
State 10,000 volumes—now housed in some inacces-
sible fashion in Macquarie-street—of the 30,000
Australian books, &c., which he prom'sed to
bequeath, the condition being that a suitable
building must be erected for their reception. The
building is a long way from haying arrived yet,
and unless Carruthers shifts things along when
the Public Works Committee has got through,
N.S.W. may easily miss this fine donation after
ali. Mr. Mitchell is over 70, and his will provides
that his collection must be properly housed within
one year of his decease, or else the gift lapses.
One scheme is the erection of a new National
Library, at a cost of about £114,000, in Sydney
Domain, about the spot where the landscape is
now encumbered by a mossy stone, dumped there
in bygone ages by some premature person as the
nucleus of new Parliamentary buildings.
A droll incident lately reported by the
cable is the capture of two British officahs by
Moorish brigands. If one could only see those
officahs protesting against the indignities they
suffer in the custody of the dusky desperadoes !
Bweakfast and dinnah shockingly served, and no
luncheon to speak of. No shaving utensils. No
changes of linen. No anything that makes life
worth living, bai Jove ! The situation must be
very like a scene in a Blandolt melodrama, where
Baker, in irreproachable regimentals, snorts
defiance at a host of mock Arabs and other dis-
guised supernumeraries, who have cut him off from
the British forces, and are feigning ignorance of
the fact that a brass band playing “Rule Britan-
nia” will shortly come to the hero’s rescue.

GOD HATES GLUTTONS!
A couple of Melbourne’s worst-known
citizenesses were driven through the city the
other night in separate cabs, one with her head on
the floor, the other with her feet through the
window. Next morning the Law fined them 40s.
apiece or thereabouts, and remarked wearily that
it was tired of seeing the ladies. They replied
they were equally tired, and had not the slightest
desire to intrude ; they each owned more than 50
convictions, and found the fine monotonous. LOll-
- the folly of this weary old “ 40s. or 14 days.'
They are both young women, yet in the last few
years they have cost the State at least £2OO in
gaol-maintenance alone, without any advantage
whatever, either to the State or themselves. A
law that could shut them up for a few years in an
inebriate farm-retreat on the Gfippsland moun-
tains, and supply them with profitable work,
healthy recreation and education, might do some-
with them, but there is no healing virtue m
“40s. or 14 days.” Any cockatoo could fay as
much.
* * * *
Christchurch, the last of Maoriland s chief
centres to adopt electric tramways, is building the
lines on the old steam-car gauge of 4ft. bin. At
present there are horse, steam and electric cars
running over the same rails. In wet weather
everything travels on the tram-lines; hansom
cabs fit the tram-lines; motor buses, motor cars
traction lorries and funerals fit the tram-lines ; and
sometimes a dog has a fit on the tram-lines.
... *
W. L. Bodine, Superintendent of Com-
pulsory Instruction at Chicago (U.S.A.), recently
produced some Government statistics, covering 20
years, to demonstrate that woman and child labor
is driving men away from the cities. “ Man, like
the Red Indian,”'he said, “is dying out. In
1890 3 914,571 women occupied paying positions
in the IT.S. ; in 1900 the number had increased to
5.329,807. The female birth-rate is increasing, and
the death-rate is diminishing, while, with reg<ird
to the male sex, the reverse is the case.”

Ballarat (Vic.) council is just now showing
how much grovel can be squeezed into an alder-
man without bursting him. When the Yorklet
was inflicted on this country he laid the foundation
stone of a Boer gore memorial in Sturt-street m the
alderman’s city ; but the site was wanted for the
electric trams, so the grovelling council wants to
shift the Burke and Wills memorial, and plant the
other in its place. Burke and W ills, having been
mere Australian explorers, can be pushed out into
some back yard; the best that’s going must be
reserved even for a butcher’s block provided a
blessed dukelet has touched it, or breathed on it.
And yet there isn’t a place in Australia that talks
more'about “the hardy pioneer” and puts on
more frill about “ the development of Australia
than Ballarat.
A THOUGHT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A little moon that steals across a pearl-grey sky,
A fire out in the West where the red days die,
Hedged with dark gums a twilit pool where pale lilies lie.
O little moon ! A new white leaf uncurled !
O dying sun ! A flag of glory furled !
0 silent lily pool ! A grave,
The story of a world.
QUEENSLAND.
A.G.J.


The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Half of Maoriland’s financial year was passed
on September 30, and it looks as though Seddon will be
able to make another good Budget speech. He expected
his revenue for the whole year to be £121,530 ahead of
last year’s ; and for the half year alone he is £104,000 to
the good. The railways are mainly responsible ; they
have brought in £63,000 more than in the like period of
1904—for which credit the policy of driving off the sheep
alongside the lines to make room for the men. Under
the circumstances, Maoriland’s public stocks ought to
stand far higher in the market. Its 3.1 per cents are
only valued at the same price (99) as those of Tasmania,
and J p.c. lower than those of S. Australia ; and its 3 per
cents are at 87, whereas Queensland’s are at 87|. Con-
sidering that Tasmania and S. Australia have been
steadily exporting adult population for years past, and until
lately Queensland wasn’t importing any to speak of, this is
a serious reflection on Seddon’s borrowing policy. Twelve
months ago Queensland’s 3 per cents were at 85f ; but
Queensland has gone in for honest finance, and mostly as
a result of its determination to pay the bill and not water
down Cohen’s security any further, its 3 per cents are
now up to 87J. But Seddon’s inexplicable kink in the
matter of borrowing—he has increased his annual borrow-
ings from half-a-million to three millions—has, in spite of
the State’s prosperity, brought down the M.L. quotation
from 87£ to 87. Cohen isn’t concerned with the Arbitra-
tion Act, or the Factories Act, or the Liquor Act, or
Seddon’s other social legislation ; but he is concerned
when he sees other Cohens coming in year after year to
share his security.
» * * *
The last wild-cat pension scheme that happened
along was in connection with the N.S.W. public service.
It was a scheme by which the oldsters of the service would
be supplied with pensions by the youngsters, but it
wouldn’t be possible for the youngsters to accumulate
any fund to pay their own pensions with ; yet it was rushed
through by the Public Service Association, and with all
its absurdities, has been in operation for about a .year
past. Now another wild scheme has been issued by the
Commonwealth Public Service Association of Queensland;
and a special 50,000 issue of The Federal Service (the
association’s “organ ”) has been sent out in order to rope
in the servants of all the States to support the nightmare.
The preface to the prospectus is a trifle hysterical, but
otherwise admirable. It mentions that several men who
were in the service last year are this year in benevolent
asylums, and it adds that if the service doesn’t do something-
in the way of immediate contributions to a pension or
superannuation fund, the asylums will before long hold a
good many more who are now tying up the Common-
wealth’s sealing wax with the Commonwealth’s red tape.
But, after proving- that some scheme is necessary, the
association comes hopelessly to grief in propounding a
specific scheme. The trouble is the usual one of offering
too much for too little.
The idea is that every officer shalj, contribute 4
per cent, of his salary ; the Commonwealth is to add 1 per
cent. If the Federal servants wait until they persuade
the Government to do that, a lot more of them will have
plenty of time to get into the benevolent asylums. The
public servants can agree to hand over 4 per cent, of their
own salaries; they might possibly induce the Govern-
ment to provide that nobody shall enter the
service without coming into the scheme. But
the other is outside their control ;-and if they incorporate
that in their scheme, they simply hang it up indefinitely.
The scheme provides that, with the contributors’ 4 per
cent, which it ought to get and the Commonwealth’s 1
per cent, which it can’t rely upon getting, every
officer shall be entitled to retire at 60, and if he has paid
in for 10 years, receive a pension of £4O for every £lOO he
has paid in. Thus a man of 35 receiving £2OO a year
would for 25 years pay in £8 a year, or £2OO in all, and he
would be entitled then, being 60, to a pension of £BO a
year. Now, if the fund earned 4 per cent, com-
pound interest without a break and never made a
bad debt, and didn’t cost anything whatever to
run, the improved value of the £8 a year contribu-
tions in 25 years would be £333 and a fraction. The scheme
offers a pension of £BO a year for that, whereas if a
man of 60 took his £333 to the A.M.P. Society.it would
pay him £3O 16s. 3.1. and no more. And even that is in
excess of the ordinary English rate. In England the
Guardian Co. would pay only £2B 17s. Bd., the Provident
Life £29 12s. 2d. The American offices offer a trifle more
—the Mutual Life of New York £3l ss. 3d. ; the Equit-
able £3l 6s. Id, The City Mutual (Sydney), whose
annuities are more liberal than those of any other
office in the world of which this paper has any know-
ledge, would pay £35 6s. A life' office not only finds
it can only afford to pay about £3O where this super-
annuation scheme proposes to pay £BO (or £64 without
the Commonwealth’s subsidy); but if the insurance-
society’s annuitant dies the very next year the annuity
ceases, whereas the Queensland Public Service scheme
proposes to pay the annuitant or his heirs five years’
annuity certain. This is adding folly to foolishness.'
Take another case. A man at 55 may come
into the proposed scheme by paying five years’ contribu-
tions with 4 p.c. compound interest added. Say he
is receiving £3OO a year. He would pay in not quite £65
back pay ; then for another five years £6O ; total, £125. He
would after that be entitled to £4B a year. Even if the
Commonwealth paid the 1 per cent, which the scheme
mentions, the fund would have £lBO with which to buy a
man of 60 a £4B pension for life. The best that any life-
assurance office in the world would offer, so far as The
Bulletin knows, is £l6 18s, 6d. This wild-cat scheme
proposes to pay almost three times that amount.
And it is impossible for the scheme to pull
through by means of forfeited premiums. If an officer
who has been paying in for less than 10 years retires for
some other reason than ill-health, or if he is kicked out,
he gets his premium back ; if he lias been in for 10 years
or more, he gets 2 per cent. Compound interest also and
in neither case will the interest retained more than cover
expenses. If a member under 60 dies after paying for 10
years or more, his widow receives half benefits during her
widowhood, and if she dies his children receive the same
benefits till the youngest is 18. This gun is loaded, too.
A member at 45 has paid in £l2O in 15 .years. Again
giving the fund credit for the Commonwealth’s 1 p.c.,
there will then (with interest additions) be just £2OO
available. If he has a child of five, that means payments
of £3l2 —and there is £2OO to pay' it with. Or the widow
of (say) 43 has an expectation of life of about 25 years—
which would, if she remained a widow, call for £s9o—all
to come out of that £2OO ! The A.M.P. would offer (with-
out regard to re-marriage) £ll 14s. 2d., instead of £24.
It isn’t necessary, perhaps, to bunt this crack-
skulled scheme any further. If it were, other absurdities
might be pointed out—for instance, the absurdity of pay-
ing the same pension to the man who has paid in £2OO in
10 years, and to the other man who has paid in £2OO in 40
years, regardless of the fact that the latter man’s £2OO
has been improved, by interest additions (at 4 p.c.), up
to £420, while the other’s has reached only £240. The
old men of the service under such a system benefit at the
expense of the younger, and by the time the younger men
are ready to draw their pension, there’ll be a hole where
the pension fund ought to be. A pension scheme is badly
needed in the public service—it is a good thing to have oil
the;premises in any service—but the 50,000 (or so) people
who have been appealed to in the interests of this one
shouldn’t have much difficulty in deciding what to do
with it.

The bank is not quite so absurdly restricted as it was at
the outset, but it is still seriously hampered. The S.
Australian bank, the most successful of the State banks
in the Commonwealth, might very well have been taken
as a model ; there the business is handed over to trustees,
who are given such wide authority that they may make
advances even on town and suburban lands ; and the ex-
periment has been completely successful. When the last
figures on the subject were issued that bank had re-
ceived back £287,334 of advances, and less than £ll,OOO
of instalments were overdue. It looks very much as
though, if the Queensland experiment fails, it will be be-
cause it has been hobbled. It is true that, being hobbled,
it cannot chase a man and insist that he takes a loan, as
the Q.N. Bank did in the drunk days ; but, being hobbled,
it cannot chase the man to whom a loan has been granted.
Thus the hobbled department is already stuck with an
abandoned homestead. It advanced money on the im-
provements ; the man left ; the bank hasn’t power to
carry out the conditions ; nobody else has taken up the
land ; so the bank can neither use the improvements nor
get paid for them. Yet, in spite of such handicaps, pro-
gress is being made

Profits have moved down a shade still further, and the
dividend has been put up. The distribution now absorbs
over 87 per cent, of the profits, leaving less than 13 per
cent, available for reserves. This isn’t nearly enough
in view of the fact that the earnings are still small enough
to suggest that a considerable amount of the comparatively
trifling reserves might very reasonably be written off.
The whole of shareholders’ funds—capital and reserves
combined—amount to less than 18 per cent, of the total
assets ; they amount to only 25 per cent, of even those
assets which are liable to severe fluctuations ; and that
isn’t a sufficient margin. The reserve ought to be built
up much more rapidly than the present very ill-advised
increase in dividend will allow. Some banks seem
to forget that they were wrecks only 10 or 11 vears
ago, and they continued in business, instead of
being wound up, on the tacit understanding that
they would apply a substantial part of future profits to-
wards clearing up the wreckage. It is extremely
doubtful if the wreckage has been completely
cleared up yet; nevertheless they distribute quite as
large a proportion of their profits as if there were
no skeleton in the cupboard. In fact, some of them
distribute more, on the ground, apparently, that share-
holders haven’t had much of a dividend for a few years
past. They forget that shareholders didn’t get much of a
dividend for the very good reason that they had lost a
great part of their capital. The National is no worse
than others and not as bad as some ; but this rushing up
of the dividend in the face of falling profits and of a com-
paratively trivial reserve fund, nearly half of it forfeited
capital, calls for severe remarks. Whether profits will «o
on falling or not depends mostly upon (1) whether the
present rates for deposits are maintained, and (2) whether
a greater demand for money can be brought about. As
this comparison shows, the bank has abundance of
resources to meet such a demand :

The increase in the interest-bearing deposits has been
greatly in excess of the increase in advances ; yet the re-
duction in deposit rates, coupled probably (though this
isn’t certain) with a smaller allowance for doubtful debts
enables-the bank to shdw better net profits than a year
ago. Even at this time of plentiful money, when other
banks have up to 50 per cent, of their debts to the public
represented by liquid assets, the Colonial has a reserve of
only 274 p.c. It looks none too large-; and yet, of course,
a bank that can s&fely work on a small margin (and
that depends upon the nature of its deposits and the sort
of depositors it has) has a great pull over other banks.
But the management which trusts its depositors and
works on less than a normal reserve takes a great respon-
sibility ; and the bank that does it ought to lay by cer-
tainly more than the average. The Colonial fills the bill
in the latter respect ; its dividend absorbs 60 percent,
of the profit, allowing 40 per cent, to go to reserves!
Further, the balance-sheet doesn’t appear to be over-
loaded with paper values, which call for writing
down ; thegross earnings (after paying interest) amounted
to £2 11s. 3d. per cent, on total figures, which is better
than the average. And on the basis of shareholders’
funds (capital and reserves) profits show up in this
manner :
Sept., 1901 .. £6 12 7 p.c. Mar., 1904 .. £7 15 0 p.C.
Mar., 1902 .. 7 6 6 „ Sept., 1904 .. 7 510 „
Sept., 1902 .. 610 10 „ Mar., 1905 .. 711 11 „
Mar., 1903 .. 614 9 „ Sept., 1905 .. 7 1 6 „
Sept., 1903.. 7 1 8 „
The Colonial appears to have a good borrowing connec-
tion, and is suffering less than most from the sluggishness
of enterprise. It is quite possible that this means that it
would show less improvement than most others if enter-
prise and industry were stimulated. Certainly it hasn’t
apparent the available resources to meet much extra
demand.!

The accumulation of money in Australia is one of the
most striking features in the Commonwealth’s history of
the past 12 months. The money is here, and there is no
outlet for it. No factories or industries worth mention-
ing are being established ; in fact, under the tariff which
6. H. Reid, Cook, and the Freetrade crowd in general
whittled down, some industries are drying up. Meantime
imports of foreign goods are huge. For years the gibe was
thrown at the Labor party that it was driving out capi-
tal. There is absolutely no doubt that the Reid-Cook
Freetrade crowd, and to some extent the fiscal-sinking
crowd, is driving out enterprise. Take the figures of this
bank :
Sept., ’O4. Sept., ’O5.
Notes in circulation .. £8,611 £B,OlB Dec. £593
Bills in circulation .. 39,360 52,290 Inc. 12,930
Deposits, current a/c.. 164,342 190,437 Inc. 26,095
Deposits at interest .. 576,083 699,167 Inc. 123,084
Liquid assets .. .. 261,957 499,367 Inc. 237,410
Advances .. .. 644,518 576,805 Dec. 67,713
Estate (Premises, &e.) 75,924 75,767 Dec. 157
Here we have an increase in deposits of £149,179, and a
decrease in advances of £67,713, so that, helped by other
movements, £237,410 for which the bank can find no use
in the ordinary way of banking has accumulated. Twelve
months ago the- bank held 33J per cent, of its liabilities
to the public in liquid assets (coin, bullion, public stocks,
and bank balances). Considering the large proportion of
fixed deposits, that was a perfectly safe percentage. But
now the holding is an abnormal 52J p.c. Included in the
liquid assets 12 months ago was £164,638 of coin and
money at bankers ; most of the latter is no doubt money
at short call in London, where comparatively high rates
prevail—2J per cent, for call-money in some cases. This
amount has now gone from £164,638 to £274,281; and the
increase is represented chiefly by money which has been
driven out of the country, and is now in London banks.
Under the circumstances this bank’s profits have been
remarkably well maintained—thanks, no doubt to some
extent, to the temporary keen demand for money in
England, so that the surplus assets are earning more than
they would do at most times. But that English demand
won’t last, and then, unless a fresh demand can be stimu-
lated in Australia, bank profits will look much less
attractive than they do now. As it is, they have only
been saved by cutting the deposit rates ; and even then
the Royal Bank of Australia, though its profit is still
excellent, shows less than it did the previous half-year.
If this movement continues, and the London demand for
money ceases, a further cut in deposit rates is more than
possible. It is more than the bank shareholder—it is the
depositor also—who must suffer from this importing-
disease, coupled with the land-locking disease. Profits
op this bank’s shareholders’ funds (capital, and reserves)
have moved :

This co.’s remarkable run of luck has stopped suddenly
and instead of showing one of the lowest, it will show one
of the highest, loss rates for the year. The business is
small, and one very moderate fire more or less makes most
of the difference between a good year and a bad. For

but going back as far as possible, these have been its

The capital is £37,500, so the reserves are getting well on

corner ; but so long as the big fire holds off it is earning-
fine profits for its shareholders. On capital and reserves
last year’s return, in spite of the co.’s hard knocks
reached £5 15s. p.c. The reserves, by the way, include
£2921 to cover unexpired risks—which is only a pro-
visional sort of reserve.

For the first time for many years the goodwill item is
shown separately ; it now stands at £18,844. As that is
purely a paper item, and ought therefore to be discarded,
the real deficit is now £24,762. Very little can be said in
favor of retaining a goodwill item in the case of a profit-
able business, and nothing at all can be said for it where
the business has shown a continuous loss for years past.
The amount of capital at present intact appears therefore
to be £15,238, or 7s. 6d. per £1 share. But this, again,
depends upon the writings-off for depreciation of plant
and leasehold. If they haven’t been sufficient (and the
amount has never been shown), then the capital really
intact is less than £15,238. The improvement on the
previous year’s record, though substantial, is in one
respect not as good as it appears to be from the table,
for in 1903-4 a disputed railway account for £520, covering
services rendered in previous years, was paid. In other
words, the loss in 1902 and 1903 was somewhat greater
than is shown above, and in 1904 somewhat less. Apart
from the fact that the loss wasn’t as heavy as in the
previous year, about the only satisfactory feature of the
accounts is the reduction of the sundry debtors item from
£5846 to £4065 ; but the overdraft stands at £3565, an
increase of £545, the outstanding money collected having
apparently been sunk in plant. And as the plant doesn’t
appear to be able to earn profits—well, the position isn’t
cheerful.

The Federal Building Society (Melb.) has
issued its first annual balance-sheet. The old society, a
vast concern which spread itself out over three States and
then put up its shutters in 1892, panned out less disas-
trously than might have been expected. When it col-
lapsed it owed the public :
Deposits and interest .. .. £330,347
Prepayments 362
Overdrafts, &c 66,169
Total £396,878
The capital was £200,000 and nominal reserves £26,662.
After being in the melting pot 12£ years it was reported
that £389,939 of debts had been admitted, that the whole
of these had been paid in full, without interest since the
melting-down process began, and that the assets that re-
mained might be worth £122,000 if they were carefully
nursed and realised. The new society has a capital of
£96,351, and its first year pans out in this way :

The society has entered into business briskly. Properties
in possession include shops and dwellings' £28,878, and
vacant land £10,581. Whatever profit may be got from
realising them, there seems to be little in letting them ;
the net rental return on the average value for the
year was only £1 15s. 6d. p.c. Still, repairs were fairly
heavy. It doesn’t, however, seem likely that the
profit on sales will be a regular thing; and, apart from
that, the profits show just under 4| p.c. on the written-
down capital alone. And that is low enough to suggest
that, in estimating the present value of shares, the
reserve fund which the new society took over may be
disregarded.
* * * *
Cobar (N.S.W.) is feverish over the rich silver-
lead development in the resurrected C.S.A., six miles
north ; and the stir isn’t surprising, for, so far as the
eastern crosscut has gone from the 450 ft. level, something
like 70ft. of lode has been driven through, which on
checked assays shows the remarkable average value of
£7 15s. per ton. Highest assays (taken from the last few
feet) show silver 1670 z., lead 56 per cent., and gold 13dwt.
Originally worked for copper, the upper levels of the
G.S.A. didn’t indicate anything definite ; there was a big-
formation, but no value in it. Then G. H. Blakemore
and others became interested in it, and the strength of
the syndicate they formed, backed up by State aid, gave
the show its first real chance in the sulphide zone. The
subscribed capital was £2200, which didn’t seem enough
at the time ; yet it has turned out all right, and each
shareholder who backed his pluck with a £5O-note
can now exchange it for a couple of thousands.
Of the subscribed capital the syndicate still has £lOOO ;
but flotation is already agreed upon, on the basis of
£44,000 nominal capital—22,ooo shares of £1 each to be
issued to present holders, with 22,000 preference contrib.
to the public at 2s. on application, 2s. allotment, and
with a calling power of Is. per month. Policy is to
thoroughly equip mine, sink new main shaft, and erect
smelters ; and as nevv float provides £4400 cash along with
the old £lOOO, and gives in calls £llOO per month, there
should be enough to open up. Experts like John Howell,
Brown, G. H. Blakemore, J. O. Armstrong, W. H. Tre-
wenack, and Low state that the lode in present crosscut
is almost bound to be very wide ; certainly there is no
indication yet of either wall. Of course, one can’t tell
positively ; but on the limited data available, the G.S.A.
seems likely to be one of the biggest things yet found in
the district.
North of the C.S. A., upwards of 1000 acres have
been pegged ; but, as country is covered with alluvial,
speculators are simply making a guess at it.. Nearest
leases (40 acres held by Tie.venack and 120 acres by
Doyle) take in country with the top chance ; but until
some development is done on these leases and the shoot
located, there isn’t anything to indicate the value of
ground further north.
At the south end of the C.S. A., Andrews Bros.’
claim, recently purchased by Frank Gardner on the basis
of £BOOO cash and one-sixth interest, looks like a good
deal ; and as it is now contended that the lode is a direct
continuation of Gt. Cobar, Gardner is likely to make good
use of this information or suggestion in his proposed float.
But the connection between the two is another guess.
* * * *
Great Peak Extended, Cobar (N.S.W.), pre-
viously referred to as a syndicate with speculative
chances, is now sufficiently satisfied with its silver-lead
lode development at 250 ft. level to launch out as the
Great Peak Silver Mining Co. (No Liability), in 5000
shares. Latest assays gave gold 13dwt., silver 360 z.,
lead 13 per cent., from a fair ore body. Previous to this
silver assays reached 2000 z., and lead 87 per cent., on
which showing Great Peak Extended may be reasonably
expected to open into a payable mine.
Premier (Cobar), comfortably situated between
the phenomenally rich and patchy Peak and Great Peak
Extended, also meditates reconstruction and enlarge-
ment. Present syndicate, whose boundary is within
30ft. of the well-known Blue lode, is now crosscutting ;
but operations are too slow, so syndicate is going to
see if aNo Liability Co. can’t facilitate the business. The
Peak, but a few feet away, is on 2oz. ore, and as the
strike is direct for the Premier’s property, a few moments’
thought on the Premier won’t be wasted.
* * * *
Boppy Mountain, which isn’t Mount Boppy,
but a huge conglomerate hill, with a likewise huge iron-
stone-gossan formation on its eastern slope, and-from
which cobalt, gold and silver assays were obtained in the
’9o’s, has, after years of neglect, been secured, partly by a
local syndicate, partly by an influential mining crowd.
The mountain lies between the State’s two top-notch
mines, Mt. Boppy and Great Cobar, and has an abandoned
shaft which was sunk in ’95 by Suttor, now N.S. Wales
Commercial Agent in the “East.” From this working
4dwt. of gold was got; so it is at least likely that Boppy
Mountain will pay for being thoroughly prospected. Any-
how, it invites that much consideration.

Wilfley Ore Concentrating Tables. Important
notice to mine managers and users of Concentrators.
The Supreme Court of Victoria has deckled in the action
brought against N. Guthridge Limited that the well-
known Wilfley patent is valid, and has been infringed by
N. Guthridge Limited by the manufacture and sale of
tables which they ca 1 the Card Concentrating Table.
The Court granted a certificate of the validity of the
Wilfley patent and ordered N. Guthridge Limited to pay
damages and costs of this action, and granted an
injunction restraining N. Guthridge Limited from in-
fringing the Wilfley patent. This is to give notice
that the Wilfley Ore Concentrator Syndicate Limited will
enfore its rights against all persons buying or selling or
otherwise dealing in or using such infringing Concentrat-
ing machines. By order of Edward J. Rigby, Attorney
for the Wilfley Ore Concentrator Syndicate Limited,
care of Austral Otis Engineering Co., South Melbourne.*

The Commercial World (London) July 15,
1904,. writes with regard to the Colonial Mutual Life
Assurance Society Li n eed: —“In responding to the
cordial vote of thanks passed to the staff at the meeting
alluded to, the General Manager stated that the stability
and strength of the society had been growing- year by
year and that at the end of the year 1903 it was in a
stronger positi on th an ever before. ”*
The Cathcart Proprietary Gold Mine, Ararat,
was the first mine in Victoria to adopt electricity as motive
power for pumping, hoisting and puddling. This plant
commenced work in September, 1904, and has been in
constant and successful operation ever since. It consists
of two return tube boilers, 160 horse-power engine, 110
kilowatt dynamo, switchboard, an electric hoist capable
of lifting 800 tons in 24 hours, an electric three-throw
pump raising 25,000 gallons of water per hour and a 35
horse power motor driving the puddlers. The dynamo
also provides electricity for 40 lights by means of which
the surface and underground workings are lighted.
Experience obtained on this mine during one year’s
service proves that: (1) electricity makes an actual saving
in fuel amounting to about £350 per annum ; (2) the
dynamo and motors cost practically nothing to maintain ;
(3) the cost of lubrication is reduced to a minimum ; (4)
electric motors re juire much less attention than
steam engines; and finally (5) electricity is much
more flexible than steam power, permitting ready exten-
sion or change as mining conditions frequently require.
The contractors for this installation were the Austral Otis
Engineering Company Limited, corner of Hanna and
Kavanagh-streets, South Melb >urne, Sole Agents for Ganz
and Company, Budapest, Hungary.* [1

The British Broken Hill Silver Mining Co.,
after careful consideration of all types of boilers, have
placed an order with Babcock and Wilcox for three large
boilers, each of 500 h.p. Superheaters, Chain grate
mechanical stokers and economiser have also been
ordered from the same firm. This now makes 40 boilers
of the Babcock and Wilcox type supplied to the various
mines and industries at Broken Hill. Wherever economy
i i fuel and maintenance of boiler has to be seriously con-
sidered by competent engineers, the Babcock boiler is
installed. Hence the recent unanimous decision of the
Expert Admiralty Committee to install Babcock boilers as
the standard for the British Navy.*

The strength, lightness, durability and low cost
of spiral rivited pipe have given it a place of superiority
above any other pipe. This pipe possesses enormous
bursting strength, and is protected, inside and out, with a
perfect coating- manufactured from asphalt. Asphalt
Coated steel pipes have lasted from 25 to 40 years without
any sign of decay, and may last as long ag-ain. Spiral
pipe is manufactured by Mephan Ferguson, at Wanganui,
New Zealand, and at his engineering and pipe works,
Melbourne, where the latest up-to-date machinery is also
used for making boilers, liuming-, refrigerators, bolts, nuts,
and every discrintion of cast or wrought iron work.*

This is what they call “ introducing capital to
develop our great mineral resources.” The prospectus of
the Diglum Dyke Gold-Mining Co. was lately issued in
Melbourne, 12,000 shares, 4000 of them to the vendors,
8000 to the public, at 6d. on application and 6d. on allot-
ment. Of the £4OO thus realised £lOO went to the vendors ;
probably another £lOO in flotation expenses. That would
leave £2OO on hand to develop a mine in North Queens-
land !
given by last month’s results —mining 12s. 6jd. per ton,
development 2s. 3Jd. per ton, milling 6s. IOJd. per ton.
Compare with the South—mining 10s. 2d. , milling 3s. 9Jd.,
development Bd. And the South is doing far more
thorough milling than the Junction North can ever do
with its incomplete, patchworkplant. It isn’t to be expected
that the Junction North, so far as one can judge, will ever
be able to work down to the South’s costs, which is so
much the worse for the Junction North. The estimate of
ore-reserves put forward (123,819 tons) is also worth very
little. The grade isn’t uniform, and how much can
be profitably treated can only be guessed at. This is the
position : The magnetic process failed. What the wet
method can do has never b ;en really tried, because the
mill is, according to mo lern ideas, an absurdity. Yet it
is known that it is a difficult ore to treat. It seems to be
a more refractory ore than Block 10’s, and Block 10, with
the very best and newest in the way of mills, is only
getting out 37 per cent, of the silver and 66 per cent, of
the lead. Therefore, until a remodelled mill gets to work
under fair running conditions on Junction North, the
size of the profitable reserves is unknown, and the value
of the mine a riddle.

The Zinc Corporation, the new concern which
has been buying up the Broken Hill z.nc dumps, was
registered in Melbourne on Wednesday of last week ;
capital £350,000, £340,000 issued, £l shares paid.
Same day there were buyers at 375. 6d. Very
pleasant for the promoting syndicate (Lionel Robin-
son, Clark and Co., of London ; Bewick, Moreing
and Co. ; H. .7. Daly ; and the Baillieus, of Melb.),
which took all the. shares ; but what do shareholders in
the Barrier co.’s which sold their zinc dumps feel about
it'? -For the syndicate doesn’t even propose to do a stroke
more than even the poorest of the co.’s could have done
for themselves—doesn’t propose to erect any expensive
works ; doesn’t, in fact, propose to do anything except
produce a very crude concentrate and sell it to Europe,
where the foreigner will have the work of turning it into
.metal, That export duty on crude and semi-crude ore
will have to come yet.

Exit H. O. Hoover, of 8., M. and Co., s from
Melbourne. ’ The next scene in. all probability will be
London, where the way for the return of this smart
engineer of the. West has been paved by Lionel Robinson
and Marks’Financial Noosn. Hoover and H. J. Daly are
now like Siamese twins. The latter scores a distinct
saccesi in getting the appointment of managvng-
director to the new Zinc Corporation Limited. In-
vesting public does not look with the eye of approval
on the advent of the Westrahan and old Melbourne
land-boom elements at Broken Hill. But they have come,
and, having plenty of boodle, have taken up wliat looks
like a g-amble on the metal market. Hoover in three
months has practically snapped up the Junction North
and the South Blocks; lie has got the Baillieu-Tyrell
North Broken Hill combination on Ins side ; and the
Block 14 Baillieu-Lionel Robinson shoal has swum
into his net. His spell extended to the South
Broken Hill, which made the remarkable deal in
tailings ; then he had a try with Block 10, but
it heeded not the voice "of the charmer. This was about
his only rebuff; and, as he is credited with mopping up

Bavay process, it. maybe.admitted'that he wasted no time
and did well. All he wants .nowhs a high price for spelter;
and presumably he would not turn up his nose at a boom
in Hill stocks.
New Hillgrove Prop. (N.S.W.) explosive rock
has “ kicked ” again, and, as in December last, the “kick”
has made a mess of the shaft between Nos. 5 and 6 levels.
Last time, the trouble was mainly around No. 7 level.
Present disturbance is serious enough to keep the mine
idle for six or seven weeks—except, of course, for shaft
repairing. This Ji Bull 00. has shameful luck ; it Had
just tasted divs. when this renewed trouble hit it. The
latest burst supports the theory that the Hillgrove slates
are built on the same principle as the g-lass drops
that the youngsters used to play with—drops that were
made by suddenly cooling molten glass, and would
explode when struck. In Baker’s Creek, Hillgrove Prop.’s
neighbor, rocks have been flying oil’ in this way for years,
but there the “ kick,” as the local miners called it, was
local; at the Prop, the business amounts to an artillery
discharge. Just how much force there is behind the dis-
charge, even when it is local, can be sized up from the
fact that, six or seven years ago, a small chip Hew off and
cut a Baker’s Creek.miner’s eye out. Molecular strain,
they call it,

Ivanhoe, Kalgoorlie, announces that its ore re-
serves, as at June 30, were 695,200 tons, worth H'B3dwt
This compares with 715,000 tons, worth 15dwt. on Dec. 31
last. The decline is insignificant, being less than 3 per
cent, in quantity and just over 1 per cent, in value. At
present rate of consumption ore reserves are good for 31
years. Many things may happen in that time. So far as
tested the lodes at the bottom levels continue as strong as
ever, and the slight decline in values is counterbalanced
by a decline in costs. Extra Wilfley slimes concentrators
are also being installed to increase percentage of extrac-
tion. It has been found, too, that grinding pans are
cheaper in this case than grit mills, about which much
nonsense has been written. Manager Nicholson does not
miss many points, as his management of the Ivanhoe
proved.

Associated, Kalgoorlie, had a very short run of
its treatment plant during September, and treated only
half the usual tonnage. The profit worked out at. £4900,
but, as £4997 was expended on alterations, co. came out
about square. Plant alterations are just about complete,
and it should be up to its full capacity of 9090 tons by end
of year. At end of last year co. had investments vaJiteif
at £160,000, chiefly British Consols, so the £39,000 sanc-
tioned for remodelling treatment plant need not be*
debited against current revenue. Under the circum-
stances, co; may pay a surprise div. before long, as it has
been earning steady profits since manager Roberts took
charge in March. The'worst pill was the discovery of the
error in survey, which made the 20ft. west lode appear to
be in Associated ground when it is really 10ft. inside of
Perseverance. ,

Directors of Great Fingall, Day Dawn (W.A.),
have at last said something about future divs. These are
to be 7s. per quarter, which means 20 per cent, per
annum on £7 per share. Future monthly output is to be
reduced to £53,500, equivalent to £3 per ton treated,
whereas ore reserves on June 30 were valued at £4 a ton.
If the 657,000 tons estimated to be in reserve on June 30
were worth £4 a ton, why. add 219,000 tons of mullock to
reduce the average to £3 a ton? Hr, M. and Co. have an
enormous reputation as m.ning engineers and managers,
but the cases of Fingall, Oroya, Laneefieid, Hellevue,
Burbank’s Birthday Gift, East Murchison United, and
others make one sceptical of their calculations as to re-
serves. As urged here again and again, gpld contents of
ore reserves should be only the recoverable gold. What
is the use of including metal that cannot be g'ot out, even
though assays prove it to be there?
Oroya-Brown Hill, Kalgoorlie, which had been
on the down grade, has had strokes of luck recently.
First, in the east crosscut at No. 5 level in Doolette shaft
on Oroya North block, a lode, or rather a pipe of
ore, has been cut 50ft. east, averaging sft. wide,
and 22dwt. for a distance of 130 ft. driven. Ore
in this drive has been very erratic, ranging from
ldwt. to 117dwt. This Doolette shaft is about 750 ft.
north-east of Pomeroy shaft, and the lode cut is a con-
tinuation of the lode worked in the oxidised zone in
1898-9 by Capt. Parkes, who g-ot from it some 50,000 tons
for 21,0000 z., out of which divs. of Is, were paid, or £9491,
On the original Brown Hill block, on which working
miners have long' been trying to secure a tribute, very
good values have been located. Some of these miners
indicated the spot where they intended sinking, and the
management took the tip and sank a shaft “ on its own.”
Years ago, when Feldmann was in charge, there was a
tremendous fall of earth in the upper levels, which
smothered thousands of*tons of pay ore. This could not
safely be worked from below, but is now being worked
from the surface, and prospects look g-ood enoug'h to
lengthen the life of the mine considerably.

Litigation re Hidden Secret, Kalgoorlie, is over

Westralia Mt. Morgans still works at a dead
loss. During September the 60-head mill ran less than
half time, and treated 5349 short tons for 245. lOd. per
ton. Tins means a loss of some £1490. The new 900 h.p.
engine is directly coupled to the mill, so receives the full
-jar from the stamps, which are the heaviest in the world.
iFrepaent breakdowns result, and mill has recently only
been running ji.i.f time. Until belt or rope driving is in-
troduced, as well as a systematic style of development,
mine will continue to be worked at a loss ; and recon-
struction is inevitable.
Golden Pole (YV.A.) is still too deep a mystery
for the ordinary man to grapple with, and for his peace
of mind the outsider had better stay where be is. .Spite
of board announcements, the mill was shut down half of
September for overhaul and repairs, and only 1800 tons were
treated, worth 87s. 9d. a ton ; profit, £5OOO. Only a small
quantity of slimes and sands was cyanided, as the plant
required some adjustments. Full run i.s expected this
month ; interesting- to see how it pans out. The con-
tinued decline in grade of ore treated is, meantime,
ominous. Manager Nat White says he has been treating-
more oxidised ore. This seems a lame excuse, for the
oxidised ore used to return 2oz. to the ton exclusive of
residues. Lode in the north drive .at No. 4 level is sft.
wide, but this includes formation in the centre of the
reef, which is split.

Boulder No. 1 ( W.A.), which has been worked
by tributors for years, has had a stroke of luck. In a
winze below the 166 ft. level a pay shoot 4ft. wide, worth
15dwt., was found. In following this down, the lode
widened to sft., and values went to 45dwt. per ton. The
shoot is, however, only 69ft. long at 133 ft. level, and it re-
mains to be seen if it lengthens at depth. The tributors
have been sinking but sft. weekly, so it will he some time
before anything much is known of the find. Shares have
jumped ; but investors are reminded that' there is a 3s.
liability on the scrip, and that there are 225,090 shares.
“Bob ” Anderson retired from the chair of the
Stock Exchange of Melbourne after 12 months unceasing
toil. He took office wiieii disintegration was imminent
because of the row between the Exchange and the Stock
Exchange Co. over the relative position of the two con-
cerns and their mutual financial obligations. The new
blood in “the room ” wanted to shake off the boom incu-
bus which the old blood had let the co. pile up. But to
do that meant repudiation. It needed a strong
man to save the situation. They got one in “Bob”
Anderson. He had been an official assignee, had run
an <erated water establishment, had been a horse-breaker,
and last and least had been a broker; and he brought
much experience of the world, besides business knowledge,
and fought until he got the tangle out. Result is that
the co. and “ the room ” have a sensible working agree-
ment which ensures peace for many a day. If Anderson
did this, old Joseph Thompson’s tact as chairman of the
co. helped to the end. In the chair Anderson was a
stickler for rule and form. No onedoubted his honesty, no
one questioned his wish to do right. Still, somehow,
he often missed the mark, though everyone wanted him
to score bull’s-eyes. A. J. Noall, his successor, is a son of
old William Noall, the doyen of the “house.” If blood
tells, Noall, jun., ought to make a good chairman.
Like his predecessor, he is a bit of a Hotspur.
Long Tunnel Extended, Walhalla( Vic.), is again
talking divs., and consequently there are more.smiles on

are wrinkles on the face of a pug dog. As things have

ground. How they will scowl now they learn the scant
underlie towards the Tunnel !
**. , * *
John Cock has a ticklish job ahead of him to
locate the old Dona Mundi workings, which the eastern
face of the Chiltern Valley mine, Chiltern (Vic.), is
approaching. His aim is to save a rush of water which
might endanger that end of the mine. It is a coincidence
that the two biggest alluvial shows in Victoria—
the Berry Extended and the Ciiiltern Valley—are
on the grope for old workings. In each ease
. the miners don’t feel too happy. What circumstance
can better show the necessity of the Mines Department
insisting that all mines shall keep plans as a measure of
safety? Cock’s mine does not prospect so well in the
west end as up the lead to the ea*t. Owing to the big
pressure of water on the south side of the lead in the west
end, the ground mined has been fairly wet, and costly to
. work. Improvement cannot be looked for till the branch
level gets away under the, wet ground south ; but as there
. is fully 1000 ft. between No. 24 cross-drive and the present
.block line, Cock ought to be able to get the south ground
.drained in tinie to prevent any check to his work. The
wash at the old Wallace-Chiltern shaft is not opening up
.well. Cock seems to think he is not across the lead,
so that he may yet reach a run of gold. Co. is financially
•strong, it has £5OOO in reserve, and it is unquestionably
well-managed.
*■ * * *
> No one quite knows how it is that North of
.England, Maldon (Vic.), scrip stands so high in the
■market. Taken all round, there hasn’t been a publicly-
owned mine at Maldon for the past two years outside of
Eaglehawk Co.’s that has not duffered out when put to
the battery test. North of England has, so it is said, an
80ft. shoot at 160 ft. level. Lately some specimen dirt
was met, and so the public is notified that prospects have
been greatly improved. The cruel deduction might be
made that the other quartz needed a sweetener. How-
ever, a little Stock Exchange “buck” was attempted,
but the Mt. Tarrangower fiasco lives too strong in the
memory for the public to bite on a mere bagful of
specimen stone. The co. has its battery, and when that
talks the public may come in. It should await more than
one clean-up, too, and the proving of more than an 80ft.
shoot.

Mills United, Ch. Towers, for a fortnight sent
up 926 tons for 2700 z. ; concentrates, £1065 ; residues
£2ll. Total equals £2 7s. 6d. per ton. Management de-
serves credit, as the mine is being developed beyond the
ordinary Towers - level. Stone is mostly broken in course
of development work, consequently it is none too clean.
Sloping has not commenced in earnest yet, although the
output is increasing. The half-yearly meeting is ap-
proaching-, when the cost of crushing is to be considered.
At' present co. pays 11s. Bd. for carting and crushing.
Prospects of th.e mine, together with the bodies of low-
g-rado stone available, warrant the purchase of a battery.
With a treatment plant of its-own, handy to the mine,
Mills’ could put up local records for cheap mining. Every-
thing is in its favor ; large blocks of.ground are ready for
stopingyand others are being opened up. There will be
little or no handling of stone once it is broken, as the
grade of the ground is steeper than anything else on the
field.

fied that their system of treatment and extraction is
good ? Are they' satisfied that their method of market-
ing the product is sat.sfactory ? If they are, then
they' are easily' pleased. The ore carries some gold,
and the mine is also supposed to be worked with much
secrecy. An interested visitor was on that account, not
so long ago, refused permission to have a look at the
workings. Without, much hesitation, -that interested
visitor proceeded to pour out a cart-load of detailed
information concerning the mine workings and prospects.
Instead of engaging that man as an official, the co.
“ shooed ” him out of the place. “ Cheek ! How does he
know?” He knew because it was his business to know.
It is no use having secrets if you put them in a box that
leaks.

* * * *
Robert Schloesser has applied for a 20-acres lease
of Mount Hogan, Etheridge district (N.Q.). Mt. Hogan
holds the old General Gordon line of reef, which in its day
was responsible for many good yields. It has not been
worked for some years. Schloesser is a mining engineer
employed by Max Gorier, of Melbourne. Gorier in turn
•represents Brandeis, Goldschmidt and Co., of London.
It may be that that crowd sniffs the Etheridge railway
coming. Bewick-Moreing thought they sniffed it 18
months ago, and hustled round after leases and options,
and dropped them smartly when they found the Queens-
land Government was taking- quite an intelligent interest
in the railway deal.

to pit it, as the co. knew the clay bar had to be
dred ,ed. Grayson’s estimate for this work was three
weeiis. That it took twice as long with a new dredge
ne;ds a lot of explanation. Ryan left 10 days before the

t ie gr und was all pug and no tin. So, actually, to be fair,
ta<e work as starting from about the time of Ryan’s de-
parture, as Grayson s wires soon after that indicated that
be wa i getting into the wash. On the 17tli Grayson tele-
graphed “ Started dredging wash afternoon shift yester-
day afternoon ; wash prospects well.” Apparently he
must have been stripping before that. If so, that ought
to be counted in. If be was only then out of the clay he

As matters stand, what Grayson will probably claim is—

he has proved the existence of deep ground ahead of his
barge, thus substantial ng his boring ; (3) that he has been
able to savejdn ; and (4) that the dredge will work.

Nym : I was bitten once by a snake,
t felt a prick on my right hand, below the little
finger—just sufficient to cause me to raise the
member to my mouth to suck it—mechanically.
As I saw the snake gliding away I grabbed my
fishing rod and despatched him. Then I tied a
ligature (of fishing line) round my wrist—painfully
tight. The satisfaction of having killed the
wriggler was full within me—but it slowly gave
way to a sensatiou on the spot like the sting of a
nettle. Bending my arm to suck the spot”again
I thought my wrist would burst—the blood flow
had stopped ! I cut out the two purple spots (like
twin flea-bites) visible amidst the redness, and
sucked, as it seemed, my fist dry. Whether it
was the^ taste of blood or not (it was salty) I became
sick. This made me move towards the township,
but I met a mate on the road who saw something
was wrong. He quickened my steps. My boots
seemed filled with lead. I hardly knew I was
walking. The power of feeling was gone, and the
helpless sensation began to creep upwards. My
heart seemed to miss a beat or two, my ears bulged
while interminable cricket chirps filled my head
Then the tightening of my chest strings became
unbearable. I thought my lungs were paralysed
I remember being hurried into a pub—the uncork-
mg of a three Star bottle—the hum of human
voices saying “What’s up?”—and I knew no
more ! When I woke five hours later the pub
was m darkness. I rose with a mighty effort and
groped my way into the night. How the stars
twinkled ! The cool air brought me to my sober
senses though my head still throbbed.
“ Janor,” on “ panning off” :
In cleaning' gold per dish, the ancient dodge men-
tioned by “ The Prooshan ” (B. 28/9/’O5) of “ spitting in
the eye of the gold” is good enough when handling coarse
metal, but with fine gold it is the surest means of getting
rid of the profits, and is never used by good and experi-
enced panners. This can be easily proved by panning
into a second dish and re-washing the residue. The
better way is to get the metal together in the belly of the
dish, with just enough water to cover the bottom, tilt the
pan slightly towards you, and gently sluice sand over the
bottom until it is collected in the side opposite the gold,
then imprison the lucre with a wet forefinger while wash-
ing out the sand.
Every miner loses more or less gold in dishing, for the
• IS "i is not an ideal gold-saver. The loss occurs generally
in the first part of the washing operation, not in getting
rid of the final spoonful of sand, for the less dirt in the
dish the less risk of loss. Insufficient puddling loses gold
per ‘ clay-balls ” ; unskilful raking out of the heavier
stones, likewise ; while thick, sludgy water, which the
fossicker has so often to use in a droughty land, is another
gold sneak. But with easily-puddled wash ( i.e., free from
clay), clean water and gold not too fine, the loss with the
bulk of alluvial men is trifling.
As in all other callings, there are miners whom experi-
ence does,not teach, and they never become good panners.
I knew an old hand at the game, who was the prospector
of an alluvial field in N.S.W. His claim was rich, shallow
ground, yielding up to seven and eight pound's worth of
gold per week, and he dished all his gold in the gilgis
which abounded in the neighboring scrub. He lost so
much gold that a tired individual who put in his time
shadowing him from water to water made up to 2oz. per
week washing his pannings. Also, I once saw a bloque,
on joking bent, slip an imitation 2dwt. speck into a dish
of wash about to be agitated by an experienced dish-
puncher. The joke did not arrive, for the man with the
dish lost the chunk of brass !
The gold dish-as a washdirt worrier has dropped out of
use of recent years. What dirt remains is poor, and
trends to the pension of old age ; so quantity is the only
hope, and it rattles through puddlers, troughs, cradles
and sluices, and the dish tries an odd prospect and cleans
up.
Most difficult metal to handle in the dish is mercurv.
It requires different manipulation to washdirt. At an
ordinary bush battery clean-up, where the dish is largely
used, an alluvial panner is quite up a wattle. Should
this strike the optic of any gold-seeker who reckons he is
just “it” with the dish (there’s whips of ’em around), let
him take a small bead of quicksilver, drop it into a dish
full of black sand, and pan off. If he recovers the bead
his opinion of his dish work is built on a rock, and he is
without any flies whatever as a pan-whirler.

have to “ pack ” their goods owing to the badness

from Murrurundi, who was

dered into the tap-room, and

* * ♦ 4
Car&doc uses some vigorous language;
Re the trancontinental railway line from Oodnadatta
to Pine Creek. Apart from the vast importance of the
project from a defence point of view, and the opening un
of excellent pastoral country from Mac Donnell Ranges
northwards, the moral effect will be considerable • it will
shift the horde of horse-thieves and cattle-duffers which
now infests Central Australia. The most notorious brand-
fakirs in the Commonwealth are to be found there. Ever
on the alert for “ clean-skins,” or figuring out how a
brand can be faked or successfully blotched, they live a
debauched existence, which must, in its “ inner own ” be
a veritable hell. So bad has the duffing of cattle become
that Bond Springs station offered a reward of £lOO for
conviction of the culprits. But who dare speak?
I recently trekked north as far as the Mac Donnell
Ranges and back. Among other things I saw bullocks
with lumpy jaw m the slaughter yard. The towns-
people, and the few other unlucky wretches who have to
go there, have this filth dealt out to them, or perhaps
they eat goat, which does not always carry the brand or
earmark of the man who kills it. A few weeks ago an
old resident of one town denounced the “lumpy law”
business, whereupon it was proposed to hunt him out of
the place as a public nuisance. Police? Yes thev
are supposed to supervise the slaughtering- but
residence in those surroundings would make nmst men
callous.
Let them get their railway. It will wipe out many
horrible conditions which now exist, and clear the air in
that country of pastoral and more or less mineral wealth.

“ Wilbea” : A correspondent (B. 20/7./’O5)
mentions a curious New Guinea method of catch-
ing fish. The fish kite used
at Dobu (8.N.G.), however,
is probably unique. It con-
sists of several leaves of a
tree ( Morinda Citrifolia),
each about a foot long and
three or four inches broad,
sewn together and supported
by a framework of the ribs of
a palm leaf. To this are
attached two strings, one of
which the fisherman holds
and regulates the kite by.
The other is long enough to
reach the water, and may be
from 100 to 300 yards. To
the lower end is fastened a
tassel of dense cobweb. The
fisherman goes out in his
canoe, and flies the kite so as
to keep the tassel bobbing on
the surface of the water.
The teeth of the fish are en-
tangled in the web, and the
capture is then made by means of a small hand-
net.

“Jay”: I once saw a-wallaby project
himself into a log-fence with disastrous results. It
happened in rather a curious Way. I was shooting
in the ranges round Bungendore and found myself
on a ridge with a crest of big loose boulders. Set
one of these rolling. It bounded off down the
gully faster than a land agent returning from
furrin parts, and made more noise. Half-way
down it broke in two, one half landing on the spot
vacated a small fraction of a second earlier by said
wallaby, who was getting out of the way of the
other half as fast as he knew how. He evidently
lost his head at finding himself surrounded by
flying boulders. Anyhow he hit the top log of the
fence when he came to it, with a whack like a
great Amen. Went down to investigate and found
him dead.
WELLY NT.
Judge Molesworth decided to-day another appeal by a
Chinese against a conviction under the Immigration Be-
striction Act in a court of petty sessions. It was shown
in evidence that appellant had been asked to write the
following by way of “ educational test ” : “ Much interest
has been excited by an article on the relations of-radium,
the sensational charai. of which is only partly concealed
by the, abstruse and technical terms in which the writer
clothes his meaning. The discovery of this new form of
activity of matter is regarded as a most momentous one.”
Judge Molesworth found in favor of appellant, whose occu-
pation was described as that of a gardener, on a technical
point, and quashed the conviction. —S.M.H.
“ Did you pass the educational test?”
“ No savee.”
“ All right. Pass in.”
1 ‘ Thurla ” : I don’t wish to pose as an art
critic, but Lionel Lindsay’s picture (B. 21/9/’O5),
“ Easily Annoyed,” seems to call for some remark.
It is rather unusual, and decidedly dangerous, to
erect a windlass on the bare earth ; one requires
to log up a bit for tipping purposes. Also, if
the man at the handle end intends to wind from
the side he is standing on, he having the rope on
wrong side must wind underhand, which is very
unusual and awkward. It is better to shift the rope.
THE SQUIRE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
With a frequency distressing’ to all who desire to see
Australia kept free from the humble bow and scrape of
England s Hodge, paragraphs appear in the Argus set-
ting forth that Mr. Very-Common-Smith, or some other
hyphenated landlord, has returned from spending in
Europe the money earned for him by his Victorian tenants,
and apubhc welcome was accorded him. If we’re going to
f tait hat-touching and bob-curtseying in Australia, what
in the name of common sense did our fathers come out
here for?—
-Che Western District squire away to distant Europe went,
Meanwhile his tenants grafted hard to earn his heavy
rent;
And when at length the strange far lands on him began to
pall,
The hyphenated squire returned to Hyphenated Hall.
alien all his faithful serfs stood round and cheered in
ecstasy ;
The State school children all marched out to sing their
songs of glee.
ft made him think himself at ’Ome, where yokels touch
their caps,
To see the glad servility of all those humble chaps.
And then as Mulga Bill came up, the cheers rang out
again,
Bill took his pipe from out his mouth and spat with deep
disdain.
But as the carriage passed him by he raised a large brown
hand ;
The coachman pulled the horses up as if Bill had
command.
The squire beamed from his carriage with a blandness
good to see—
He was not wont, metiiinks, to often act so graciously.
He thought, perhaps, Bill wished some slight petition to
present,
As humble men named Bill would do down in the Vale of
Kent.
But with a graceful nonchalance that raised the land-
lord’s ire,
Bill took from out his clothes a match and struck it on
the tyre ;
Then, with the graciousness of one who does a kinglv
deed,
He waved his hand, as in command, and proudly said
“Purceedl"

“ Panto” : A Chinaman with a bad tooth-
ache came into the telegraph office where I was.
Our funny man cured him. He disconnected the
main battery and gave John a loose end of the
connection to put in his tooth —then put the other
side of the connection in John’s hand, wet.
Result, a yell of “ Whaffor,” a frantically cavort-
ing Chow, seven smashed battery jars and a con-
sequent interruption to the line—officially un-
accounted for after three months’ correspondence.
The theory advanced was, “Must have been
smashed by expansion owing to the extreme heat.”
Thus does the climate of a healthy State get
Stinking Fish thrown at it to cover the misdeeds
a Chinaman.

“C.X.G.”: “Credo” (B. 5/10/’O5) says
there are no Afghan hawkers. If he came up
this way (Northern S.A.) he would find enough
and to spare. We also have the Hindu and the
Assyrian variety, but they are about played out;
the Afghan is in full bloom. He combines hawk-
ing with carrying. When loading is scarce he
buys a ton or two of flour and some potatoes and
onions, and starts for Queensland, selling as he
goes. He can cut the storekeeper’s throat at this
class of business, for where the latter has to pay
£lO a ton, say, for cartage, the Afghan will cut
the carrying price for himself, and do business all
along the line. Then, if there is any wool to
bring down, he will turn carrier again. Even
when fully loaded as carriers, most of them cart
a small bundle of drapery and fancy rubbish, and
sell as they go—mostly without a license.

sickly effluvia
which temporarily
paralyses anyone
within 20yd. To
see a worm like
this would be
worth a long jour-
ney.
* *
“ Yigilans ”
alleges that S.
Australia is worse
than Westralia in
regard to one form of nigger treatment -
Off the northern shores of North Australia is a place
called Malay Bay. Six or seven prows visit it yearly from
Macassar, and remain for six months at a time getting
trepang. Each boat has from 40 to 50 Malays. When
they go away theyjeave us a legacy of leprosy, or some-
thing else nearly as bad. They are allowed on shore, and
most of their time are camped with the blackfellows, with
whom they barter their shoddy. Gins go off to the prows
at night in dug-outs ; there is no one to interfere. The
Westralian Government has taken steps to stop the lubras
going off to the pearler’s luggers ; also the Malay from
landing. Here, in S. Australia, gins go off to the luggers
in Port Darwin waters, and some horrible cases have been
known of opium-drugging and maltreatment. Malays
land right under the town of Palmerston and give opium
and square gin to the blacks and their lubras. There are
huts in Palmerston where they meet and have wild orgies
unmolested. An unmentionable disease is so prevalent
amongst the blacks that one shudders to think of it.
Chinese in the North-
ern Territory are not al-
lowed to take up mining
leases, but they are allowed
to become tributors. One
white man has 30 leases
(mostly of 80 acres), which
he has let to Chinese, with
out a white man on the
lot. Some have as many
as 10 tributors. The white
man gets 10 per cent, of
the tin won. Drastic action
should be taken at once
in this matter. Solomon
and Mitchell, the Territory
Ms.P., are perfectly aware
of the facts, and should see
that a new Mining Bill
dealing with this matter is
introduced and pushed
through the S.A. Parlia-
ment. There is plenty of
metal in the Territory, and
if there were no Chows
there, it would be a good
place for a working miner.
Now, it is only good for
sharks and boodlers, who
live by sweating. At Wandi
(N.T.), a mere handful of
white men have 300 Chinese
working leases. This place
alone would give work to
at least 100 whites. Pine,
Yam, and Brock’s Creeks are the same; where
you see one white man you see 100 Asiatics work-
ing leases for him.
* * * *
“Deeby” : I lately passed a Government
tank on the C< ndobolin-Nymagee (N.S.W.) road.
It is let to the present “ caretaker ” at a few pounds
a year. There are hundredsof acres of good fenced-
in land included with the tank, so the “caretaker ”
runs a dairy and pig farm. The stock has access
to the tank itself, therefore you can imagine what
the water is like. Everywhere the fences are
falling down for want of a little attention, while
the expensive front gate is dropping to pieces for
the lack of a bolt. There are many other Govern-
ment tanks in as bad a condition. And these are
some of the reproductive works in which have
been swamped hundreds of thousands of our hard-
borrowed money.
ADVERTISING AUSTRALIA ABROAD.
THE FAT PERSON (to J. Bull family): “Ladies and gentlemen, I have
pleasure in introducing to you a full-grown male specimen of the true
Australian. Pray keep your seats; he is securely chained. Observe
the animal closely, and you will not be surprised to hear that his
manners are bad, and his morals are awful. I exhibit him in the hope
of encouraging British emigrants of the better class to flock to Australia.”
“Freewheel,” from the native part of
Maoriland :
“ Hello,” I said to my friend, “ where is the racing
track?” “That’s it in front of you.” “What! That
ditch ! Why, it looks like a tunnel with the top off ! ”
On closer inspection I found it had been excavated, thus
leaving the track with a steep bank about two feet high
on each side, from which hung dry cocksfoot grass. One
rider, % hen complaining bitterly of the pain caused by
the dry ends of the grass hitting his shins, said, “ Why, I
cool'd have won, only I thought someone was throwing
stones at me,” The bell to summon riders to their marks
was not ai. wered promptly, as the competitors refused
to start until all dogs were secured. This step was quite
justified, as there were four times more dogs than Maoris.
If a rider were to cast his eye heavenwards, when riding
round the track, it was to gaze into the face of a Maori
dog, who, with protruding snout, stood on the bank
scrutinising things with a devouring look. When all dogs
had been fastened up the ground presented an amazing
appearance. Here stood a Maori woman with three dogs
tied to her waist with flax, her waist measuring about
three laps to the mile. Further on, a dusky child had a
huge mongrel tied to a perambulator, in which peacefully
reposed several copper-colored children. The starter
stood majestically on the bank, with a look which plainly
said, “ I shall do my duty or die.” Beside him were the
three dogs which were using the old lady for an anchor.
When the whistle sounded “ All ready,” the starter raised
a cumbersome weapon in mid-air and fired. Never was a
louder report in Maoriland since Tarawera blew up.
With one accord the three dogs leapt skyward, taking the
anchor with them, and fell through what should have
been the top of the tunnel on to the track. Several riders
immediately charged into the seething mass of dogs, flax,
and woman, and to add to the commotion, a pram was
seen advancing at a furious rate, with a dog attached,
straight for the scene of action. One infant fell out safely
on to the grass, but the others were hurried remorselessly
into the fray. The starter was disarmed when just on the
point of firing on the combatants. The heat was eventually
started again, but owing to the absence of several officials,
who were discussing dog-fights in the refreshment room,
the competitors were allowed to ride several miles further
than they should have done. In fact, they intended to
ride until darkness intervened had not someone shouted
out for them to sprint. All concerned, except the placed
men, wanted it run again, as the officials had unjustly
awarded the places to the first three men at the end of six
miles, whereas the race was a 2-mile one. For two solid
hours argument ensued. The placed men refused to start
if the race was run over again, and threatened to sue the
promoters for £57,000 damages. The officials brought the
local doctor to argue with the competitors, but he only
made matters worse. Then they tried the local lawyer,
but his decision was upset by a well-known racing cyclist.
Then everybody gave his advice gratis. Eventually the
entrance fees were refunded and the race declared off.
Two dogs were injured.

A S.A. teacher asked a pupil the use of
rivers and was answered by a lad with, alas, much
experience : “ When they are dry you can get
sand from the bottom of them.”
* * » *
Dr. T. F. MacDonald, of the Bureau of
Tropical Disease, (deraldton (N.Q.), lecturing in
Melbourne:—■
Under tropical conditions the white people could work
and flourish. The old danger of disease had, to a large
extent, disappeared. North Queensland was growing a
fine white race, many with the almost abnormal chesf
expansion of 7in. His observation showed him that the
white gangs in the sugar fields are really healthier than
colored gangs working- under the sam,e conditions. Any
large alien population would only lead to the physical,
mental, and moral deterioration of the white races.
A BALLAD OF BATTLE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bill Thomson was a burly bloke ;
His weight was twelve stone ten.
No dog dare bark when William spoke
Among the station men.
At boxing he was very deft —
Or so he used to skite ;
He said he had “ a dirty left ”
And “ very ugly right.”
He used to boss the other chaps
And order them about ;
They seemed to be afraid ; perhaps
They thought they’d get a clout !
The station hands at Dingolong
Mere nervous seemed to grow ;
It certainly was very strong
How Thomson ran the show !
At length there came to Dingolong
A silent sert of man ;
He too was sinewy and long,
His name, we learned, was Dan.
He came to act as station cook
And bake the ’possum pie ;
He had a calm, determined look,
And steely cold grey eye.
One day Bill Thomson growled a bit
About the breakfast meat,
An 1 swore the brownie wasn’t fit
For Chinamen to eat.
“ It’s all you’ll get,” observed the cook,
In gentle tones and bland ;
“ And growling doesn’t suit my book,
You’ll kindly understand.”
Bill Thomson stared ! Bill Thomson swore !
“ My special oath ! ” said he ;
“ Why, never did I hear before
Sich words addressed to me !
Just come outside, my gentleman,
And then, by all that’s blue,
I’ll show these fellows how I can <
Knock saucepans our of you ! ”
The slim-built cook, I may remark,
Was brought from Sydney town
By devious means and dodges dark,
To take Bill Thomson down !
And it was whispered, I may state,
Although he looked so tame,
He was a first-class middle-weight,
And understood the game !
Who won the fight, you ask ? Well, look—
Of all the sickening scenes 1
Bill Thomson bashed that blessed cook
To blessed Smithereens !
His cold grey eye did William close—
He knocked him all about,
He knocked hiip on his cold grey nose,
And then he knocked him out.
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.

No notice (except of a hostile character) will he taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and

James Marshall: The Bulletin doesn’t worry
about any religion except where it touches on politics.
A section of N.S.W. Protestantism (the Orange sectionj has
allied itself with the Foreign-trade party, and every candi-
date it puts up is a Foreign-trader and a virulent enemy
of Australian industry. Also a large number of the
Protestant clergy have allied themselves with the nigger-
labor party (the Anglican Synod recently' passed a pro-
nigger resolution, and allowed an Anglican bishop to tell
it that Asiatic civilisation was ahead of the civilisation of
Europe); and others (Fitchett, by way of a sample) have
joined the Stinking Fish gang—Fitchett is now telling
Europe generally about the imaginary horrors of this
country 7. So far, we haven’t come across any R.C. priest
who hollers for Foreign-trade, the destruction of Aus-
tralian industry, low wages, Kanakas, and the giving
away of this country to the scum of Asia, but when we
do we will make remarks to him. Perhaps when you call
The Bulletin “ the R.C. organ,” you reckon that it
should advocate the destruction of Australian industry
because most of the R.C. crowd seem to be for Australia..

ON YARRA’S SHORE.


A HAT DELIRIUM.
The Australian House of Reps, has the Hat Mania badly. It contains about six members who never make a speech without moaning “ Six
Hatters!” And last week 31 questions about hats (16 of them by B. Smith) were put on the notice-paper, because a consignment of hats had got
into trouble over a matter of alleged under-valuation. Samples of the arrested hats were brought into the House and tried on by excited
members. Prime Minister Deakin rose to explain amid a maddened chaos of hats.


After Henry Irving had achieved fame the
D.C.L. degree was thrust upon him. But to what
extent did that other D.C.L. the whisky to wit
—assist his eareer ! Most men admit that for
flashes of inspiration at critical moments the
whisky of the scholarly brand is to be relied upon
every time. No one can partake judiciously of
D.C.L. without proving by result that it is in
truth the Whisky of the Wise and the glory of
Life’s drama. “All the world's a stage, and all
its greatest men drink its Wisest Whisky ” as
Shakespeare didn’t say.*

ARCHDEACON HINDLEY, OF MELBOURNE.
A darky at a Wyalong (N.S.W.) Quarter
Sessions case, badgered by G. H. Reid to illustrate
the size of a hole down which he threw something,
finally said “ It was as big round as your stomach ”
The Court roared. The hole was found on subse-
quent measurement to be 3ft. in diameter. So it
wasn’t a very bad guess.
August Englehardt, the eccentric founder
of a sect of Sun Worshippers numbering four
cranks, whose chief “ plank ” was to live in a naked
condition on the beach of a remote Pacific isle is
still alive and naked. He was slain by a circum-
stantial .press report in July last, and The
Bulletin shed a tear over his demise. Said tear
is hereby swallowed.
