Mr. S. Irish, the first Druids’ secretary in
Australia, and the man who secured the first dis-
pensation for a lodge of long-beards (this was at
Adelaide in 1850), has passed out at Brisbane,
aged 79. He came to Australia 60 years ago,
and spent most of those years in the Queensland
capital selling drapery. Consequently when he
IJruided he had the advantage of being’able to sell
himself his own long pale shirt at cost price.
Brewers and ex-brewers are a healthy
race in Australia. F. J. Butting is 86, and goes
to business regularly. E. T. Smith is 74. and
►w-fo ca K e all the social functions.
V\ ilham Knapman is very active in his antiquity.
As a young man Botting was a builder, Smith an
ironmonger, and Knapman a carpenter. Last-
named turned a theatre and hotel, built at Port
Adelaide by George Coppin, into a beer foundry
away back in the times when the average citizen
drove his thirst before him like a herd of cattle
because it was too large and numerous to be
carried in the ordinary way.
Died in S. Aus., “Corny” Proud, who,
like two or three others, left the Inky Way to do
something considerable on the Adelaide Stock Ex-
change. But most times he had to play a lone
hand there. Even when at the top of the silver
boom he was running one of the biggest share-
broking businesses in Australia, he still kept up
his letters to the papers and his speeches against
I at and Monopoly ; and the rest of the Stock Ex-
chonge reckoned that that wasn’t playing the
game. He put in good work in recent years on
behalf of the municipalisation of Adelaide trams,
and in most things his energy was on the right
side. ‘
At a recent meeting of the Collingwood
(Melb.) branch of the P.L.L. the allegation was
made that Wilkins, M.L.A., had ceased to be
a financial member of the League and was six
quarters in arrears with his subscriptions. The
president decided that he had lost his membership
of the branch, and the question of finding some
Labor candidate other than Wilkins for next elec-
tion - was vvarml y discussed. Next night Mr.
Wilkins moved the adjournment of the Vic
Assemble “the position of the Victorian Racing
Club and the by-laws which have been exercised
against Wren of the Tote. Did Mr. Wilkins mean
that to assert his fitness to represent the Collins-
wood Labor League in Parliament ?
Died the other day, aged 86, William
M Intosh, the virtual founder of Narracoorte
(b.A.) township. He came to Sydney in 1837
passed down to Victoria, and thence in 1845
“overianded” to the Narracoorte district. In
1.848, as a storekeeper, he started a settlement
which he called Kincraig, after his birthplace in
Inverness (Scotland), on the south side of Narra-
coorte Creek. Nine years later the Government
laid out Narracoorte township on the other bank
but Mac.’s village had the business, and kept
most of it. Later on he “ squatted ” pretty con-
siderably, among the estates he either owned or
shared being Baker’s Range, Mount Scott
Cromer, Narracoorte, and Moy Hall. Reverses
came m time, and he went off the land, and put
in the rest of his days in a stock and station
agency business.
, North Queensland even the parson
isn’t the ordinary sort of parson. The best-
known of him, Archdeacon Campbell, of Cairns
is partly preacher and partly metallurgist If lie
preaches a sermon on Tophet, he straightway
delivers a lecture on some mineral or other to
balance things. Back of Cairns more prospecting
is going on now than anywhere else in Australia 0 ;
and the archdeacon spends most of his time
making free assays and giving gratis advice to
the man who reckons he has dropped on to some-
thing. Which suggests a new scheme for the
training of parsons, just officially alleged to be
most defective. The parson or parson ess who is
sent out to the heathen land ahead of the rifle
and the rum has to study medicine; why not
tea,oh the local parson how to treat lumpy jaw
and pickle wheat ? Why should only the heathen
get something thrown in with his Christianity.

day, August . Fnederick Heppner, a sturdy old

was at the instigation of Heppner, who stumped
the electorate and practically secured the seat for
the present Minister of Customs. Heppner could
neither read nor write, but was singularly shrewd
and for more than 30 years was the most influential
man in the district.

Marrnondelle had a friend to whom he made
speeches ten minutes long about the abstract
virtues, while the friend stood wearily first on one

Marrnondelle were dead. That was many years
ago, but the friend has been gone in the feet ever

Pastor Worthington, the man who swin-
dled various women withincomprehensible remarks
about Egypt, is likely to get out of liis Victorian
gaol. It is alleged that his health is affected by
oakum-picking and by confinement in a building
that is wholly devoid of ornament. The Govern-
ment has lent part of an ear to the appeals and
promises that if medical evidence shows the grey-
headed conbdence man s health to be really going
an order will be signed for his release. Yet any
number of men who were not cronk parsons, and
didn t have beautiful grey hair and an upward ex-
pression like one looking for an angel in a tree-top,
have been allowed to die in gaol. The difference
really lies m the hair and the holy aspect. Those
whom Worthington took down helped to send him
to gaol in a burst of temper. Now they have re-
pented and remember the old man’s hair again,
and picture it hanging out of the cell window in
t ie moonlight, and being trodden on by the coarse
warder as he goes by on his rounds. Great is the
power of ha r.

Some facts about W. N. Willis (sum-
marised), from an evidently admiring friend, in
All About Australians
His grandfather was a Peninsular War veteran, Private
Willis, who married and settled in N.S.W. in Governor
Darling’s time. Private Willis had a son, John Willis, a
farrier, of Mudgee, noted for his physical strength.
John Willis had a large family, of which one was William
Nicholas, born at Mudgee 47 years ago. John Willis left
for [Frisco, died there, and Mrs. Willis was left with
William Nicholas and the rest of the family. She made
a brave struggle and brought up the youngsters de-
cently. Among other enterprises of the widow was a
dressmaking business, and she employed there Sarah Bed-
ford, the first wife of John Haynes, one of the Haynes who
haunt William Nicholas. The great land agent picked up
his education mostly at a night school. His first job was
in an office at 2s. 6d. a w eek. Then he was a “ super ” at
the old Victoria Theatre, Pitt-street, managed by John
Bennett. William Nicholas was a handsome youth with a
curly mane and a bonny blue eye, and John Bennett took
a fancy to him and promoted him to small parts. W.N.
played as W. N. Kingston. A chest£gmplaint drove him
out West and he was a store-hand, then a successful
hawker, again a storekeeper, and then a pastoralist. He
dipped into journalism as part owner of Truth and finally
took to land agency.
An old fogey thus :
Apropos “ Wentworth the Lag” (B. 19/10/’O5). Don’t
blame Macquarie only in the matter of this error. I don’t
suppose the Wentworths of to-day care in what position
their Australian progenitor arrived. In a despatch to
Gastlereagh (April 1810) Macquarie wrote : “ The number
of persons of this description—emancipists—whom I have
admitted to my table consist of only four, namely Mr.
D’Arcv Wentworth, principal surgeon ; Mr. William Red-
fern, assistant surgeon ; Mr. Andrew Thompson, an opu-
lent farmer and proprietor of land ; and Mr. Simeon Lord,
an opulent merchant. Three of these persons have
acquired property to a large amount ; they have con-
ducted themselves with the greatest propriety, and I
find them at all times ready to come forward in the most
liberal manner to the assistance of the Government. In
order to mark my sense of the merits of Mr. Andrew
Thompson, I have already appointed him a justice of the
peace and magistrate at the Hawkesbury, where he has a
large property, and I intend to confer the same marks of
distinction on Mr. Wentworth and Mr. Simeon Lord when
vacancies in the magistracy at Sydney, where they both
reside, may occur.” These two were appointed J’s.P.
17/5/1810.
In 1812, in a publication known as The Newgate
Calendar, the following appeared : “D’Arcy Wentworth,
once a famous highwayman on the road leading to the
metropolis, became a Botany Bay Esquire with two offices
under Government. He was Surgeon-General, a Justice
of the Peace, and a trustee for the public roads.”
. B. Barton, in “ Poets and Prose Writers,” published
in 186-said this “ William Charles Wentworth was born
at Norfolk Island in 1701. His father, D’Arcv Wentworth,
went there in the previous year, and a certain authority
tells us that he was ‘particularly noticed’ by Major
Ross, the Commandant, who on account of his ‘abilities
and reputation, immediately appointed him a superin-
tending surgeon of the island.” Bligh, on the trial of
Colonel Johnston, alluded to D’Arcv Wentworth as
having been sent out, and the statement went un-
challenged.
In 1898, nearly 40 years after “ Poets and Prose Writers “
was published, Mr. G. B. Barton published in a Sydney
newspaper a life of W. C. Wentworth, and was taken to
task for “ Bowdlerising ” history in the account given of
DArcy Wentworth’s advent to Norfolk Island. Mr.
Barton backs down somewhat, and ignores Macquarie’s
mention, giving Bligh as the author of the scandal. Mac-
quarie’s despatch reached England before Johnstone’s
trial, which took place on May 11,1811, and lasted a
month. Barton practically calls Bligh a liar, inasmuch
as he (Bligh) must have known that Wentworth came out
free, he (Bligh) having all the papers concerning prisoners
6tc., at his command. But in this Barton errs, as the
early Governors—notably King—complained that ship-
loads of prisoners and exiles were sent out without a scrap
of paper to indicate their offences or their sentences.
A man of great holding power.
Czar possesses more capacity than any of his
Ministers.— SYDNEY MORNING HERALD.
Queensland Hansard, through a rush of
work, has now to engage an extra man for the
Assembly one night a week. An average night
there means something like eight ordinary news-
paper columns per man. With a late sitting it
means more. Yet all the chief has been allowed
tor the extra hand expected to turn out this quan-
tity of copy is £1 a night (or, at most, 2s. 6d. a
column). That is sheer sweating, which "even
liard-up Queensland ought not to find necessary.

The Directors are pleased to be able to report a satisfactory half-year. The increase of business referred to in
March last has been well maintained through the dull season of the year, but profits have again been adversely
affected bv the close-cut rates for London Exchange. , „ ,„ ri ~,
The Directors have to record with extreme regret the death of their esteemed colleague, Mr. Charles Camp bell,
who always took a very keen and active interest in the affairs of the bank, of which he was one of the original
directors and a large shareholder. The vacancy thus created has, according to the articles of association, to be filled
by the board, who have this important matter under consideration. ,' ’ ' . ... ~ . ~ .
The Directors again beg to thank Shareholders for the substantial support accorded to the institution in the past,
to which its success is largely due, and they hope for a continuance of the same “ enlightened selfishness in the
future. By Order of the Board,
H. T. WILSON, Manages.

. The return fare for the Indian trip (and renew-

nard on an aide-de-camp and blows him down it
may disarrange things for two months.
It has come as a great shock to a prospective
man-o -war bridegroom to learn that his mother
has married her younger son’s tutor. So when
the local girl bride (that is to be presently) gets to
England, she can compare bridal trousseaux with
her mother-in-law, and help the latter to shake
rice out of her gomg-away gown.
What would not the Australiennes give for a
visit from that section of the British fleet known
as the Second Cruiser Squadron ! “It is composed
ot six of the fastest and smartest ships” and
this is a gratuitous statement—officered by men to
whom the same adjectives might be applied.
1 nnce Louis of Battenberg commands it, and it
has a sub-title, “ The Dancing Squadron.” When
its Admiral gives a ball on his flagship, a special
dancing floor is put down over the deck. Farm
Oove aches for that squadron.
One of the most gold-leaved girls in Sydney,
Miss I. Lyons, goes hence presently to marry a
man who was on the Australian station for a few
months. She is a clipper-built style of girl, and
knows how to spread her hat just the right way.
Her late father, Captain Lyons, was a Balmain
identity of note. A farewell Tea of great august-
ness was given her at the Australia last week.

Miss Muriel Marks who, after her return from

Piccadilly droop. Two sisters—N.S. Walers, not

Douglas Gerrard has been taken up by the belle
whose name was so bracketed with Cuyler
Hastings, and her set, and is, generally speaking
being stroked down by society.
I would like to start a Society for the Prevention
r Lobsters. On my morning sprint
from the Quay I pass a fish shop at the door of
winch is always a basket of live lobsters, their very
advanced eyes staring wildly, their claws clawing
the air feebly. Once I asked a Tito Melema be
hind a prawn counter how he killed the lobsters.
His reply was too ready—“ Put them in cold water
and they drowns.” It was a fish story. They are
by some fishmongers at any rate, slowly boiled into
the tomb. No wonder so many deaths are
due to eating lobster. Scores of charming young
actresses and members of the corps de ballet eat
a lobster mayonnaise before retiring, and that
night their soul is demanded of them. To be
*u°° j d a lobster is a fearsome thing. But if
the dramatic profession and clubmen will eat
lobster and die, some more humane method of
killing the poor shell-fish ought to be adopted.
Of course hardly any food—of the kind that
used to be alive —will stand very close investi-
gation. Remember the Angel in Wells’ “Won-
dU U ~ Slt ”• who loat his appetite altogether
when the train of facts leading up to roast lamb
was unrolled to him? There are many who
sympathise with that feeling. I know several
suburban homes where the home-fed poultry can’t
be 1,7, or ie table because “the children
wouldn t eat anything they’d seen alive.” Most
people, however, practise a happy hypocrisy in
such matters. They know that cutlets don’t grow
on currant bushes, but shut their minds to the
thought of the slaughter-house. But a stand
should be made against unnecessary cruelty in
food preparation. And - lobsters boiled slowly to
death (I believe they scream quite humanly as
things begin to get warm for them) are beyond
the pale.
By the way, a Maoriland school-master recently
got a note from the mother of one of his pupils
requesting him “not to teach Johnny any more
about his inside because it made him sick.” De-
scriptions of blood or “innards” affect the vivid
imagination of a child, as teachers of physiology
know well, and lessons on the intestines are often
interluded by faints or nausea of the pupils. An
Australian doctor, who studied in Edinburgh, tells
how, at the Royal Infirmary there, it was no un-
common sight to see three or four students “ coming
to on the stone floor outside the lecture theatre,
lo go off at a tangent: It would be difficult to get
any woman to look at a prize fight, yet one of the
most attractive pictures to women in the series
shown during a recent flickergraph season was a
prize fight. It looked horribly realistic, yet
women used to go just to see that “turn,” and
then they would leave, shuddering, “because
there wouldn’t be any more fights.” Makes one
think of one of Macaulay’s lays—that in which
two severed heads, stuck on spears, are carried
through the applauding streets, “and maids that
shrieked to see the heads, yet shrieking pressed
more nigh.”

Co. took him up at once. Tenor Roberts told
JNuttali he was doing well, singing in grand opera.

not very good health, is on the way back to Aus- '

Miss Nora Kerin’s matrimonial choice is a Mr.
Cyril Michael. Miss Kerin is one of the five
famous ccmsins, of whom the other four are Miss
Julia Neilson, Miss Hilda Jacobson, Miss Hilda

Of a travelling She various hostels, or rather
their guests, speak much and loudly. The tourist
announced that she sang. She said it with the air
of one who has had Great Advantages. Into the
hostel drawing-room one night another woman
brought a Kosciusko sized pile of music and begged
the tourist to choose and sing. There was none of
her songs in all that 1J yards of melody. But
so that the guests should not go dis-
appointed to bed the visitor said “I shall sing
you a Cherokee love-song.” For ten minutes she
howled like a dingo, occasionally turning round to
translate a Cherokee phrase at the listeners. When
the dirge ceased the dirger said, “ I am so sorry I
can t sing you ‘Entreat Me Not To Leave Thee,’
but on the voyage out someone stole my music.”
rive men who had been listening arose as one
and went into the bar and drank the thief’s
health.

to Australia. ‘
The “ Thunderbolt ” poster, “ Bail-up,” show-
ing the coach stopped by bushrangers, has caused
Douglas Fry, the animal painter, now visiting
Australia, to speak the admiring word. Many of
them, in fact. Mr. Fry has gone to Melbourne,
and wdi paint the winners of the Caulfield and
Melbourne Cups. Others going towards the great
annual drinking utensil are Mrs. Burdekin and
her daughter, also Mr. Owen Cox and Miss
Bonsor, and heiress Walker, and a string of
friends. The steamship Potters will quit Maori-
land salmon fishing in time for the Great Neigh.
Major Robert Stephen, hussar-son of Septimus
Stephen, is returning to Australia for a sojourn.
He has been an absentee more or less for 18 years.
But he still held hands across the sea, and some
five years ago married Miss Mollie Osborne. His
brother Colin s horse, Elvo, had, it may be men-
turned a strong following of Pointers for the
Caulfield Cup. But the animal was one of those
that commanded a splendid view of the other
steed s tail.
Mr El well, an American sailor, and his bride,
a Melbourne girl, are having a honeymoon. Some
months ago they left Australia for America in the
Koanake, of which Mr. Elwell was first officer.
I fie Roanake, which prided itself on being the
largest wooden ship afloat, was burnt to the
waters edge at Noumea, and the Elwells, with
the rest of the company, escaped on board
the Susquehanna, an American barque lving
handy, and also homeward bound. But two days
from port the Susquehanna “opened out” (or
broke its back, or something) and sank. The
boats couldn’t beat back against the trade winds
and for five days the Elwells pursued their honey-
moon on the Pacific with little to eat, and not
much more to drink, till they hauled the remnants
of their trousseau ashore at the Solomons. And
when last I heard of them they were still following
up. that indefatigable honeymoon there or there l
abouts.

Wellington girls are making much of boyish-
looking Dr. Bell, Maoriland’s newly imported
Director of the Geological Survey. Bell is a clever
young American who has dropped into a well-paid
billet. Only 28 or so, he has seen and done a good
deal—graduated as Doctor of Science, lectured on
geology at Harvard University, explored in the
great lone land of Northernmost Canada, shot
grizzlies, Esquimaux and other strange animals,
even confesses to having written a novel—an
American novel at that. He is an enthusiastic
youth, and it was perhaps to cool his ardor that
Premier Seddon sent him down for the winter to
the Westland bush, gold-hunting. The doctor is
strong on outdoor sports, and talks of importing
his Canadian snow-shoes to “ski” over the
Southern Alps, and his birch-bark canoe to shoot
the Wanganui rapids. His sister arrives from
America shortly. Other men’s sisters will
certainly think her a superfluous quantity.
The Australian voice abroad isn’t always as that
of one crying in the wilderness. Artist Arthur
Colhngridge, whilst going through the “innards”
of the 1 yramids lately, got awfully greased by the
sputtering candles used by the guides. He wrote
to the Department of Antiquities in Egypt before
leaving that angular land and pointed out this seri-
ous drawback to the exploration of old Pharaoh-
dom, and the other day got a courteous reply saying
that the authorities, moved thereto by Mr. C’s.
letter, were going to light the passages with
electricity.
Are there any relics of Nelson left in any other
part of the world ? So many have turned up in
centenary week one is reminded
of Mark Twain on “ Bits of the True Cross.” Old
ladies and gentlemen nearing 90 have “ distinct re-
collections ” of being chucked under their infantile
chins by Nelson and noticing that he was minus a
limb and an eye. At the great flag-flap at the
-Lown Hall on Saturday night, a great-grandson
of Admiral Pasco—who, as a flag lieutenant,
hoisted the famous signal on the Victory a hundred
years ago—hauled up a like signal across the plat-
form. Kiddy was introduced by Admiral Fan-
shawe, and as the Telegraph remarks, with
patriotic tears rolling down its nose: “It was a
moving sight—the Admiral, the child, and the
expectant audience.” Mrs. Hugh Dixson, the
principal bunting presenter, was absent. The
lady has just undergone an operation for appendi-
citis. Anybody would be absent under such
conditions.
A herd of University under-grads, had the
gallery of the Palace on last night of “ The J.P.,”
and made a witless display. They lowered
“high” crayfish on to the stage and the like,
i-hey determined that the co. should remember
them by their bad odor. A local merchant, in the
stalls with the wife, was struck on the head by a
bag of rice. It burst, and he has angry opinions
on the wearing of rice next the skin.
The Ramaciotti automobile is a stylish thing in
toot machines. Yellow wheels and a dark body—
I have seen grasshoppers so colored. There is
also a Ramaciotti motor launch lying on the face
of our harbor waters.
* Cable announces that “Earl Cowley will be
married to Lady Cradoek-Hartopp, the divorced
wife of Sir Charles Cradock-llartopp, at Colombo.”
I his is the lady who, for the three .weeks that her
suit lasted, wore a different and ravishing costume
every day. And the charm of her toilettes must
have gladdened the jury. Now, a jury-influencing
gown, unlike .Jonah s gourd, does not grow in a
night. Think of the prescience of the lady ! She
must have had at least 15 gowns built for the trial.
How did she know that the revelations and the
arguments and the points were going to last out
that long ?
A high-jinks bachelor in Sydney keeps a pair of
beautifully modelled legs to be used as “pin-
cushions.” They are nearly life size. The bend
of the knee, with three silk frills above and an en-
Uancmg garter, and the pretty foot are all there.
Other day, whilst dispensing afternoon tea to a
small crowd, talk turned on Phil May, and the
high-jmker took the group into his bedroom to
show it some Mays. A unit of the party was a
young Englishman studying for the Church (some
church). At sight of the Legs he fell back a
pace and turned tomato-faced. The high-jinker
looked at him tiredly and quoted, “‘Man'is the
only animal who blushes—or who has need to.’”
David Jones and Co. are turning out some
special gowns for the Cup. Lace and chiffon and
silken fabrics woven and wrought into cunning
works of art that might be hung in the National
Gallery. Might be —onlv they’d want a special
guardian against women until change of fashion
put em out of date.
. The Frank Gardners will be in JMelbourne
m time for the Glorious Fourth, and if Madame
does not take the pineapple for face and style—
well, my opinion of Melbourne art sense will
drop over a great precipice. He—F.G.—is
said to be in a fair way to re-assert himself as a
mining magnate in N.S.W. Also various local
Men of Standing have balanced up to him saying,
‘Start ye an automobile factory in’ this fair
mtyV’ The Gardner-Serpollet auto works
in 1 aris cover five acres. Their part owner
thinks N.S.W. is on the eve of a huge mining
revival, and he is backing his thoughts by ex-
tensive lease-buying in the Cobar and Orange
districts.
A capital portrait of Mayor Allen Taylor has
been achieved by Falk, and will be presented to
the original by his constituents and friends to-
morrow night at the Town Hall.
A little golden-haired darling of five or six
caused many beads to turn on Sydney Block on
a recent Saturday morning. She was escorting
her mother, a well-dressed, sensible-looking
matron, and wore a white bonnet, red cloak, and
no stockings or shoes. It was a bitter morning,
and her legs looked red and chill, but missie pat-
tered along as if thoroughly happy. Boots are
mostly a bother Jx> children, anyhow.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.
MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, October 23, 1905.
My Dear Yarrie,—
An air of complimentary bereavement sobered
the G.G. contingent at the Caulfield Cup. Her
Ex. trailed a purple velvet coat,, which she wore
all day over the lawn, while Governor Bedford’s
spouse took her subdued strut in temperate mauve
chiffons. The polished black head-piece of the
G.G. and staff completed the mournful look of
things. But outside that pen the whole racing
tribe rioted in gay apparel. Toorakites swarmed
in simple attire, leaving the splash to the popula-
tion of beer and books, always strong at the big
meeting. Miss Ruby Madden, back from the
Osbornes’ cattle-run, showed up meekly in a
French-blue garb and sat-upon hat. The younger
Miss Lesly was scarcely better decked in rusty
green. A whole flock of forward Toorak chickens
showed the same indifference to a chiffon sensation.
So Hebe and the Block charmer had the field to
themselves. Miss Wilson scored another point
for Geelong in a silk-pink glace and a hat that
was a cross between a Romney and a coolie’s straw.
Mrs. Everard Browne reared a vermilion toque fly-
ing a paradise plume from her defiant top-piece,
but the rest of her didn’t match the gaudy note.
Mrs. J. Alston Wallace sported the wealthiest
white frock of the Terrace swarm. Mrs. Dyce
Murphy and Mrs. Edward Miller seemed much
more important than their garb. They both
looked scornfully superior. Mrs. Wesley Hall had
a resurrection in black and repentance purple.
Dame Talbot’s renewal of mourning has damped
the spirits of many official and semi-official Gup
hosts. The death of a brother is the cause. All
engagements except those marked “ charity ” are
wiped off the slate, and the grey dame wears the
habiliments of woe once more. She had only
evolved a few colors from a wardrobe darkened by
a similar family bereavement on her husband’s side
some time ago, and had ordered some gay apparel
when the second piece of bad news arrived.
However, their Exes, have decided not to cancel
the one large Stonington spree of the year—the
lawn party—for which pasteboards are promis-
cuous, and many of the guests come from very
outer circles.
The President Wrixons mourn the Talbot
mourning. The State Exes were to be their
piloe dc resistance, but the black-bordered sta-
tionery has intervened. It isn’t possible to
secure the Commonwealth Vice-Regals, and now
Mr. Bent will have to star before the frilly
multitude on the 2nd. The horror of it !
The A.D.C .-in-waiting once more “ is desired by
their Exes.,” &c., to invite Mr. and Mrs. Blank to
the strawberry scramble that ushers in the clothes-
horse season. The Y.R. Lawn Trail of the 31st
will see the freshest of the chiffons, as it precedes
the races this year of nag.
After a great deal of scheming and plea, the
Alexandra Club has roped in Dame Northeote for
its top-notch patroness. The club that wants its
beer license has been after this blank honor for a
long time, but her smilesome Ex. kept the
Tabithas waiting. On Thursday there’s to be a
grand crush at the Pianola Hall when the
patroness will beam during the dreary reading of
the annual report. It is pleaded in extenuation
that this is the first report ever submitted by the
frilly management. There are 550 members in the
club, and of these there are 250 not wanted. The
cream, such as it is, runs to 300 in this State.
The difficulties the Society dame experiences
in bobbing to the greater Exes, amused the
Stand crowd on Saturday. One of the cream was
banged brutally forward, and nearly extended her
bob to a prostration, because she was run into by a
backer, helter-skeltering along the terrace to collect
his little bit. Not every dame can preserve her
balance when bumped amidships as she endeavors
to accomplish a demi-semi genuflection.
The race-cloak was the strong .novelty of the
Cup. One forward matron liked hers—a ‘tomato-
toned affair—so well that she adopted it for the
day and strutted so persistently that the Stand
took on _the look of a tomato splash. The silk
variety in many instances looked like theatre
cloaks out on the loose. The linen kind were as
the butcher’s overalls, and the tussores alone
resembled the thing needed.
The latest in startling millinery effects is a set
of ribbon streamers worn aft in the manner of the
undertaker’s crape weepers. The funereal idea
was the better carried out, last Saturday, by one
Caulfield strutter having her streamers in the
purple hue of regret. These hung to the rear of
her festive hat with a solemn suggestion of the
grave behind the gay. Another, flourishing
green rear ribbons like something stolen from a
green parrot’s hinder decoration, merely gave one
the idea of a bird-tail of inordinate length.
The fashion of scattering Val. insertion and
edge over the summer raiment spreads. I saw in
preparation for the Cup a garb which owned four-
teen dozen yards of Yal. Someone in the brewery
line of business is to wear it. There is nothing
like beer to froth into lace. The dress builder is
in despair over other orders to lay on the Val.
thick and profuse. The sewing on of this finest
of lace fripperies is a minute and laborious per-
formance. “The only thing,’’ said the weary-
eyed, nerve-wrecked clothes-architect, 'is to
charge for it.” And charge for; it she does with a
vengeance. Infinite pains must be taken
in stitching leagues and leagues of the Val. all
over the material. Even the roses and wheels
formed of it take countless dozens to produce any
effect. And Mistress Smart pays through the
nose for the trouble.
Mrs. Fred Payne gets “ Maretimo ” ready for the
particular Toorak hop of the carnival. The thing
is to be done vastly well. The usual Cup hostesses
are holding off this year. Even the Cliveden
dowager abates her entertaining zeal, and limits
her hostly attention to a Cafe Chantant for a sick
fund of some sort.
The T. H. Paynes had a fine turn-out for the
Caulfield whirligig a tandem pair of prize
prancers, which raised the dust along the road

right valiantly. The weightiest carrier-van kind
of motors snorted aggressively past the gay
tandem, but not even the garishly decorated

could beat the spanking turn-out.
Two Vic. politicians came back from a grand
gad by the China —Agar Wynne and William
M‘Culloch. The former, who successfully mingles
horse interests with politics, has been seeing how
things are done at Ascot, and shaking the fat paw
of Wriggler Taverner with great cordiality.
Landlord M.L.C. M'Culloch has mostly dawdled
with youthful relatives. He’s a frisky veteran, is
William.
The Australian Club is forward with paste-
boards for a dance on November 10. This club
gets very extravagant about its annual revel, and
the appointments are always perfect, even to a
wonderful arrangement of light and shade for the
sitters-out and the couples who climb to the tower
for a breath of fresh air.
The suburban Worships are still dispensing the
reception biscuit. Down at South Melbourne the
paw of greeting is extended to the local gasfitter
and the paperhanger of ambitious notions this
Wednesday. The populace generally is invited to
roll up and nibble.
The birthday, and the anniversary of the first
tooth, and the fiftieth year of wedlock celebra-
tion seem to tempt the Fates. Gyles Turner
had scarcely finished receiving his golden wedlock
congratulations when he fell ill. And he hasn’t
been very well since. The same thing happened
with old Dr. Neild. The wise thing is not to
rejoice too loudly over any good fortune.
The surprising engagement of the day is that of
a musical charmer —daughter of a prominent
manageress and Conservatorium engineer—to a
champion golfer. The man of the record scores is
being put to the test of appallingly long concerts,
and his sporting phiz is a study thereat. But the
girl is a very young and fresh dazzler, and her
near profile, even through an age-long classical
strum, must prove a comfort. A squatter friend
of mine who put in a recent evening at a particu-
larly profound .recital, gave out afterwards that
Beethoven’s Pastoral Sonata reminded him of
sheep (some of them lame) running down a moun-
tain. Perhaps the engaged golfer mentally com-
pares a Chopin Berceuse to a series of fast-driven
balls splitting the wind of the links.
A titled visitor has been affectionately interested
in a petite charmer who once frisked it with a
vaudeville troupe. Another instance of the
eligible jolinnie turning from the blandishments
of the Toorak flutterer to the joyful regard of a
gay footlighter.
Mrs. James Traill hasn’t tripped back to the Cold
Country and her languid spouse yet. It will be
remembered how the captain languished in the
long-ago role of A.D.C. For the time the dame
loiters at Stony Park, and puts in a lengthy
appearance (mostly in clingsome black) at any
function worth while.
Mrs. George Chirnside issued forth from a
lengthy retirement to break bread with the
Talbottles at one of their recent small and early
dinner parties. Since the swan-note of last
summer Mrs. George has refrained from joining
in any fashionable frisk, much to the disappoint-
ment of other friskers.
The sudden shining forth of Miss Olive Godwin
among “ The Orchid ” stars took a thronging race-
house on Caulfield Cup night by storm. As a
successor to Florence Young, the new tripper had
her responsibilities, but she carried them out
well—mainly owing to a soaring top-note, and as
graceful dress blandishments as Palotta’s of old.
One song, a duet with baritone Jackson, and one
dress, a golden symphony, with a crown-piece in
nasturtium tones, won the crowd completely, and
other sins of omission or commission (due to in-
experience) were forgiven her. She’s a fetching
young thing, and her dresses are a delight.
Mrs. DyceMurphy, a superior dame with the lofty
manner, is lodged up at the Grand with her family.
The dame, round whom so much romance has been
woven from time to time, walks to the fore with
majestic mien. Her entrance into the theatre —a
lace mantilla fluttering from the top-mast—is more
magnificent than any stage-effect. A grown
daughter carries all her ma’s airs and graces multi-
plied by two.
A dame of interesting theatrical antecedents
and tragic domestic experience has re-married,
and joined the ranks of grease. Perhaps the re-
marrying came after the grease-dabbing. How-
ever, both occurred.
The stormy authoress of “ Arcadian Adelaide,”
after a marked season of gaiety at Menzies, has
sailed for ’Frisco. Hubby too. The Fisher pair
left their Melbourne pals many festive memories
and an aching head.
A few of the Federal dames have a yearning to
get some pleasure and profit out of the gardens
surrounding the twin houses of Babel. Members’
wives have a growing sense of their importance to
the Commonwealth. Dame Forrest thought of
organising lawn sprees of some kind, but nothing
came of the intention. Dame Fysh nodded her
bonnet many times in approval of such garden
flutterings, but nothing came of that either.
However, Mrs. I. Isaacs took the Bananaland
dame—Mrs. Groom—ipto council, and the pair
determined on a croquet party. Last Mon-
day she shed her air of dreamy languor to
make this a success. The guests represented
the cream and skimmed milk of society
—Federal and otherwise. There is a new
arrangement holding with regard to these Parlia-
mentary lawns. Though locked while the House
is up, members may secure the key from the
janitor and trot their she-chums into the green
Eden—a great boon to philandering members, who
demand that key most of the adjournment period
and have a garden party pretty well to themselves
and the girl.
Miss Emily Dyason recital-ed on a grand piano
to the most frocky audience that has put in an
evening at Glen’s this year. The Dowager, the
Mayoress, and a few others of the very glassy were
there in considerable splendor. The Dowager
has a passion for the cane seat of the mighty and
the carpet patch of reserve. The strumrner of
this recital was most stylish. She interpreted
Beethoven and Chopin in ivory glace with the
silver glistenings that proclaim the ex-debutante.
Expense is nothing to the Dyasons, who own
leagues of territory and mining plant in Bendigo.
A whole clan of much wool-wealth is rejoicing
over the arrival of a son and heir to a couple who,
after 12 years of domesticity, had counted cribs out
of their province. A dozen aunts, a grand-dame
or two, and a host of the more remote kith and kin
are crowing over the arrival of the belated infant
The veteran widow of old-time Speaker Francis
Murphy had a birthday frolic the other day, when
she flourished a double eight on the scoring-board.
She is a vigorous party, with green memories of
the late Francis and his record fourteen years of
Speakership, a period so long agone that many
rather old people are surprised to hear of there
ever having been a Speaker named Murphy.
The cunning notion of publishing picture books
to illustrate Summer fashions has earned for
Buckley and Nunn more serious attention than
the average drapery publication gets. To see a
girl in a cheap cambric frock cut so carefully as to
make her look a Greek goddess is a seductive sort
of ad. To view a picture-girl looking like a
freshly-plucked peach in a frilly muslin that costs
little more than the dirt-cheap linen is a further
decoy. Buckley and Nunn are evidently studying
the sex closely to hit right on its fancy as in the
present picture-book case.
A fresh character comes over the face of needle-
work. Small effects in infinitesimal thread are no
more, save in point-lace designs. Now broader
patterns prevail. - Sumptuous roses, of every hue
but those of Dame Nature, 101 l with Turkish
sumptuousness on bold backgrounds of orange,
purple, or strident blue. Other bold designs are
Oriental. Only a Cleopatra of Old Nile could
fitly rest her coppery head on a bronze-toned
cushion with tiger lilies flaunting wicked green
faces.
The long-postponed call to the Bench of our
learned brother Box must make that elderly
bachelor grin to himself. His single state was
mentioned as an obstacle to promotion at the last
shuffling of the judiciary cards. Still he re-
mained severely single, and kept to his club
quarters closer than ever. Now, still a bachelor,
he takes his place among the Solomons.
The She Writers’ Club will shortly utter its
swan-note if many more of its pillars remove
themselves to London. Already three have been
transferred, and now Miss McGowan, the cheeriest
and gladdest scribe in this State, talks of a trip ;
but hers will be a flying one limited to six months.
One of the best diplomaed girl-grads, of the
University, with the medical degree lately added
to her list, goes off in November to the distant
missionary quarters of India to instruct the heathen
in the ways of Christian Endeavor, as well as to
finger the heathen pulse. Dr. EfAe Stillwell, the
young medico in question, would have done better
on her native heath curing local appendicitis. All
the clever Stillwells take their studious and reli-
gious ways from their printer dad. But to make
Zenana mission work the object of her talents is
a sort of pathetic anti-climax to a long, laborious
course at the University. Considering, too, that
the local heathen, physically and spiritually, is so
much in need of skilled attention, Dr. Effie might
have fixed her brass-plate nearer home.
Mrs. Arthur Tuckett’s book on “The Garden ”
has made her an object of regard and curiosity for
a while. The stern-featured society dame gardens
like a professional, and learnt her trade at the
Burnley Horticultural College where an interesting
instructor (C. Luffman) talks flowers, grubs and
soil to a number of girls. Mrs. Tuckett glories in
the champion garden of the State. Her book is
the result of a practical year’s round of the garden.
Society hails the dame now as an authoress of
fame.
Frantic efforts are being made to realise a
decent effect by ' Henley-on-the-Drain this jear.
Several Toorak householders have consented to
disguise the boot-box or dog-kennel that serves
for a house-boat in a fairly extravagant way.
The clubs have followed suit in promising. Even
the Alexandra is to drop into line with the
male bodies. A special pen to occupy the
grassiest bank site of the drain is to be fitted up
for the Federal Exes. Bands by noon and
crackers by night are to help make the Henley
attempt go. Some haw-haw johnnies are going to
see, bai Jove, that the thing is done decently this
time. It’s quite time it was.
Failing the Vice-regal dames, committees lassoo
in the next fittest functionary for the brick-bless-
ing and the church-bazaar. The dowager of East
Melbourne is most frequently besought for the
job. Sometimes it is the Waxcellency dame.
Mrs. Alf. Deakin is requisitioned when the affair
has scholastic or churchy objects. Recently com-
mittees have been employing Dame Fysh, the
fussy little Tasmanienne.
The fashion-plates of the hour call for the infla-
tion or stiffening of meagre parts of the figure.
The high-puffed sU-ves that make the wearer look
compressed by invisible side-planks have to be
buckramed out, and the full skirt in most cases
flows over a roller of more buckram. Regular little
pillars of wadding and kapok are used by the
angular she to gain a little of the soft curving
effect pictured about the hip and back portion of
the figure in the Paquin plates. The discomfort
of such hot packs is borne with the stoic smile
that distinguishes the sex when something has to
be suffered in the cause of appearing to be beauti-
ful.

“Squidge” re the proposed statue of
Victoria: —
He was only a beer-eater, but he assured me that he
had a vote and this is what he said to me : “ S’pose she
was a good enough sorter old person—as queens go ; but
she never did nuthin’fer me. I don’t want to remember
’er. If I did want to, wot good is a statoo in London
with a ’edge leadin’ up to it. I won’t never get to London.
An’ yet I’ve got to part up my w’ack of 25,000 quids so the
people in London will be stopped from forgettin’all about
’er. Now, what I ses is this : if Deakin wants to make a
triboot of affeckshun in the ’arts of the people’e’ll split
up that there 25,000 quid into tanners. That’ll give ’im a
million tanners. Well, ’e’ll make a few ’undred casts of
the widd.v and he’ll arst every fambily in the Commin-
wealth—I’m a fambily meself, yer know—w’ether it ’ud
rather ’ave a plaster cast or a tanner. An’ I’m takin’ the
tanner every time, an’ so is lots of blokes I knows. W’en
people is dead, I ses let ’em rest.”
A PAYING BIRD.
[For ThI Bulletin].
Let “ statesmen ” whose watchword is, “ Go on the land !

That a tax-paying worker’s a bird in the hand ;
And a factory hand is worth two in the bush.
s.a. Den.
The wife of a Gore Hill (North Sydney)
grocer has just had a tilt with the city coroner.
Early in October the lady’s house and shop caught
fire, and for her damage and loss she claimed
large sums. The co. hesitated a little The
claimant deposed that her losses included
£575 in sovereigns in one of the bedrooms, 10
oil paintings worth £lO each, and a valuable
carpet. The coroner found out that the imperish-
able carpet had been in use for 20 years, and that
the oil paintings were not by Rembrandt, but
were perpetrated by the daughter of the house,
and that while £575 was alleged to have been
upstairs, getting in everyone’s road, the grocer’s
wife had borrowed £350, and was paying per
cent, interest thereon. A thoughtful policeman
mentioned that he reckoned there were ten
separate fires in the partially destroyed premises.
Then the coroner spoke disrespectfully of the 575
sovereigns alleged to have been left in an unlocked
box in an empty house, and the verdict was “ up
against ” some party unknown. But the jury
should certainly have added a postscript to the
effect that it deeply admired the durability of the
carpet.
A file of papers, produced the other day
before the Victorian Police Inquiry Commission,
showed that the chief Commissioner, a superin-
tendent, an inspector, and two sub-inspectors had
written, among them, 40 letters in the course of
a wrangle whether the sub-inspectors should get
6s. 2d. for tram-fares or not. This is the police
force that every year allows 5000 to 6000 crimes to
go undetected. The officers and constables are
too busy putting minute No. 6,723,409 on an
official file to have time to do much else.
“ Land Agent ” writes :—-
Begging- Tub Bulletin’s pardon, a fearful lot of rot is
sometimes talked by people who advocate a stiff land-tax
about the unearned increment. The other day I sold
an allotment in a country town for £2O. Now this block
was bought from the Crown in 1841 for £2l, was sold in
1875 for £l9,.and is now sold for £2O. Here’s a sum :
How much ought that land to have brought to have
repaid the original purchaser his £2O at 4 p.c. ?
Without begging the land agent’s pardon, a fear-
ful lot of rot is written by people who think land
values should accumulate at compound interest, so
that a man can make an income by sitting down
looking at the soil. The owner of the above-
mentioned allotment should have made a lot more
than 4 p.c. per annum by using the land.

POLITICAL
POINTS
John Haynes’ Newsletter now says that
the Tobacco Trust is “ rather beneficial than other-
wise.”
Broken Hill workers are talking of a
general strike as a protest if the Arbitration Act
is strangled. Such an event would be a calamity,
but it s a calamity that the Garruthers Govern-
ment is inviting.
Victorian Premier Bent, as a State brick-
maker, has reached the last stage. This Socialistic
proposal to make the State bricks out of State-
owned clay with State-owned machinery was a
gleam of hope in a sordid mass of Toryism. But
the Tory tongue and the Tory pen were too much
for him. Now, to show how real is his repentance,
he has sold the buildings and plant erected at the
Thornbury brick-works to the very Combine he set
out so boastfully to burst.
Witness T. 0. Mackley, secretary of Melb.
Charity Organisation Society, in evidence before
the Federal Old Age Pensions Committee :
_ In the year 1904-5 we dealt with 1434 cases of distress.
The causes of distress were: Misfortune, 81 '3 per cent. ;
misconduct, 14'6 per cent. ; undeterminable, 4T per cent
In the 12 years, 1893-1905, out of 12,242 cases of distress,
829 cases, or 6'B per cent., were due to old age. We have
been told that 90 per cent, of the poverty of Melbourne
was due to indolence, intemperance, and unthrift. That
is absurd ; it is a libel on the people.

The idea of Irvine going into Federal
politics is again harped on in Melbourne, and the
is particularly fervid in describing the
aching nature of the void in the Commonwealth
Parliament which he is to fill. But the Victorians
that the Akgus admired the most have gone
hopelessly under in the Federal swim, and Irvine
would do the same. He has acquired his reputa-
tion on the strength of a cold reserve. He is a
self-possessed man. That is to say he never gives
himself away, but there is no proof yet that there
is anything to give.

Extract from a circular issued to members
of Lands Boards by W. P. Crick when Minister
for Lands:—
. T he administration of Justice ought not only to be pure
m itself, and capable of being demonstrated to be so, but
nothing should be done by those administering it to
throw on it a substantial doubt. It is not enough that
the conclusion arrived at was right, and that it has been
arrived at on right principles, for every person having a
personal interest in any litigation, or having a direct or
indirect motive for desiring a particular decision to be
come to, should abstain from putting himself in such a
position as that, unconsciously to himself, a bias adverse
to the due administration of justice might take posses-
sion of his mind.
“ Quien Sabe ” from Kalgoorlie (W.A.) :—-
I have attended a few sittings of the Tariff Commission
here, and was sickened by the tales told by witness after
witness as to Australia’s inability to produce machinery.
The whole burden of the song I heard was that this
country is too poor a thing to make anything for itself,
that everything would be lovely if only the Federal duties
were lowered and we imported more and made less, and
so on. To one who has loved and roved this land for over
forty years, and who believes in it, if only properly
protected against the foreigner, all this whine about our
inability to produce most of our requirements is indeed
disheartening. What the Commission will decide if it
hears much of the rubbish I heard put before it I don’t
know. I was hopeful it would recommend a good High
Tariff. Now I am full of fears.
The anti-peace riots in Japan, as reported
by cable, were mild and watery affairs compared
with “the horrible brutality ” of similar happen-
ings in Russia. Now the facts, not as told by the
Japs, but as observed by casual foreigners in
Japan, begin to come out. A sample of the Tokio
riots:—•
Then a scene of horrible butchery took place. The
police made no effort to get the mob on the run, but
went in with short swords, stabbing and slashing merci-
lessly. I saw one man with his head cut open to"the ear.
Close beside him fell a poor little fellow, about II years of
age, who had been slashed under the armpit. A junsa
rushed past on my right and thrust a big coolie through
the throat. The blood gushed out in a jet and bespotted
the junsa’s white uniform with scarlet. The rioters had
scarcely any weapons but stones and cudgels. The police
were drunk with blood. They did not use the fiat of the
swords, but even when the mob was scurrying away pur-
sued their victims pitilessly, thrusting and hacking at
cowering backs and arms thrown up to protect heads.
Sydney Telegraph strangely-mixes up the
proposed universal training of the people with
conscription, and headed a shrieking article in a
recent issue “The Conscription Craze.” Then it
went on to urge the abandonment of any serious
attempt to provide for the defence of Australia
because it is “repugnant to British instincts.”
How would it be to make the attempt, and find
out for certain if “British instincts” can put up
with it ? There is nothing to be gained by rushing
at conclusions about “ British instincts.”
Two instructive cables in one issue (Oct.
19) of the Australian dailies :
The appeal to the nation made by Field-Marshal Lord
Roberts for funds to encourage rifle shooting only pro-
duced £lOl7. P
The steamer Glaucus reports having spoken H.M.S.
Pioneer, towing H.M.S. Pyramus, which is broken down.
The Pyramus is coming out to reinforce the Australian
squadron.
Australia is urged by one section of its disloyalists
not to worry about defence but to pay a subsidy
to the Ma Country to look after us and keep
snakes off us. And in one day come cables
emphasising the facts that Britain doesn’t seem
capable of looking after its own defence, and that
the fleet sent here in return for our naval subsidy
is largely sent for the same reason, as the early
population was sent, because it is good stuff to get
rid of.
The latest person to talk the secession
foolishness is Premier Bason of Westralia, who is
snorting because his State hasn’t got its trans-
continental railway on the instant. Even if it
were possible for Westralia to secede, it is difficult
to see how much better off it would be as regards
its railway when it had done so. It would then
have no voice in the Commonwealth to cry out for
a Federal line, and would simply have to write
letters to the S.A. Government asking it to join
in building a line as a State enterprise, S.A.’s
share being to carry the work through its desert
to the border. Westralia can do that writing now ;
only S.A. has no money. For that matter
Westralia hasn’t the money for its own share of
the line, even if S.A. were able to move. At
present Westralia has two chances—of a State-
owned transcontinental or a Federally-owned one.
If it secedes it will lose the second chance, which
is really the only one worth mentioning.
UNANIMITY.
BOTH : “ Hallo, Nosey! ”
Dan Levy, in N.S.W. Assembly, objected
to Arthur Griffith’s Disposal of Property Bill—a
measure to prevent the ancient curmudgeon from
willing his property to a Lost Dogs’ Home and
throwing his widow and children on public charity
—on the alleged ground that for hundreds of years
testators have had the legal right to make such wills.
That argument would have been fati.l to most
reforms—among them the law permitting Jews
to sit in Parliament.
It now seems to be a fact that when
Germany attempted to hector France a few
months ago, Britain dropped a hint to the Kaiser
that it was with France on that issue even to the
point of fight, whereupon Germany changed the
subject hurriedly and said it looked like rain.
The news is good as showing the solidity of the
Anglo-French understanding, an understanding
which may in the future save Britannia from the
humiliation of house-keeping with an Asiatic.
Moran, leader of the Independent or Un-
fixed remnant in the Westralian Assembly, in-
formed a Perth audience last week that if he were
Premier he would urge the State to offer the Com-
monwealth £4,000,000 (not cash, but I O U) on
condition thac it was allowed to secede, and sink
back into the old local muddle. This reckless
sloshing around of a very dubious piece of paper
may yet bring Mr. Moran into trouble. When a
politically intoxicated person is found hanging on
to a lamp-post and offering to give away £4,000,000
that he hasn’t got, he is bound to attract the at-
tention of the police.
The horrible statement comes from London
that a systematic policy of selling white girls as
slaves is being pursued by Bruce Smith’s yellow
friends, in China. The girls are engaged to go
there to situations at high salaries, and then they
drop out of view. China swallows them up for
ever. It may cheer Bruce Smith to know that a
white girl slave is worth from £lOO to £2OO in
China.
The most disgraceful feature of the debate
on the N.S.W. Liquor Bill was the proposed
amendment of Holman, M.L.A., that a publican
should be allowed to supply drink in unlimited
quantities to any one (infants and aborigines
apparently included) so long as the said drink was
not consumed on the premises. The only time in
his life when Premier Carruthers rose to some-
thing like eloquence was when he threw this pro-
position back at Holman in disgust, and the
returned object struck Holman in the eye and
made him stagger.
One Rev. Craven, of Westralia, who has
been lecturing through England, returns with a
very modified Stinking Fish yarn—so modified, in
fact, as to give the lie to many preceding decom-
posed Friday dinners. He says that during his
travels he failed to observe any prejudice against
the Commonwealth by reason of its Immigration
Restriction Act; but adds that the clauses in the
postal contract re white labor “ were a subject of
universal discussion.” And considering how the
British marine person is despised and rejected in
his own land, any policy which aims at giving him
a job is likely to excite a good deal of discussion.

Another of Bent’s brilliant ideas—the
encouragement of railway passenger traffic in
Melbourne suburbs by grant-
ing free passes to persons
building new villas in cer-
tain outer districts—has
gone adrift. But little
advantage has been
taken of the concession,
but a good deal of ad-
vantage of the State.
Mostly the speculative
builder ran up a new
residence just inside the
free-pass area, and then
persuaded a tenant over
the road to shift across,
at the same rent he was
paying, with a pass
thrown in. So there are
now several new houses
occupied inside the free-
pass areas and as many
empty ones outside—
and the railway revenue
has lost instead of
gained.
The criminal law
revision recommended
by Yic. Crown Prosecu-
tor Finlayson and Bar-
rister Woinarski goes a
good way towards the
abolition of the death
sentence. They ad-
vise that, when a j ury
recommends to
mercy, the judge
shall merely “ re-
cord ” sentence
of death ; and,
further, that no
“recorded”
death sentences
shall be carried
out. It will thus
rest almost
wholly with a
jury whether
there shall be a
hanging or not.
At any rate, it
will be open to
the jury to say
that there shall
not be a hang-
ing. But why
not go the. whole
hog, and leave it to the jury to say “guilty ” in the
first, second or third degree, or “not guilty”?
And why go through the mummery of “ recording ”
a death sentence that is never to be carried into
effect ?
A refreshingly broad-minded alderman,
named Howard, of the borough of Paddington
(Sydney), moved the other day to merge the
municipality into the city of Sydney. He pointed
out that Paddington was getting deeper and
deeper in debt, and that the only solution of the
financial problem was union with a Greater Syd-
ney. But the mayor and the remaining aldermen
gasped, and wrathfully demanded why they
should sacrifice their position and authority as
long as they could float a new loan to pay off the
old one. The motion lapsed without a seconder.
Bumble won’t give up his bumblements until the
bailiffs are actually in.
S.M. Herald (14/10/ 05)
The Government considers it unfair to expect that the
Crown is always to have picked land available for settle-
ment, especially under our system of “select early and
often.” Things have reached that stage now that the
Government has a right to look to private landowners to
assist in settling people on the land. The majority of the
immigrants reaching Canada buy private land there at
£4 an acre, and it is only the minority who take up
Government land.
Well, there is one sure way to get private land-
owners to “ assist in settling people on the land,”
and that is to make them do so—by a heavy pro-
gressive land-tax which will render the holding of
big estates unprofitable.
After all, how much does Bent’s barracking
for Australian industry and Australian-made
goods amount to ? Whenßeazley, M.L.A., moved,
in Vic. Assembly lately, that no machinery or
goods for the State service that can be manufac-
tured or produced here should be obtained else-
where, Bent and his Ministers voted against the
proposal, and against every amendment moved
with intent to give an effective preference to local
goods. When McKinnon proposed a preference
in accordance with the settled policy of the State,
Thomas jumped up and moved the adjournment
of the House, thus wiping the motion off the
paper. It was a trick, but Bent’s “statesmanship”
is made up of things like that.
Three more or less Tory M.’sL.A.—
Fair bairn, M‘Gregor, and Bowser—have before
Vic. Assembly a Farriers Bill, to regulate the
practice of horse-shoeing, and to appoint a board
of control, etc. It is doubtless a desirable reform ;
but has anybody yet heard of a Bill to prevent
the passing off of paper-puff as leather in chil-
dren’s shoes ? Or of selling shoddy and mungo as
tweed, or flannelette as genuine all-wool? The
well-being of Fairbairn’s horses seems to be of
much more concern than the well-being of
Victoria’s children.
Sydney Telegraph, which not so long ago
sat at the feet of Bill Lyne when that long person
was dispensing favors to its friends, can’t find a
good word for him to-day. The Telegraph has
just finished a campaign demanding that every-
thingshould be done to remove the opium curse from
Australia. Lyne has done something practical to
meet the Telegraph’s demand ; he has gazetted a
regulation providing that all the cheap smuggled
opium found on the unobtrusive and elusive Chow
is to be confiscated and destroyed. Therefore the
King-street journal complains that Lyne’s action
will mean the loss of a quantity of a good and use
ful drug.

select committees re :

General’s Department.

Refusal of the Pharmacy Board to register Mr. Walter
Trafford.
And members, for their £3OO a year, are expected
not only to attend wearisome, stonewalling
debates in the House, but to give a share of
attention to such maundering trifles. Little
wonder that we mostly seem to get “a certain
sort of person ” as an M.P.
Mr. Henry Lee writes :
In your issue of October sth there appeared an article
“Looking for Land.” The writer says that after five
days “ breathless exertion ” the present Premier of Vic-
toria has discovered a place near Warrnambool known as
the Barren Plains, which he considers an ideal spot
for a parcel of General Booth’s immigrants. Your
correspondent confounds a place long known to residents
of Victoria to be of very little commercial value, with
another very large tract of country situated in the County
of Heytesbury. The plot of land which is selected by the
Government for experimental purposes is fully 30 miles
distant from the area mentioned by your correspondent
of the sth inst., and decidedly of a better quality.
By the same mail The Bulletin received a letter
from another local resident who says that the
writer of “ Looking for Land ” was correct, and
that it is the muddy hopeless area he referred to
(and not the better area that Mr. Henry Lee refers
to) on which Bent proposed to settle immigrants.
Also, by the way, ‘ ‘ Looking for Land ” was con-
tributed by a man who has spent almost his whole
life in the district.
“San Remo”: Be par. (B. 12/10/’O5)
about Leahy’s demand for a division in Queens-
land Assembly. Here is the incident in full:
Kenna’s Bill was drifting along. Very few had
anything to say for it, and no one spoke against
it. Apparently the House understood it would
be a unanimous vote in favor. Speaker Cowley
woke up suddenly and put the question, “All in
favor of this measure will say,” &c. There was
dead silence, broken only by the snores of members
who were leaving it for the other fellow to say
“Aye.” “ All against the measure,” &c. Then
“ Janny ” Leahy took in the humor of tlie.situa-
tion and shouted “No.” His was the only voice.
“The Noes have it,” called the sleepy Speaker,
and was proceeding with the next business (more
sleep). Just then, the House awoke with a start
to realise that “Janny” was outvoting all the
rest of the House, and “Janny” himself, terrified
at the position, demanded a division and fled for
his life.
“ Cassandra ” lets down his back hair in
the far West and prophesies :
The Westralian Labor party picks up the gloves for the
hottest fight in its history. An unequal fight, which can
have only one issue. The dissolution has been sprung
upon the party just when public opinion is loudest
against it, and while it is divided against itself, unpre-
pared and disorganised. The party goes out with 22
members; if it struggles back with 16 it will achieve
wonders. The Collie seat will not be won again. Three
Fremantle seats go down the wind. Northam takes a
long farewell of its Labor member. Labor member
Keyser, of Albany, announces that he will stand as an
Independent—he will probably have to remain standing
in any case. Kalgoorlie is doubtful; North Perth is
hopeless ; Coolgardie hangs in the balance. All these are
at present Labor seats. The only non-Labor seat that
can be won is Boulder, which “ Bomb ” Hopkins quits to
try his fortune on the coast. A big effort will be made to
wrest Perth and East Perth from the Rason grip, and
there is some slight prospect of success in the first case.
The Eastern Goldfields seats, with the exception of Kal-
goorlie, are safe enough, and so are the Murchison seats ;
but elsewhere is written in large letters —“ Ichabod."
But the Labor party hasn’t abandoned hope—not being
built that way. It can be relied upon to put up a sturdy
fight, even on the losing side ; and though it cannot hut
emerge from the melee with diminished numbers, it will
probably be a stronger party in other respects.

...ACTION v. AIR-
PERFORMANCE v. PROMISES
On October 4th, 1905, a deputation of local manufacturers of
harvesters waited upon the Minister of Customs, and intimated that
if a prohibitive tariff were declared upon harvesters they would
reduce the prices then ruling by £5 the first year, and another £5
the second year. They were, however, so far as can be learned from
press reports, suspiciously silent about what the third, fourth, and
subsequent years were to bring forth re harvester prices, and those
who follow that good old rule—“judge the future by the past ”
will mistrust that should the manufacturers succeed in their designs
to prohibit harvester importations, they will find some way to get
back the £5 and £lO cut promised for a limited time only. It is
well known that they, like all other sellers have in the past, always
got ALL THEY COULD, hence these indefinite promises look
strangely like “ HOT AIR.”
On October 7th, the harvester buyers of Australia got ACTION,
—they got performance instead of promises—they got something
substantial NOW instead of vague illusory promises to be realised
on in the dim future; for on that date we made an open
quotation on our harvesters as follows:
£7O cash at starting.
£73 March Ist, 1906.
£77 half March Ist, 1906 and half March Ist, 1907.
£BO quarter cash and quarter each March Ist, 1906, 1907, 1908.
£B5 third each March Ist, 1906, 1907, 1908.
This is a cut of £l2 10 0 per harvester, averaging cash and
terms prices together. There will be about 4,000 harvesters sold
in Australia this season, and hence our action obviously saves the
farmers approximately £50,000.
We shall settle with the hundreds of purchasers whose orders
we now hold at the reduced prices quoted above, believing that
those Avho entrusted us with early orders should be loyally protected,
although we must lose thousands of pounds in so doing.
