SPORTING NOTIONS.
The disqualification of Emir for carrying short
weight in the Eclipse Stakes at Caulfield, last week, raises
the same old question as to what ought to be done for the
protection of the outside public in these cases where they
stand to lose their money for certain Jpy backing the horse
that comes in first. The best safeguard would be to delay
the weighing-out of jockeys until close upon the time
when the horses have to leave the “ birdcage,” but a
thumping big penalty for the delinquents might serve
well enough. In the Emir case it appears that the lead-
bag with which jockey Ross had duly weighed was hung
across the side rail of the horse’s stall whilst Emir was
being leisurely saddled up, and hanging there it was
strangely forgotten. The horse might have mentioned it
if he had known what he was expected to hump, but
Emir is a sulky animal anyhow, and he made no
sign on the subject. He went out to win the
race, and was disqualified for want of that lead-
bag. Another famous horse, Marmont, brought his
backers to grief in exactly the same way last year.
The lead bag had been overlooked. He started under
false pretences as to the weight, and the stewards, as in
Emir’s case, were satisfied that no fraud was intended.
To the public who back an underweighted horse it makes
no difference whether the omission of the lead bag from
the winner’s burden was intended, or otherwise. To the
owner, jockey, trainer, and their particular friends it
would make a vast difference if they backed the gee that
came in second and got the race, with a knowledge of the
fact that the popular favorite was not weighted up to re-
quirements, The first time a bookmaker’s horse, trained
in a stable of dubious character, happens to win a race
whilst its lead-bag Is hanging over the rail of its stall, the
stewards will probably introduce a bye-law for the chastise-
ment of the delinquent parties. Surely it is more than
one person’s business to see that an Emir or a Marmont
travels with all its luggage.
* * * *
The Caulfield .Cup was a repetition of last year’s
result as regards long-priced horses. The outsiders had
nothing to do with the finish, the four placed ones—
Marvel Loch, Warroo, Torah, and Emir —being quoted at
10, 14, 4, and 12 to 3, respectively. Of these, Warroo
would have been more fancied but for the series of small
troubles debited aga’nst him in his preparation for the
race. Among the favorite-, the unluckiest were Boabdil,
who got considerably squeezed and bumped, and Emir,
who had always a bad place until he got clear of the
beaten horses approaching the turn, and was subsequently
interfered with in the little scrimmage that brought
Scot Free to grief. But, taken as a whole, the Oup
seemed to be a more truly-run race than would
ordinarily result from a field of 31 on the Caul-
field course. The finish from the turn homewards was a
procession headed by Marvel Loch. The mare’s nearest
followers were Warroo and Torah, the Adelaide youngster
losing a little ground towards the finish, whilst Emir
was the only one of the back lot to make a noticeable
show in pursuit of the leaders. From the head of Marvel
Loch to the tail of Emir (placed fourth) there was a
distance of ten lengths or thereabouts, and the race had
ceased to be exciting before the vvinnei- passed the post.
By a strange coincidence the four places were divided
between two horses that started close to the rails and
two that started from the outside of the field. Marvel
Loch and Warroo had the inside places. The warmest
admirers of the speedy Sydney mare must admit that she
probably covered less ground than anything else in the
race. Nowadays it is a great advantage to get away
close to the rails for a Caulfield Cup. The tradition of
two shocking wholesale accidents as the result of crowd-
ing in at the turns has made the boys somewhat wary of
the risk. The field makes a wider sweep than of yore,
and the luckily-situated jockeys profit by the cautious-
ness of the others.
- * * *
Comparing the Caulfield Cup result with recent
form in w.f.a. races, one is forced to the conclusion that
the main body of ordinary handicap horses running just
now—and especially the Victorians, bar Gladsome—are a
very poor lot. In the October Stakes, at Flemington,
nearly three weeks ago, Torah ran a respectable second to
Gladsome, the others “ nowhere.” A week later Torah
and Marvel Loch beat Gladsome. Again, in the Eclipse
Stakes, Emir (disqualified). Marvel Loch and Boabdil
filled the places, with Warroo fourth. And-in the Caul-
field Cup it was the w.f.a. performers that showed up—-
Marvel Loch, Warroo, Torah and Emir getting right away
from the mob. Boabdil, perhaps, had a rough passage,
and Maniopoto was clearly suffering from the effects of a
bad cold. These two are pretty certain to do better in the
Melbourne Cup, if they thrive meanwhile, but for most
of the others there is no apparent ghost of a hope.
Maniopoto, indeed, has the best of Melbourne Cup chances
“on paper.” His weight is only 8.10, including the
penalty for winning the Metropolitan— i.e., he will have
to give Warroo only 91b. for beating him by eight lengths
at Sydney. The handsomest horse in the Caulfield Cup
field, the Maorilander was sweating profusely as soon as
he went out, like a convalescent, but on returning to the
“ birdcage ”he looked all the better for his gallop. If he
can be fully prepared for the two-mile race, as presum-
ably he can, and if there is no doubt about his stamina,
what is to beat Maniopoto in the Melbourne Cup ? Marvel
Loch and Warroo were at his heels when they turned into
the straight at Randwick for the Metropolitan. Six
seconds later he was winning by the leng th of the pro-
verbial street in record time. And. now the Oamlfield
Cup running of Marvel Loch and Warroo points to Manio-
poto as a good thing for the Melbourne Cup, in which he
will carry 111 b. less than, his weight last Saturday.
* * * *
Marvel Loch’s time has only been twice beaten
in the Caulfield Cup records, but it was 3j-sec. slower than
Maniopoto’s great gallop for the Metropolitan.
Marvel Loch is another argument in favor of
allowing horses to season before they are raced. She
was an advanced three-year-old when she made her first
appearance in public—and won.
Torah, thus far, has run 12 times, and has only
once been unplaced. So the natty little South Australian
three-year-old must be given a chance for the Melbourne
Cup, although he didn’t shape like a stayer in the Caul-
field event.
* * * *
Two stiff nobblers of brandy, in small lemonade
bottles, were administered to Emir before he went out
for the Caulfield Cup. The brute took the liquor un-
graciously, but seemed quite willing to eat one of the
bottles.
* * * *
Colman, a Melbourne bookmaker, who is at
loggerheads with the V.R,C., has commenced a law action
to test the club’s power to charge fees or impose any
conditions whatsoever on bookmakers. Under ordinary
circumstances the case would not come on for hearing
till after the running of the Cup ; but Colman has per-
suaded the Law to get a move on, in the hope that the
V.R.C. may be prohibited from charging fees at the
November meeting. The Chief Justice will hear argu-
ment in an application for an interim injunction against
the V.R.C., and on the result it depends whether Colman
will howl the odds at the Cup, or whether he will have to
go away into the middle of a vacant selection and bellow
at himself for practice. If the club goes dow'n, then it
has no right to prevent anyone calling the odds, and the
bookmakers will be cnished by unlimited competition,
and that will be a lot worse than the tyranny of the power
that grants them their monopoly. And then, when
everybody puts on a bag and yells, the position will be so
dreadful that “ hasty legislation ” will be introduced to
cope with the evil, and in the haste and anger the book-
maker may be killed altogether.
* ♦ * ♦
“F.C.” : There was a distinct tip about the
name Torah —but it was of the wrong sort; it was a hint
to leave the prad alone. Torah—pronounced Towrah—is
a Jewish term meaning a sacred ordinance or command.
The day following the Cup was Simchas Torah —a
festival of rejoicing—and when Torah, who had been a
short-priced favorite for weeks, toiled home five lengths
behind Marvel Loch, Jewry rejoiced exceedingly. A horse
whose name suggests even remotely that Hebrewdom is
going to rejoice seems a good steed to neglect.
* * * *
Last week Wilkins, a member for the Tote
electorate in the Vic. Assembly, moved the adjourn-
ment to discuss the position of the V.R.C. Wilkins
professed himself “at a loss to understand why the
Government should permit a huge gambling business
to be conducted on the Flemington racecourse.”
The Chief Sec. explained that “ the power to register
bookmakers was included in the V.R.C. by-laws
20 years ago, and was intended as a protection to the
public. Asa matter of law, any person could make a bet
on a racecourse. It was only made illegal by statute to
bet in certain places.” In short, the Law has the
honesty to admit that horse-racing and betting must be
co-existent, and it makes a pretence of endeavoring 4 to
confine race-gamblers to the actual course, where the
issue of their gamble is decided. Here is a statement of
fact amounting to an irresistible argument in favor of a
legalised totalisator under proper control. If the V.R.C.
was formerly granted the power to license bookmakers
for the protection of-the public, the club should now be
permitted to ensure further protection and fairer play for
the public by substituting a silent, decent tote for that
mostly abominable person, I key Mo. The public in
general, always getting the worst of the odds and being
victimised by the roguery attaching to “ market ” con-
siderations, is anxious for the tote. The V.R.C., living
on public patronage—not upon the entry fees and license
fees of cronk horse-owners and fatted bookmakers (one
and the same thing- in many cases) —is also desirous of the
legalised tote. Yet the Vic. Parliament illogically blocks
the way to this reform.
* * * *
The opening clay of the third round of Sydney
first-grade cricket was not remarkable for its batting.
Only one century appeared, and that was what the classic
Greek poet Pindar called a “bonzerina,” A. B. S. White, of
North Sydney, carrying his bat out for 198. The afternoon
was fine though cloudy, but the ground was soft from
rain that had fallen during- the week. The next nearest
approach to a century was the 90 which Mackay, of Bur-
wood, put up between the time that his side got Gordon
out for 130 and the drawing of stumps. It was a beautiful
exhibition of cricket, for he hit them all round the wicket
and never gave a chance. No one else did anything
with the willow worth whooping about. Bowling all
round was good. Hill, of Waverley, took the loaf,
knocking- down five wickets for 37 runs; Middleton,
of Sydney, took five of Balmain for 49 ; Cronan, of Central
Cumberland, g-ot away with four for 42 ; Fisher, of the
Uni., knocked down three for 30; and Garnsey, of Bur-
wood, shivered seven of Gordon’s for 72.
* * * *
North Sydney faced its recently severed limb,
Middle Harbor, and made the score of the day, 357.
Balmain only managed to put up 127 against Sydney, the
side - including no less than four poultry-farmers, and
three others who only escaped the same profession by the
skin of their teeth. Glebe and Central Cumberland went
in for quick cricket, the butchers disappearing with 150
to their credit. Central Cumberland rushed to the sticks,
and at sunset had piled up 93 for fou'r w-ickets. Waverley
got Paddington all out for 152, and had made 35 for a loss
of two wickets at the draw’ng of stumps. Redfern left
210 on the board, and : the University showed 31 for the
first wicket. Gordon only made a humble 130 against
Burwood, which put up 151 for the loss of four wickets.
* * *
Hill, the young Waverley (N.SW.) bowler, fell on
the Paddington batsmen like a bomb on a Russian grand
duke. He got his first two wickets before a single run
was scored off him, and when he took his third only four
had been scored. Finally, he had five for 37. This is his
second appearance in first grade cricket, and practically
his first opportunity. He is a left-hander, a “fast
medium,” and breaks from either left or right at will.
He graduated from Sydney Grammar School.
I ‘ ,* * * *
Adelaide has had to drop strictly electorate
cricket, one of the suburban constituencies being alto-
gether too -big; but the district principle will be still
adhered to. On the other hand, East and West Adelaide,
the two halves of the city proper, have been allowed to
amalgamate, making one strong club. It won its first
match by one run against North Adelaide. East Torrens
lost on the same day to the Port by one wicket. Joe
Travers made top score for Adelaide, besides taking four
wickets.
*•* * *
The three State associations have decided to
abolish the gilt trinkets hitherto given each year to the
boss team in the Sheffield Shield contests, except in the
case, of the player .who has not already received one to
hang, outside his .waistcoat. Which won’t g-rieve the soul
of the present day flannelled fool much—the f.f. is still
wise enough to prefer the “brass” to the bauble. The
spirit of the age !
Victorian Cricket Association is going to allow
electorate cricket to slide for another season, and the
amalgamation of League and Association teams will be
given an opportunity of bettering cricket in the Lent
State. The influence of the gilded Melbourne 0.0. has
much to do with retarding the -introduction of the elec-
torate system ; and the Vic. Association and clubs generally
bow the knee to the M.0.C., because, in the dim long
ago, the M.C.O. was the mouthpiece of Australia with the
Mar.vlebone Cricket Club in the matter of the visits of
English teams to Australia.
* * * *
Joe Darling, captain of the late lamented Aus-
tralian Ashes Syndicate, arrived with a big brush and a
large bucket of whitewash amongst his luggage, .and pro-
ceeded to splash vigorously around directly he touched
Australia. After some large dabs of puerile excuses for
Test-match ignominies, which made every cat in the
country writhe with merriment, he decorated his crowd •
individually and collectively with three thick coats from
the aforesaid bucket, until the rankest failures of them
all assumed the aspect of brilliant successes. A query as
to the finances.touclied the canny Joe on his strong point,
and he replied warily that probably other tours had
panned out more successfully. No information is available
as to the number w'ho went “ on terms,” and the conse-
quent fattening of the shares of the big guns.
* * ♦ *
Stated at tlie Victorian Cricket Association's
meeting-, t’other night, that the Australian XI tour will
pan out at £9OO a man, and that each of the “ tests ” gave
a profit of £75 to every Australian player. But Captain
Darling denies the figures.
* * * *
Harry Trott and one-time brilliant left-hander
Bruce willbe missing from Victorian cricket during the com-
ing season. They have decided to retire instead of waiting
for forcible ejection. Young left-hander Vernon Ransford
(who batted ably in the Vic. State team last year) made a
good start for Melbourne, t’other day, with a fine innings
of 35 (on a bad wicket); and another promising batsman
named Christian, who took the premier average in the
East Melbourne Club last season, knocked up 94 in 90
minutes. Altogether, it seems possible that the Victorian
representative team for the coming season will have many
new faces in it.
* * * *
The new method of allotting the points in
Sydney first grade cricket will work out badly in one
respect. In order to get a three-point win clubs will, if
last season’s mammoth scoring be repeated, “declare”
immediately the star batsmen have finished, and the tail,
which most wants practice, will get mighty little of it,
and be used for fielding only.
Re Charles Tudor Bird, recently found dead
near Ararat (Vic.). For the information of numerous
inquirers as to whether “Charles Tudor’’was identical
with “ Albert Edward ” Bird, the English ped. whom
Coppin imported to Melbourne in 1869, The Bulletin is
now prepared to vow and declare that he wasn’t. Up to
last week the flying Bird of yore could be seen in Mel-
bourne as usual, and he didn’t look likely to be a dead
Bird yet awhile. The old ped. is nothing of a flier
nowadays. He just potters about, a quaint bent figure
with the assistance of two stout walking- sticks. Makes
himself useful to the stall-holders at Victoria Market as a
watchman, and when not on duty at the market his
principal dissipation is to stand around in Bourke-street
and exchange greetings w-ith members of the “ sporting ”
fraternity who knew him personally in his younger days,
or are acquainted with his history.
* * * *
The Law has advanced a step further in the
gambling-suppression maze, through Victorian Justice
Hood’s decision that Calcutta sweeps are a game of chance,
and therefore illegal. In a Calcutta sweep the names of
horses or dogs (it seems to be mostly drawn about alleged
dogs) are drawn against the names of subscribers. The
chances of each dog are then put up to auction, anyone
being allowed to buy and the holder of the dog’s ticket being
allowed to buy in ; half this money goes into the prize
fund, half to the ticket holder. His Honor held that the
success of the original competitors depended entirely
upon chance, and that, if anyone purchased'a ticket he
fancied, the chanOe of winning was already attached to
that ticket, and could not be dissociated therefrom by
the riiere act of subsequent purchase. Conviction upheld
iyith costs, so there should be a considerable slump in
Calcutta sweeps, especially in country hotels, when the
coursing season comes round again.
The performances of Australian woman swimmer
Annette Kellerrnann have been attracting the attention
of Uncle Sam’s daughters, and a shoal of invitations to
come to Amurka and be licked have been forwarded to
the nut brown mermaid. The U.S.A. pins its faith to
Elaine Golding, spinster, of Bath Beach, New York State,
who is credited with having downed all who have met her.
* * * *
N.S W. cycling season has hardly got a proper
start as yet, and already there are odors of fish and cronk
practices rising all over the country. The League of
Wheelmen has discussed the idea of appointing a stipendi-
ary steward to take charge of all meetings at which prizes
of over £25 are competed for, but decided that it could
not afford the salary of a suitable man at-present. Any-
how, a man who knows all the rider's, as such an official
might have a reasonable chance of doing, seems to be
the only one who could deal with the swindlers at
present “ doing ” the country leagues by racing under
assumed names, getting light handicaps, and sweeping
the board. Cycle-racing under an assumed name is now
one of the thriving industries of the country.
*** ‘ *
Racing cyclists are beginning to wonder why it
is the foreign rider is usually a better sprinter than the
Australian. The explanation-seems to be that the rest of
the world trains for speed, and the Australian riders train
for stamina ; and the question is whether it is possible to
have both. The Australian riders have not had the
advantage of fast tracks like the Americans and Euro-
peans, and probably the appropriation of the world’s
sprinting championship by an Australian will eventuate a
couple of seasons after the laying down of a really fast
cycling track.
N.S.W. Rowing Association pulled an awfully
wry face before it decided to send a crew to represent the
State in the eight-oar championship set down for decision
at Perth. The N.S.W. Association loses sight of the fact
that the Westralian crew journeyed to Sydney, and
plugged along in the championship event; and Westralia
is a flea-bite compared with N.S.W. in rowing affairs
generally. Tasmania, Victoria and Queensland will also
be represented in the event.
* * » * .
D. G. Soutar, professional teacher of golf to the
fashionable persons who haunt the Bondi links, has won
the professional championship and purse of £2O put up by
the Melbourne Club. Soutar arrived in these parts some
three seasons ago, and joined a local suburban club as an
amateur. In that capacity he showed that he was a
champion, and the Royal Club offered him a comfortable
billet, which he, as a canny Scot, accepted.
» * * »
Professional ped. M. Bnxton (who, it is stated,
came from Yankeeland with Duffey) bumped against a
dark-hued person named Anderson in the final of the
Charters Towers (Q.) “ hundred,” the other night, and
went down heavily. The Yankee was given out as an
absolute “ moral,” but the bubble burst, and Anderson
took the £BO, leaving a tenner for the “good thing.”
Bananaland’s champion, Postle, was a couple, of inches
away third. Professional athletics are booming in North
Queensland at present.
* * » *
Australian axeman E. R. Terry is finding the
championship honor a huge burden ; in a recent chopping
contest at Charters T. (Q.), he was handicapped at 140 sec.
behind scratch, and even then was only just beaten.
Stevenson (the winner) had 135 sec. start of Terry, and got
through the log in 3min. 31sec. The champion’s effort is
only tearing a fine athlete to pieces in a branch of sport
which isn’t worth raving about.
* * » •
Unquestionably the best exhibition of rifle-
shooting ever put up at N.S.W. Rifle Association’s meet-
ings was the battle between Motton, of Charters Towers
(Q.), and Edwards, of Sydney, in “The King’s,” which
ended in a tie. A deciding trial of three shots at
1000yds. range was shot off, and again there was a tie ;
then Motton notched a “bull’s-eye” and Edwards only
put up an “inner,” leaving the Bananalander to scoop the
£3O first prize and taking £2O himself. There was a
general all-round excellence, too, in the plugging, at
the slippery “running-man” target in the “Finn”—a
kharki-colored “dummy,” which travels 100 yd. in 60sec.,
in which time the rifle-man must fire eight shots. The
abnormal number of shootists who succeeded in hitting'
the bull’s-eye (fixed somewhere on a ievel with the heart)
gave perhaps the best illustration of the military value of
rifle-shooting. » * *
Adelaide lawn-tennis association has taken the
revolutionary step of having all its club matches played
on one central ground, the Jubilee Oval, and on grass.
Championship and inter-State matches have been played
there for years, all the clubs meanwhile being at the dis-
advantage of practising on asphalt. The first rbund of
games under the new system saw all the leading clubs
defeated, and the season ought to be interesting. The
chief want is good juniors. S.A.’s best men are mostly
about 30, and some of them well beyond that.
* * * *
Ex-Premier Waddell’s snort in the N.S.W.
Assembly on the brutality of professional bruising may
have been 1 sincere enough—his legs seemed to tremble
with horror while he read the Star’s report of the Turner-
Whitelaw fight -but it was quite ridiculous. The element
of suspicion lies in the fact that Waddell, whilst Premier,
made no attempt to abolish the alleged brutal exhibitions.
Professional bruising in Sydney is seldom anything ap-
proaching brutal. Mostly it is humorous ; sometimes
just dreary. That Waddell should have picked the
Turner-Whitelaw scrap on which to hang his indictment
is curious—it was a very milk-and-water affair. Only
Waddell’s joss knows what sort of frenzy the ex-Minister
would have worked up had he been present at the M’Oall-
Murphy scrap for the championship ; probably the spectacle
would have brought on a fainting fit. And yet McCall
will probably live longer than Waddell. The pug. is given
to longevity. What Waddell forgets is that, when a man
has had enough, he can stop the fight by giving in. If
Waddell wants to attack brutality let him go for pigeon-
shooting and coursing. These are brutal pastimes, and
depend on their cruelty for their attractiveness. The
most pitiful sound writer ever heard is the agonised
scream of the hare as the teeth close on it. And the
horrible part of coursing, as it is practised in Sydney, is
that the hare that escapes to-day is kept to be hunted the
next day, and over and over again, till it is at last killed.
This is the refinement of cruelty and drawn-put torture.
Then consider the brutality of pigeon-shooting ; there are
few more repulsive sights than that of cramped little
pigeons—dozen after dozen of them—being blown to
pieces by whisky-soaked hogs at 20 odd yards. These
coarse savages are, with few exceptions, quite useless
with the rifle, and, in the event of an invasion, would be
conspicuous in the rush for the first train to the moun-
tains. Pigeon-shooting does not make good rifle shots,
neither does’it make strong muscles. It merely panders
to the brutality which characterised the degenerate
Roman mob that used to howl for the blood of gladiators,
while it feared to shed its own perspiration. When we
are governed scientifically any degenerate found taking
pleasure in the death agony of inferior creatures will be
exterminated as utterly depraved and likely to breed
murderers.
« * * *
The Turner-Whitelaw meeting, which roused
the shocked indignation of ex-Premier Waddell, hap-
pened at the Sydney National Sporting Club last week,
and was a fairly vigorous mill. Both worked at
a tremendous rate. The blows rained so fast that it
was impossible to see them, but nothing terrible hap-
pened, except the roaring of the audience, which couldn’t
have made more noise had it been beaten itself. It wasn’t
a boxing match—it was a whirly-whirly ; and it left
Turner pumped and done. He wasn’t knocked out, but
pumped out. His boiler had burst, and he couldn’t get
out of the way of the blows which Whitelaw kept drop-
ping on him, so the referee stopped the fight with a
verdict for Whitelaw 7 . It was a wise and just decision.
Curious how the crowd loves a whirling exhibition.
« * « *
Bob Fraser, the Melbourne middleweight, and
Dan Hiam, a plentifully-tattooed mariner from Sydney-
side, gave an illustration of the horridness of the boxing
game at Mick Nathan’s hall, Melbourne, the other Monday
night. The fight—which was a nice, clean, earnest mill—-
went the whole 20 rounds, and although Hiam was down
several times considering things for seven to nine seconds,
and went to his corner more than once with a sort of
herring-bone blind stagger, he finished with only a swelled
eye and that tired feeling. Fraser came through without
a scratch. The latter could certainly have given his
opponent a bad time had he gone at him with the man-
eating vehemence that timid politicians believe to be the
customary moral attitude of the boxer ; as it was, he
finished with plenty of points to spare, and was declared
winner by referee Dan Creedon. This was so sportsman-
like a battle, that the .voice of the referee was not heard
till he was called upon to place the competitors.
* « * *
Wilkes’ defeat by Quigley in the return fight, at
Sydney Gaiety, was fully expected. Quigley beat him in
the first disturbance ; moreover, Wilkes is depressed in
spirit by being mistaken for the Foreign-trade person
who misrepresents Dailey in the Commonwealth House
of Reps. Now Wilks, the member, does all his fighting
with his mouth, round the walls of Derry and other re-
mote places ; Wilkes, the bruiser, is no relation, and bar-
rackers will please remember the fact, otherwise Wilkes
will have to change his name.
Tim Murphy now signs himself Champion of
Australia, Africa and Hawaii. Sounds like the preamble
of one of Nero Caesar’s Early Closing Acts.
* -r * *
The small boy’s cigarette-card mania about
reached its limit, the other day, when the Bendigo (Vic.)
police arrested an infant of 11, who had broken into a
storekeeper’s premises for- the-sole purpose-of burglarising
cigarette-cards, 50 of which were found on him. Nothing
else of the tempting- stock was touched.
“ H.”: The fight for the championship of
Australia took place recently in a Sydney dentist’s
room. X. is a brilliant middle-weight, with a
dirty but fertile left —his crop of knock-outs has
astonished pugdoin. He asked me to accompany.
him while he was being separated from two aching
teeth. The dentist, a" pale, ladylike little man,
invited us into his plush-padded den, and waved the
champion to the velvet throne. The champion obeyed
nervously. “ Take it easy; sir,” he said, faintly. “ I ain’t
used to this sort of thing. I don’t mind a bash on the
dial, but I can’t stand them steel nippers. For Gawd’s
sake don’t lance me face.” He was like a man sitting on
the trap of a gallows, until the little dentist caught him
tenderly by the jaw, swung back his head, 'and' plunged
the forceps into the middle of a swear word. A struggle
followed, but the ladylike dentist had a wrist of iron.
Holding back the bullet head, he wrenched out a tooth
that left a hole in the pug’s best smile. The champion
stood up wrathfully. “If yer hurt me like that again,”
he shouted, “ I’ll breathe on yer with both hands.” The
little dentist patted him soothingly and drew him back to
the chair, and when the man of many fights raised his fist
threateningly he received a wicked stab in the gum.
After being poked with a shark-nosed forceps, the pug.,
while the steel was in his mouth, referred to dentists as a
gang of slaughtermen and hogs. Then the toothsmith
got a hammerloek on the eye-tooth, and it parted from
the pug. like a cow’s foot from a bog. I took the cham-
pion home in a cab. “ These fighting men are always the
same,” said the dentist afterwards.

THE AUSTRALIAN DRUG CTSYDNE'
Certain individuals are urging that Aus-
tralia should subsidise a British-owned line of
steamers trading direct hence to Hull, in York-
shire —the English county where the woollen
manufactures are. Surely Australia pays too much
already for dragging its raw material (dirt and all)
half over the globe and back to it again as the
manufactured article. Let us establish our woollen
factories here with the aid of a stiff tariff,
and not fool about paying subsidies to other
people to make our clothes. It is much cheaper
to subsidise our own woollen mills than somebody
else’s ships.
That Japanese person, Kurachichi Succi,
who was charged in Melbourne with keeping a
disorderly house in which were three white
women, “all young and still showing traces of for-
mer beauty,” proved to be the possessor of a
passport, dated 1903, and signed by the Japanese
Consul in Melbourne and by a Japanese official in
Yokohama. Presumably, therefore, he passed as
a student, tourist or trader. Prime Minister
Deakin is arranging with Japan for an extension of
the passport system. It seems as if he should
first make inquiry as to how the present unex-
tended system has been used.
Douglas Wadclell, the recalcitrant N.S.W.
bankrupt, who has resided in the debtors’ prison
in Sydney since last April, has again refused to be
civil and answer the question put to him by the
Court officials. His frank and unassuming ob-
stinacy begins to make the Law uneasy. The
Law has not laid down for how long a contu-
macious bankrupt is to be detained in gaol, and
there is an uncomfortable feeling that he can’t be
chained up for the rest of his natural life, else
some day he will be raked up as a martyr, and a
general election will turn on him.
A tired man from the press gallery
The all-night sittings of N.S.W. Assembly in the reign
of Carnithers are miserably uninteresting - . There is talk,
of course, but it is unemotional and uninsi iring. It is
nag more than speech, and when a member attacks
another he uses flat, common abuse that is its own refu-
tation. During the all-night liquor debate Wade was
tricky, Hogue perky, and the Opposition stodgily gabble-
some ; but there was no ebullition, no spark to denote
fire, no play of fancy, no outburst of passion—nothing hut
stodgy men carping, and all anxious to be considered
well-behaved. A house of men like Parkes, Jennings,
Copeland, Garvan, N ini an Melville and Angus Cameron
sitting up all night over a Liquor Bill, would not have
been well-behaved, but it would lmve been in some danger
of being interesting.

THE LAST OF SIX


According to the cable the Marquis of
Ripon wants to know, apropos the proposed
Colonial Conference, “ what would happen if the
redoubtable Mr. Seddon was found dancing on the
stomach of the Government on the subject of
Chinese labor ? ” It would depend how long he
had been dancing on the stomach before he was
“ found.” If he had been on the stomach for any
length of time it would be a manslaughter case.
Fine points of law have been cropping up
in Adelaide, The coroner occupied himself during
the retirement of the jury in a suspicious case by
giving a witness two months for prevarication ;
but the sentence was remitted as soon as the
jury came back with a verdict which resulted
in the committal of the witness on another
charge. Judge Gordon has been remand-
ing two convicted prisoners from day to' day
to see if they would reveal the whereabouts of
stolen property —one of them being released under
the First Offenders’ Act when he had finished
revealing. A man in prison for obtaining goods
by false pretences was brought up to answer
a further charge of forging the document by
which he obtained the goods. Paris Nesbit, K.C.,
defending him, pleaded the old technical “autre-
fois convict,” and then proposed to call as a
witness Judge Homburg, who sentenced the
prisoner. Judge Gordon refused to allow this,
and a violent constitutional question about the
right to call a Judge as a witness, and about the
pomp and dignity of the bench generally, 'broke
loose and rushed like an excited dog all over the
premises.
A Vic. correspondent : —
Vic. railways have again extended the “lengths” of
line to be kept in repair by each gang of repairers. This
is the second increase in about two years. Originally,
the “lengths” were about five miles. The first addition
made them seven. Now they are 10. Four men now consti-
tute a gang, instead of three, however. But white-
collared, white-cuffed pen-officers, who never “packed”
a sleeper or drove a dog-spike in their lives, are piling a
lot of useless work on to the gangs —“ tittivating ” the
cuttings and banks, “chipping” fancy desigrns about the
earthworks, and doing “extra” jobs which formerly
“ special ” gangs were employed at. Meanwhile, speed
of trains is increasing, and locomotives and trains are
becoming heavier. Some early morning, cabs will be seen
wildly rushing to and from the Melbourne daily news-
paper offices. They will be collecting the entire report-
ing strength to go out and “ do ” one of the biggest things
in railway accidents yet known in Victoria,

From a new book, the “ Soul of London
In general, the Londoner has lost all power of connected
conversation, and almost all power of connected thought.
. . . As a general rule, London limits itself to : “ Did you
see that extraordinary case in the So-and-so to-day?”
. . . “ Have you read Such-and-sudh a novel ? Seen such
a play ? Or such a picture show ? ” and it comments,
“ Rotten, I think.”

“ Messrs. Freeman and Wallace —
«/ have been under your treatment for some considerable time for the cure of a
General Weakened Condition closely allied to Debility. For some time previous to my

accompanied by my photograph, both of which you are at liberty to publish in the inte-
rests of your profession. I think your skill in the treatment of this form of disease is

Faithfully yours,
Crookwell, N.S.W., October 12th, ’05.” G. E. VIDLER.

The Presbyterian Messenger, on behalf
of one of the churches at any rate, puts its foot
down heavily on the Stinking Fish party. In its
October number it says :
Our people are not opposed to immigration, and neither
by law nor in public meeting have they given utterance
to any such folly. We are strongly opposed to an influx
of pauper immigration such as would create in the cities
of this land an East End of sweating abominations ; but
we are ready to extend the most cordial welcome to
persons whose experience and resources constitute them
a real gain to any country. We insist that Britain is rich
enough to maintain her own paupers ; and we declare
that further Asiatic influx is not for the benefit of the
community, either morally or financially, hut outside of
these restrictions the broad acres of Australia are available
to all Europeans who care to cast in their lot with us.
The attendants around the Federal
Legislature can name visitors’ States on sight.
Tasmanian visitors look sleepy, and seem as
if their thoughts were fixed on potatoes, or on
some similar violent topic. South Australians are
gaunt and dismal, with a decided Wesleyan aspect
when regarded from the south-east. Queens-
landers are mostly vigorous fellows—men with the
vim of the tropics in their veins. The New South
Welsher is a distinct type. Any stranger who
talks in a domineering, self-important fashion is
indexed as a Sydneyite who has been struck by
the inferiority of Melbourne. And the index
doesn’t err more than once in a hundred times
either. Next to the patronising party from the
Smother State, visitors from Maoriland are re-
garded by the door-keepers with most dislike.
The older attendants always plant when they see
a fragment of greenstone coming up the steps.
Such visitors invariably descant on the glories of
Mr. Seddon, and leave a broad trail of national
self-satisfaction all over the premises. But there
is this to be said for them—there are no Stinking
Fishermen among them. Not one of them presents
the slightest resemblance to Preacher Fitcliett.
The Maorilander waves his hand proudly, and
states that his country is “pie,” and that it is
going to remain so.

Paris is the City of New Ideas. The French
spectators chafed at the sig'ht of the limit men in handi-
cap races riding slowly round the track and saving all
their energies for the last tremendous burst, so the pro-
moters ruled that every man not on scratch who is passed
by the scratch man before the bell rings, must drop out.
The immediate result of the new rule is that the limit men
go like fiends racing for a drink, and the scratch men
liave-to do likewise to have a hope, and the excitement is
tremendous.

The Voice of Nature.
By “Maks.”
Whenever anyone suffers from rheumatism, gout, neuralgia, lumbago, sciatica, backache, blood
disorders, anaemia, indigestion, jaundice,-biliousness, sick headache* general debility, gravel, stone,
bladder disease, or Bright’s Disease, it is but Nature crying aloud for help and saying,- “Your liver is
out of order ! Your kidneys are not doing their work! Restore them to health, or your suffering
cannot cease.” Nature will not be outraged. So long as the urinary and biliary poisons, which cause
the disorders mentioned, remain in the system you must suffer. When the kidneys and liver are
fulfilling actively their appointed functions the poisons which cause the disorders are expelled in a
natural manner, and suffering ceases. !
The kidneys of the average person filter and extract from the blood about three pints of urine
every day. In this quantity of urine should be dissolved about an ounce of urea, ten or twelve grains
in weight of uric acid, and other animal and mineral matter varying from a third of an ounce to nearly
an ounce. If the', kidneys are working freely and healthily all this solid matter leaves-the body
dissolved in the urine, but if, through weakness or disease, the kidneys are unable to do their work
properly, a quantity of these urinary substances remains in the blood and flows through the veins,
contaminating the whole system. Then we suffer from some form of uric poisoning, such as
Rheumatism, Gout, Lumbago, Backache, Sciatica, Persistent Headache, Neuralgia, Gravel, Stone,
and Bladder Troubles. A simple test to make as to whether the kidneys are healthy is to place some
urine, passed the first thing in the morning, in a covered glass, and let it stand until next morning.
If it is then cloudy, shows a sediment like brick-dust, is of an unnatural color, or has particles floating
about in it, the kidneys are weak or diseased, and steps must immediately be taken to restore their
vigor, or Bright’s Disease, Diabetes, or some of the many manifestations of uric poisoning will result.
The Liver is an automatic chemical laboratory. In the liver various substances are actually made
from the blood. Two or three pounds of bile are thus made by the liver every day. The -liver takes
sugar from the blood, converts it into another form, and stores it up so as to be able to again supply it
to the blood as the latter may require enrichment. The liver changes uric acid, which is insoluble,
into urea, which is completely soluble, and the liver also deals with the blood corpuscles which have
lived their life and are useful no longer. .When the liver is inactive or diseased we suffer from some
form of biliary poisoning, such as Indigestion, Biliousness, Anaemia, Jaundice, Sick Headache, Genera
Debility, and Blood Disorders. 1
So intimate is the relation between the work done by the kidneys and that done by the liver, that
where there is any failure on the part of the kidneys the liver becomes affected in sympathy anti vice
versa. It was the realisation of the importance of this close union of the labor of these vital organs
which resulted in the discovery of the medicine now known throughout the world as Warner’s Safe
Cure. Certain medical men, knowing what a boon it would be to humanity if some medicine could be
found which would act specifically on both the kidneys and liver, devoted themselves to an exhaustive
search, for such a medium, and their devotion was eventually rewarded by their success in com-
pounding a medicine which possesses the required quality in the fullest degree. Warner’s Safe Cure
exhibits a marvellous healing action in all cases of functional or chronic disease of the kidneys and
liver, and restoring them, as it is able to do, to health and activity, it of necessity cures all complaints
due to the retention in the system of urinary and biliary poisons. A vigorous action of the kidneys
and liver naturally eliminates the poisons, and troubles due to the presence of the poisons cease.
Cures effected by Warner’s Safe Cure are permanent, simply because they are natural. [l2

Gambling, Robbery, &c.
“ Homo ” : “ Two Seas,” in adversely criticising
Tatt.’s sweep system (B., 7/9/’06), says “ there is at least
one policeman less in Victoria ” thereby. Well, Victoria
narrowly avoided losing another constable through the
same agency. He drew Sudden Jerk in one of Tatt.’s
Cup sweeps some years ago, and immediately after the
race was run got a wire to say that Sudden Jerk had
won. At the time he was on duty at a suburban street
corner, and had to clutch an adjacent lamp-post for sup-
port. Then, in a dazed condition, he managed to reach
the bar of the Pig and Whistle across the street, so over-
come that all he could do was to hoarsely gasp
“Brandy!” He didn’t drop dead, but for the next few
days he almost wished he had. It turned out he had
been cruelly hoaxed, Sudden Jerk merely chasing the
field in, a bad last. He spent the greater part of the few
pounds he got for a starter in liquid solace for his
wounded feelings.
*****
“Drifter”: About the gambling spirit. I
noticed an excited group of school boys outside a Sydney
public school. A shrill argument was raging, and fearing-
that nose-blood would be shed I went over to arbitrate.
Saw a diminutive youngster with plaintive reluctance
hand over three cigarette cards to another. Then the
game went on. Each boy clutched a handful of the cards
and viewed his neighbor with dark suspicion. They lined
up toeing the kerbstone. At the word “ Go !” each spun
si card across the ,12ft. footpath towards the school wall.
The lucky owner of the one nearest the wall scooped
the pool. They called the game “Cigarette Monte.”
The expression in those kiddies’ eyes would have broken
the heart - of an anti-gambling society.
* * «‘ »
From the annual report of Col. Madley, S.A.
Commissioner of Police “ At one race meeting held in
Adelaide the names of 122 men plying their calling- as
totalisator agents and clerks were supplied to me, but in
no single instance could a conviction have been secured,
because the police are required to prove, among other
things, that these men were working for fee, commission
or reward. Such evidence is not obtainable ; yet it was
evident to. everybody that not one of them was a
philanthropist devoting his time and energy for the
purpose of assisting the public to invest on the totalisator
without the trouble of going to the windows.’’
♦ * * *
“F.”: A letter to Melbourne Age from a self-
satisfied sophist signing himself “Straight Shot” ex-
presses all the dishonesty of all the excuses raised on
behalf of the punting evil in town. He declares that
“ the V.R.O. in the matter of fostering and encouraging-
silver betting has left the tote shops and betting-
clubs in comparative insignificance. To see betting in
sixpences and shillings carried on in its wildest excess one
must visit—hot the city shops or clubs, but the flat and
hill at Flemington, where, at one meeting alone, hun-
dreds of thousands of sixpenny and shilling wagers are
made by people of all ages and both sexes. . . . Wagers
in crowns in the Flemington paddock are more numerous
than in sovereigns, and roaring business is openly done
even in florins and half-crowns with women and children.”
This statement includes a lot of exaggeration in the
figures, and one palpable lie, seeing that women are
very few and children fewer in the paddock at
Flemington, the only small fry that one sees
there being a score or two stable boys of
uncertain age. What is the truth as regards these rival
evils—the racecourse and the town betting-place'? The
racecourse, in itself, is a place of sporting entertainment,
where, as a matter of fact, thousands of people at Cup-
time get nothing but harmless pleasure and fresh air.
Among the thousands of others who gamble, the needy ,
people and reckless fools are vastly outnumbered by the
crowd which takes its losses contentedly and never feels
the gambling-itch of the inveterate punter. Nearly all
of them can well, afford the expense of going to the
races once in a way, or they wouldn’t be there
at all. They would save the expense and punt
in town, if punting were their first consideration.
The vast majority of them attend only the biggest
meetings of the year—many of them ' confine their
interest in racing to the Cup. Thev have no more
sympathy with the average slave of the silver-punt-
ing habit than the casual “ Tatt.” speculator has. Race-
goers of their class are found in France, Germany
and other countries whose Governments vigi-
lantly guard the public against the betting-shop
evil. To represent Flemington, or even a low-class
racecourse, as a snare for sixpenny punters—men, women,
and children—is merely to draw a red herring across the
trail of the unlawful betting shop men. The V.R.C. is
not responsible for the impoverishment of Melbourne’s
poorest and most populous districts. Working men and
women who persistently patronise tote shops, ill-con-
ditioned wasters and sanguine youths in billets who pay
furtive visits to tote agencies and bog - us tobacco shops six
days a week, would not be surely coming to grief if they
got no inducement to bet except on the racecourse.
* * * *
“ A.B.” : Remarks about possible treatment of
future Australian elevens in England (B. 28/8/.’05), should
they be classed as professionals, are timely. The Aus-
tralian who has never been to England has no idea of the
position of the professional athlete in the Cold Country.
A man may rob or embezzle and still retain the esteem of
the public. He may connive at his wife’s frisSiness, and
accept gifts from the co-respondent, and remain in
London society. But the professional athlete,'and all
associated with him, are outcasts, shunned as systemati-
cally as the Italian executioners of the Middle Ages, who
used to live outside the pale in houses painted red. The
professional athlete represents the very lowest caste in
English society. Even such a man as Hackenschmidt
was astonished to find that decent people in Australia
would meet him as a friend. When I visited England a
couple of years back, I met a man on the mailboat—
he wasn’t a very aggravated snob either—who fiercely
contended that novelist H. G. Wells was guilty of gross
presumption in entering the literary profession, seeing-
that his father -was a professional cricketer.
* * *
Motor-bike racing is an exciting business and
full of strange contradictions. Travelling on an ordinary
roadster at anything between 10 and 20 miles an hour a
mail can come to all manner of grief, but when he flies off
a motor-bike travelling at 40 miles an hour, and rebounds
off a picket fence, he mostly gets up unhurt to curse his
luck at having wrecked a va uable machine. Writer was
talking to a Dunlop Tyre man who, while track-racing,
opened out and lifted his speed to over 40 miles an hour,
and running close to the edge of the track, struck the
pickets with his knuckles. For nearly 20.yd. the top of
the picket fence was wrecked, the rider lost all sense of
feeling in his hand, and the machine described half-a-
dozen somersaults in mid-air, and left him on all-fours
on the grass ten' yards away. Same man, riding from
Melbourne to Sydney in a Deoauville car, ran into a
water-way shaped like V. The front wheels struck the
opposite side, the tail end of the car flew up, and his
companion, with his arms spread out as though about to
swim, dived wildly through the atmosphere and disap-
peared. A few minutes later he came running up to see
if the driver, - who was held in the car by the driving wheel,
was dead. He wasn’t. Once H. B. James was. racing
Leyhmann on the Melbourne Exhibition track. James
was on a 2th. p. motor-bike, a mere toy, and Leyhmann
was on a great pacer which soared up to something over
a dozen h.p. James had got the inside running, and to
keep it was his only hope of victory—once that
big, roaring machine got in front on the inside
the race was won. At the last lap Leyhmann
made a supreme effort. He shot past, be cut down
yards in front, but, as he heeled .over and swung his
motor on to the straight, the foot-rest (situated where the
pedal of an ordinary bike is) stuck into the turf at the
edge of the track. The machine sprang skyward, hurled
Leyhmann across the path, then crashed on to the track
and broke into two parts. There wasn’t time to think—
Leyhmann and the two parts of the machine lay right
across the track. James shut off power, stuck his foot
hard on the ground, and shut his eyes. The machine
struck the fence and reared over the heads of the spec-
tators. James plunked on the track in a sitting posi-
tion, and shot along as the engine were attached
to him. Having hardly any clothes left on him, he just
had to sit still and try to look as if he wasn’t there, till
they brought a blanket, or a top-coat, or something
•to wrap round him. It was Leyhmann who brought the
cdat. “ Ilulloa, I thought you were dead,” said James
faintly. “ Not much,” replied Leyhmann. “ You hurry
up and get some clothes and another machine. We’ ve got
to run this thing off again. The people are real in-
terested.’

ZAM-BUK GREAT PRAISE.
ME. J. T. McINNES, a well-known
Queensland journalist and explorer,
for many years special news writer
for the Queenslander, and now
attached to the staff of that popular daily
morning newspaper, the Brisbane Daily
Mail, writes to the proprietors of Zam-Buk
the following letter eulogising the healing
virtues of that Great Healing Balm. “I
cannot speak too highly of the efficacy of
Zam-Buk Healing Balm which was the
means of completely curing me of what
might otherwise have proved a troublesome
infirmity. Some six years ago when in the
‘ Gulf Country ’ of Queensland, I had the
misfortune to suffer from a bad attack of
Dengue Fever followed by severe Dysen-
tery which brought about an abdominal
weakness that I came to regard as Hem-
orrhoids or ordinary Piles, and proceeded
to treat myself accordingly. The infirmity,
however, was of an intermittent character,
but when in evidence proved very severe,
whilst the periods of attack varied accord-
ing to the change of climatic conditions
with which I had to contend. Some twelve
months ago I thought it advisable to con-
sult an eminent inter-State medical expert who diagnosed the case .as a prolapse
of the lower bowel, and advised a-costly operation as the surest and safest means
of a speedy cure. Fortunately for me my business arrangements at the time did
not permit of this course, although I had determined to act on the medico’s
advice when a favorable opportunity offered. About this time, however, as luck
would have it, I decided to give Zam-Buk a trial, and with most gratifying results.
Its application proved soothing, its properties strengthening, and after using
three large pots of the preparation, I am pleased to say my health improved, my
bodily comfort was restored, and what promised to be a troublesome complaint
has, lam glad to say, departed, and for ever. You are quite at liberty to pub-
lish this letter if desired, because I think that in the interests suffering
humanity, the value of a simple and economical remedy of this sort cannot
be too generally known.”

The Widows of Yam.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
PATSY FINN was himself an industrious
man, the type of Irishman that almost
inevitably gathers a fortune about him if
circumstances and fates are not wholly adverse,
and who does it without deliberate intention, but
in the exercise of a constitutional diligence and
innate frugality. But Mrs. Patsy was the saving
grace of the Finn family, and its exemplar in
industry. She was a small woman and not a par-
ticularly strong one, being liable to “the sick-
ness,” but she had the energy and enterprise of
the ant and the bee, and delved and carried from
light till dark, never content with acquisition or
conquest. There was plenty of land available
to the residents in the fading township of Yam,
and it was always Mrs. Patsy who took the lead
when she and Patsy plucked up their fences and
planted them further on and out. Eventually their
astonishing fences, comprising all the grades from
rubble wall to chock-and-log, embraced six acres,
and crops were grown for the cows. There being
more crop than the cows could eat, additional
cows were bought, and Mrs. Patsy added to her
labors the task of chasing round in the cold and
early hours, delivering milk and cream to the
neighbors and “gentry.”
The Finns had four young children, short,
sturdy barbarians with pale hair all over their
faces, boys and girls alike, with the voracity of
growing mastiffs, and strenuous as horses. When
Mrs. Patsy and her family essayed a difficult task,
the ground broke up before them, and great rocks
plucked themselves out of their beds and fled.
All the people of Yam gathered their firewood in
Cadden’s paddocks, which were over against the
township. The others were content to carry a
day’s, or at most two days’, allowance at a time,
but Mrs. Patsy and her boys, also her girls,
carried wood indefatigably, and with a provident
regard for rainy days in the years to be. They
piled great stacks in the big back yard, and
ravaged Cadden’s gums with saw and toma-
hawk in quest of more. Mrs. Patsy, staggering
home across the flat under a limb that would have
been a fair burden for a bullock, was a daily spec-
tacle at Yam. If Cadden dared to interfere with
the spoilers there was an Irish row in the
paddocks, mother and children assailing him in
brogue, and squealing with the wrath of free
Britons whose sacred privileges were threatened by
the usurper. For it was the law, according to popu-
lar opinion in Yarn, that residents were justified
in getting away with anything they could lift from
the paddocks, sin and illegality only coming in
with the use of horses.
Finn was a miner, rarely out of a job, and then
for a short time only, Mrs. Patsy being kept
awake of nights with the horror of it if Finn was
not earning. In spite of his wife’s industry,
Finn’s house was the dirtiest in Yam. The house
was a hovel, the yard a bog, and Mrs. Patsy
herself, with wisps of mousey hair hanging over
her smudged face, and a torn petticoat looped up
to her belt, was the untidiest woman in three
districts. All her energies ran to out-door work,
“’Tis divil a penny a clean flure ’r a nate table
’ll bring ye. Mrs. Pollard, ma’am,” was her scorn-
ful reply to the gibes of a neighbor.
When sickness came to Finn’s wife for the last
time, she battled against it with all the stubborn-
ness of her nature; but it was pleurisy in a bad
form, and drove her to her bed, where she pre-
sently went “mad in her mind,” as Patsy put it.
Being left alone, she was found an hour after
grubbing in the mud of the yard with a bairel
stave, under the impression that she was digging
the “praties.” She died that night.
Yam had an astonishing quantity and breed of
widows. “Miners’ disease ’’ and the treacherous
nature of the “country” in the Left Hand and
the Euchre Pack accounted for the quantity ; their
similarity in quality was one of the mysterious
workings of Providence. When Patsy Finn arose
from his lamentations he found his house filled
with widows —great, grim, iron-framed, iron-
grey widows, short stout widows, and small
wizened widows—and in the parlor they were
disputing with venom for the privilege of laying
out Mrs. Finn. There was a tradition, unwritten,
unspoken, and yet widely accepted, that the
woman performing this sacred office for a wife
had something of a lien on the widower.
Mrs. Cody, with one hand thrust under Mrs.
Wilson’s nose, and slapping the palm emphati-
cally with the fingers of the other, repudiated the
latter’s claim in strident dialect, and Mrs.
Wilson, her hands on her fat hips, her face
screwed to a contemptuous grimace, and a pointed
tongue exposed, retorted in insolent pantomime.
“The chake an’ impedence iv yeh !” cried Mrs.
Cody. “You the poor body couldn’t abide fer
th’ consate ’n’ th’ lyin’ tongue ye have—you t’
come here wantin’ to put the hand o’ fri’n’ship on
the corpse ! It’s onchristiao, ma’am.”
Mrs. Ward and Mrs. Knox wore shrilly Orating
at each other in one corner, and Mrs. Berryman
and Mrs. Michie quarrelled across the table, Mrs.
Berryman recalling a time when Mrs. Michie
threw the dead hen at Mrs. Finn in her own
kitchen, and Mrs. Michie reminding Mrs. Berry-
man of what Mrs. Finn had said “ wid her own
lips” to Mrs. B.’s everlasting discredit.
Patsy stood among the widows, his simian face
puckered into an expression of comical distress,
his weeping and wailing suspended for the
moment in the face of this invasion. The widows
gathered about him, each seeking to have her
claim authorised. They protested as one widow.
“ Wasn’t me name the last wor-rd on the lips of
the pore dear Mary Ann ? ” asked Mrs. Michie.
“D’you think she’d lave another creature touch
her corpse if she was alive this moment, Pat
Finn?” cried Mrs. Cody.
“Many’s the time she’s said, poor darling, that
if there was one woman in this world she could
trust it was Fisher Wilson.” Mrs. Wilson had a
fat woman’s readiness in tears, and was shedding
them fluently.
“Cudn’t yez toss fer it?” said Finn, helplessly.
“Thin ’tis to a Proderstan’ ye’d thrust th’ help-
less disased. Patsy Finn, I’m sore disappointed
wid ye, I am.” Mrs. Cody’s reproach was without
bitterness. )
A diversion was created by the discovery of
Mrs. Berryman’s shameful duplicity. While her
neighbors were disputing over the widower, she
had taken possession of the bedroom, and was
already busy upon the mortal remains of Mrs.
Finn. The others streamed in, protesting in shrill
accents, and Patsy returned to his mourning, - He
was sitting at the table, weeping copiously, and
denouncing fate with melancholy curses and
irrefragable logic, when a fresh storm arose in the
bedroom, and presently the widows broke into the
kitchen, pell-mell, and Mrs. Berryman, who led
the invasion, dropped a long', narrow moleskin
bag before the widower, too excited to speak.
“ ’Twas on herself,” spluttered Mrs. Cody, “tied
round the waist iv her.”
The article in question was a roughly-made belt,
and as Patsy lifted it there was heard the cheer-
ful clink of sovereigns. The excitement com-
municated itself to the bereaved. He forgot his
sorrow, and cut his finger in his eagerness to rip
open the belt, and then there poured out on to the
table a stream of gold. To Patsy’s amazed eyes
it looked a deluge, to the widows it was wealth
beyond avarice. There were 204 bright sovereigns
glittering on the board.
“Lord iv hivin, I’m a heiress,” gasped Patsy.
“A man iv money!” murmured Mrs. Michie.
This was the beginning of the hunt. It lasted
many days, and Patrick Finn lived to creep to
his own home in fear and trembling, and
to skulk about like a forbidden dog. As
merely a man, Patsy would have been fair
fame to the widows, but as the heir to Mrs.
’inn’s great savings, he was the most conspicuous
desirable in the district, and the competition for
him was keen, and assumed strange develop-
ments. The widows took possession of the
home, they prepared the deceased for the grave
and superintended the funeral, and for long
after the house was rarely free of one or another
of them. Often the whole drove invaded the
place, each fearing that the other might entrap
the widower by some service that would give
her a superior claim on Finn’s gratitude. The
least enterprising fell out as the pursuit grew
heated and fierce, but five or six remained, to
Patsy’s sore undoing.
Mrs. Berryman scrubbed the house out, and
Mrs. Cody undertook to be a mother to the
orphans. Mrs. Wilson supervised the milking of
the cows. Mrs. Ward prepared Patsy’s meals,
and on the first Sunday morning after the burying
Mrs. Knox sailed into Finn’s kitchen and placed
a newspaper parcel on the “ sofy.”
“What may that be, Mrs. Knox, ma’am?”
queried Patsy suspiciously.
“It’s your washin’, Mr. Finn,” ansvvered the
widow, “an’your mendin’, Mr. Finn. I was in
Friday collectin’ it, it bein’ a real joy to help the
afflicted.”
“’N how much is to pay, Mrs. Knox, ma’am ?”
“Can you ask, Patrick?” said the widow coyly.
“ Would I charge you anythin’ for a small, neigh-
borly attention, an’ you that’s been stricken by the
Loord?” Mrs. Knox had Wesleyan sympathies.
She regarded Patsy with great tenderness, and he
retreated behind the table in trepidation.
Never had his socks been so neatly darned, his
garments so smartly patched, or his go-to-church
shirt so handsomely polished. He regarded these
perfections with keen resentment, tempered by
awe of the bony relict of the late “ Henery ”
Knox, his instinct warning him of, the nefarious
design underlying her kindnesses.
At that moment Mrs. Ward, entering from the
shed with a tub of prepared vegetables, uttered a
snort of disgust at the sight of her rival.
“Good morning, Mrs. Knox,” she said, with
venom ; “ I wonder, with that crowd of kids of
yours, you can find time for gallivantin’ of Bun-
days.”
“An’ I don’t think your affairs ’re as well
tended, Mrs. Ward, that you need go pokin’ your
nose into other people’s,” replied Mrs. Knox,
sharply.
Mrs. Cody entered hastily from an adjoining
room, towing a reluctant, half-washed son ,of
Patsy’s by the ear.
“Sure, a body ’ud think ’twas a chapel wid the
Prodestan’s so thick round on a Sunday mornin’,’
she said, with fine contempt.
Patsy stole along the wall, and escaped through
the parlor. Mrs. Wilson was bearing down on
the house from the north, and Mrs. Berryman
and Mrs. Ward were sailing up smartly from the
south. Patsy fled west,, seeking cover in the bush.
When he reached Cadden’s fence he looked back.
Mrs. Berryman and Mrs. Wilson were coming
after him in company. The widower took refuge
in the sapling plantation, and he dined that day
at the Cattle Yards Inn —an extravagance that
must have turned Mrs. Finn in her grave.
The quest of the widows, became daily more
eager and acrimonious. In its earlier stages there
was some decent effort to maintain a show of
disinterested kindness in the face of Yam, but
the pretence was soon abandoned. Many times
Patsy squared his shoulders, and, “ disguising fair
nature with hard-favored rage,” went straight
home with the intention of clearing his house of
the widows, driving them off with weapons if
need be, but he always faltered at the door. He
could not put his bitter grievance into wdirds, and
he had nothing to complain of but good deeds and
great benefits conferred. His house was now
clean and tidy, his children well-washed and well-
clad, and he had hot meals and creature comforts
he had never known in the days of Mrs. Finn.
Yet, as the competition became keener, Patsy’s
anxieties increased. Coming home from day-shift,
he would hide in the crop or behind the outhouses,
whistling for his son Terry.
“Maybe there’s someone beyant, Terry ?” said
Patsy.
“Wan in th’ kitchen ’n’ wan in the parlor,
dad.”
“Mother iv sorrers, what’ve I done?” wailed
Pat. “ Tell ’em I’m took t’ the ’ospital with a leg
bruck, me son, ” he said. As a result of that •
message the hospital at Yarraman was invaded
by three breathless widows before ten the same
night.
Patsy was seen wandering disconsolately about
Yam at all hours. He was found sleeping in
Cadden’s hay, and one cold night Foster dis-
covered him coiled up in his hen-house, making
the most of a straw nest and a broody fowl.
“There’s wan iv ’em sleepin’ at me house,”
said Pat, piteously. “’Tis. Mrs. Knox. She sez
she’s nursin’ me boy Barty, what’s got the divil a
bit wrong with him, barrin’ a pimple ’r two.”
Foster was so touched that lie, took Finn in,
and loaned him a horse-rug and the kitchen mat.
Mrs. Knox was the worst of the widows. It
was she whom Patsy dreaded the most, and he
arose from his seat with bristling hair and pro-
truding eyes one Sunday evening when she
entered his house, escorting a tall, clerical-looking
stranger. .
“It’s the Rev. Peter Ambey what I told you
of, Patrick,” said Mrs. Knox.
The widower groaned, and his knees quaked
under him. He seemed to be in the act of
swallowing something much too large for him,
and his eyes turned anxiously to the door. Mrs.
Knox smartly assumed a position cutting off
retreat in that direction.
“Most happy,” drawled the Rev. Ambey ;
“ most happy indeed. And how is our ber-rother?”
He smiled a withered smile, and shook Patsy’s
limp hand.
“Mr. Ambey will talk things over with you,
Patrick,” said Mrs. Knox.
“ Where would our dear ber-rother like to have
the ceremony performed ? ” said the parson.
Patsy gulped again, and sank helplessly into a
chair. “ The which is it, sorr ?” he asked, feebly.
“The marriage, to be sure.”
“ The marriage ! Yis, yis, yis ; the marriage,
iv course.” He clawed his jaw with a trembling
!\T L . Wh °’ d be marryin ’’ would y ’ tell

said the Rev. Ambey, raising his brows. “lam

widow. He arose twice and sat down again, his
fingers plucking nervously at his whiskers “Wed
Mrs. Knox, sorr,” he filtered. “ Yis, yis, yis,
yis.” Suddenly he pulled himself together, and
made a bolt for the door; but Mrs. Knox was
ur on him, and her arms were about his neck. She

affection, had rushed to embrace her
“ Won’t you tell the good minister when an’
where, Patrick, dear ?” sighed Mrs. Knox.

“ I shall be at liberty Tuesday afternoon, if
that will suit our dear ber-rother,” said Mr.
Amber, softly.
At that moment the door was dashed open, and
a fourth person bounced into the room. It was
Mrs. Cody, red with indignation, panting with
exertion, but full of fight.
“ Nice goin’s on ! ” she said ; “ nice goin’s on, I
don’t think. What the divil’s that man doin’
here. Mother Knox?” Her voice had risen to
its highest note; she pointed the finger of scorn
at the Reverend Ambey.
Mrs. Knox was pale with rage, and her hands
were curved into claws. “An’ what business is
that of yours, you dirty slattern ? ” she squealed.
“Slattern!” cried Mrs. Cody. “Slattern!
slattern, is it ? ” She dashed at Mrs. Knox.
The Rev. Ambey intervened, and Mrs. Cody
tore his umbrella from his grasp, and beat it
about his head, driving him to the wall. Mrs.
Knox took her in the rear, twisting her talons in
her hair, and then the two went at it like gigantic
cats. Patsy saw the tempest of hair and dra-
peries go down on top of the clergyman, and then
he fled out into the night.
Finn was not seen again at Yam for three
days. Acting on information received, the
widows made an almost simultaneous descent
upon his, home on the fourth morning. They
were met at the door by a little woman about
36—a woman with dense masses of black hair,
eyes almost as black, and smiling lips. The sight of
this stranger disconcerted the widows. They re-
garded her in silence, and there was disapproval
in every eye.
“Good mornin’, ladies,” said the woman softly.
She had a mellow brogue. “Are ye neighbors ov
Misther Finn ?”
“ Then you are Patsy Finn’s housekeeper,” said
Mrs. Knox, who still bore traces of her unfortun-
ate battle with Kitty Cody.
“ An’ what of ut ? ”
“ ’Twon’t be considered decent here, ma’am.”
“Tut, tut, tut,” said the woman cheerfully,
there’s a pity now.”
“ What’s more, as Patsy Finn’s promised wife
I won’t have it! ” cried Mrs. Knox. The other
widows clamored in protest.
The stranger stepped forward and, to the
amazement of the others, gripped Mrs. Knox by
the shoulders and shook her. She shook the long
woman as if she had been a dry rush, quickening
her pace till the widow’s head flopped back and
forward violently, and her false teeth flew into the
air. Then the woman stopped, and when she
■spoke her quiet voice had a convincing tone.
“ Look ut here, ma’am,” she said, “iv ye come
t’ my house, sayin’ what ye’ll have ’n’ what ye
won’t have, I’ll tear that mane little tussock iv
hair from yer head, ’n’ I’ll sthrip ye clane’s a prop
fer the divarsion iv the town, ye she-cormorant, I
will.” Then she threw Mrs. Knox backwards,
and the widow fell ignominiously in a sitting
position, toothless, and with her hair awry and
her best bonnet down over her eye. “As fer the
rest o’ ye,” the woman continued, “ Pm’s dacent
’n’ neighborly a body's lives, but I won’t have my
husband tracked be anny woman, ’n’ Patsy Finn
is his name. Ye’ll oblige me by forgettin’ that.”
“ So you did marry a widow after all, Patsy ?”
said Foster to Finn, a day later.
“’Twas in self-defence I done it,” answered
Finn sadly.
EDWARD DYSON.
In the Judgment Hall.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
FROM the gloom of an under-world they
come,
With no ring of steel and no roll of drum ;
But a new glow shines on the grimy cheek,
And a language new do the pale lips speak,
And there is a coming of countless feet
On the line of march in the world’s wide street —
For the cry has gone forth, “ Ho ! Come ye all,
And meet to-day in the Judgment Hall.”
To ears that have long been listening
Is come the call of a gracious Spring ;
To eyes that have long been blankly sealed
Is a nearer vision of things revealed ;
To the spirit that was for ages dumb
The power to utter its need is come ;
Its voice will the lords of the earth appal,
For they, too, come to the Judgment Hall.
The maimed and shamed of the world will meet
The workless hands and the homeless feet;
And they whose, share is a crust and a shed,
And they who have made the earth their bed—
Who have dyed its soil with their precious blood,
Who have borne the ravage of flame and flood;
The marred and scarred of Mammon’s thrall
Will throng to the People’s Judgment Hall.
To you who were given the power supreme,
To mould a new and nobler scheme ;
To succor the weak, and curb the strong,
In sight of many an ancient wrong;
The trust ye bore was a holy thing,
And as the sin is the reckoning,
The sense of the Writing on the Wall
Is known in the People’s Judgment Hall.
On the towering tree that as yet looks firm
Is the mark of the mute, insidious worm ;
Never a woodmen is cunning as he,
To sap the heart of the greenwood tree ;
He knows the shock, and he feels the strain
In every rush of the wind and rain ;
But they only know when the tree shall fall
Who sit in state in the Judgment Hall.
VIO.
A.J.B.
Two Points of View.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THE slightly-built, sloping-shouldered
man with the tailor-made pants
furtively under the dray at the contractor
standing on the other side; and, having thus
made sure that the boss was not looking his way,
stole a moment’s respite from his heart-breaking
toil. The man shovelling next to him watched
him from the corner of his eye.
“ Y’better do a bit,” he growled. “ If Burke
spots yer, yer a bird.”
The other straightened himself disdainfully.
“Do a bit,” he echoed. “Good God ! a man may
as well ”
He broke off abruptly, and bent doggedly to his
work again. Through the spokes of the dray-
wheel he had caught a momentary glimpse Of
Burke’s long legs bearing their owner towards
him.
The legs halted behind him, and the sloping-
shouldered man worked with feverish energy, his
head bent low, and his ears listening anxiously to
catch the words he dreaded, yet inwardly half-
hoped for.
Six months ago, he had joined the ranks of the
unemployed, through no particular fault of his
own. The big warehouse, where he had been em-
ployed as a clerk, had decided on retrenchment,
and the sloping-shouldered man had been one of
the retrenched. That was the way of it; and for
six dreary months he had tramped the city and
suburbs seeking that elusive will-o’-the-wisp-
work. Also, for six months, he had been
gradually getting deeper into debt, until, at
length, his credit had been finally stopped. Then
someone had told him of this job.
“ Tell Burke I sent you,” he was told. “There’s
seven bob a day there for anyone that can sling a
shovel and isn’t afraid of work.”
Seven shillings a day. It seemed too good to be
true. Of course, he had never swung a shovel in
his life, but everybody had to learn, and what he
lacked in skill he would make up in energy.
So he reasoned with himself —and with Burke,
when that big and, on the whole, kindly Irish
person expressed doubts about his capabilities; so
the end of it was that Burke gave him a start,
and the man who for months had endured the
trials of a negative purgatory, was introduced to
the agonies of a positive hell.
The building speculator had bought the land
cheap because of its uneven formation, and had
given Burke a contract to level the block of
20 acres, at a fixed, and none too liberal, price per
cubic yard of dirt removed.
So Burke had engaged an army of men and
drays, and-a couple of six-horse ploughs to do the
will of the great man ; and, while it was a fairly
easy thing to get “taken on” at the job, it was
even less trouble to get “put off.” For lounging
about in groups, just clear of the slaving horde
around the drays, was always an anxious-eyed
crew waiting patiently for a chance to chip in.
For six weeks the one-time clerk had stuck
gamely to the work—his soft hands blistered, his
back and limbs aching with the unaccustomed
strain. Only the thought of his wife and
children and his desire to falsify the contemptuous
prophecies of his mates concerning the “ cracking-
up” of the “counter-jumper,” kept him from
throwing down his shovel and demanding his
time, otherwise the amount due to him.
But to-day the strain seemed greater than ever.
A dozen times he had braved the boss’s displea-
sure, and marched wearily across to the bucket
of tepid water standing in the full glare of the
sun. A score of times he had snatched a moment’s
rest by watching the boss from the opposite side
of the dray; and during the past hour he had
been sneaking hurried glances at his watch, at
average intervals of five minutes.
His neighbor turned towards him again, with-
out slackening for an instant the easy swing of
the big shovel; for George Smith prided himself
upon throwing a pretty blade.
“Gittin anxious?’ he queried.
The ex-clerk was silent for a moment. 1 hen he
into a stream of bitter curses—bitter,
yet strangely awkward, for “language” is not a
requisite in an up-to-date warehouse ; and be had
not been long enough among the navvies to
Ranches! ° f Van ° US
stdl, so far as his limitations allowed, he cursed
things generally. The boss, the work, the nature
of. the soil, and the hours all came within the
range of his invective ; but it was with the hours
his principal grievance lay. teix hours, he con-
sidei!gd) sh o„ld satisfy any reasonable employer,
As a matter of fact, if he were an employer, he
wouldn’t think of asking men to work more than
bv ®
tapped him gently on the shoulder
J*e wheeled quickly and met Burke s outstretched
hand-with a half-sovereign and four single
shillings showing like islets in an ocean of palm
nd wa \°! dy Tuesday Even the fact that
there was a shilling more than was fairly due to
h.m seemed hardly an alleviation of the wrong,
* * * *
The board of directors of the Black Damp
Colliery Co. had met to consider what steps
should be taken with reference to the existing
strike. The bones of contention were pretty much
as usual—wages and hours, but principally hours,
Things had been .going well with the Black
Damp Co. of late, and the men considered they
bad a p excellent chance of gaining their point,
During the early part of the meeting it
certainly looked as though they would. Opinions
were almost evenly divided ; but, if anything, the
meeting showed a tendency to yield, until the
new director spoke. He had only recently be-
come a large shareholder in Black Damps (per
medium of a legacy which had raised him from
absolute poverty), and he had very lately been
appointed to the board of directors. Up to the
present time he had held his peace. Short and
slight, with narrow, sloping shoulders, he was not
an impressive person. He stood for a moment as
though uncertain how to begin ; but once fairly
started, his words seemed to follow readily
enough. As a speaker he was not brilliant, yet
in his rugged utterances and raspy voice there
rang a note of sincerity which did more to con-
vince his hearers than the most polished oratory
would have done.
In vivid colors he pictured the danger of yield-
ing to “ these ruffians,” whose one idea, he said,
was to place the iron heel of Socialism upon the
neck of Capital. In eager tones he besought his
fellow directors to stand to their principles, and
never to retreat one inch before the onset of
worthless agitators who seemed to imagine that
their whole mission in life was to extort from the
unfortunate capitalist the highest possible wage,
and at the same time to reduce the length of the
working day to an absolute minimum. The thing
was absurd, unworkable, absolutely ridiculous ;
and, as the new director sat down he expressed a
hope that his fellow directors would cordially
agree with him.
And, sure enough, his fellow directors did. He
never could understand by what association of
ideas it came about, but as the undersized man
with the sloping shoulders sat down he looked
round with a start. He had a vague, utterly un-
accountable impression that behind his back a
large hand was offering him fourteen shillings,
